### [Photonic Analog Quantum Simulation of (1+1)-Dimensional $U(1)$ Lattice Gauge Theory with Dynamical Matter](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02756v1)

**Authors:** Nathan R. Gonzalez, Thea Budde, Klemen Kersic, Zia Steele, Alex H. Rubin, Joao C. Pinto Barros, Marina Radulaski, Marina Krstic Marinkovic  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02756v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `analog quantum simulation` **5/5** · 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **3/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **3/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.02756_figures/2606.02756_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. The U(1) gauge theory can be mapped exactly onto a bosonic chain with 2L −1 sites. The bosonic occupations are uniquely mapped to a gauge-theory configuration depending on the site’s parity. The left/right pointing yellow arrows represent the polarization of the gauge field ⟨Sz l ⟩= ±1/2. A filled/empty circle indicates an occupied/empty fermionic site. An occupied bosonic matter site corresponds to the presence of a charge in the QLM, while the location of doubly occupied gauge field sites indicates the polarization of the gauge field.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.02756_figures/2606.02756_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Mapping between the JCH model and the spin-1/2 QLM. (a) Schematic of a coupled cavity array in the strong coupling regime (g ≫J), consisting of alternating matter (blue) and gauge (orange) sites, each composed of a cavity coupled to a two-level emitter with a coupling rate g. Photon hopping between cavities occurs at a rate J. Colors indicate varying cavity frequency ωe. (b) Polariton energy level structure for each cavity. The anharmonic splitting of the JC ladder is tuned to support the resonance conditions E2i 2,−= E2i−1 1,−+ E2i+1 1,− to be satisfied simultaneously for all i across the array.</sub>

</details>

(a) Matter site and (b) gauge field JCH occupation mapped to QLM observables (charge density and electric flux, respectively) as described by Eqs. (8) and (9). (c) Absolute value of the expectation value for the Gauss’s law operator |⟨Gl⟩| for each matter site l and the two adjacent gauge sites, which remains close to zero for all times, confirming confinement to the physical subspace by the gauge protection term λ. Gauss’s law Gl is defined in Eq. (4). Projection onto the physical state for (d) gauge fields and (e) matter sites as described by the set of equations in Eq. (10)....</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.02756_figures/2606.02756_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. The possible strength of the effective interaction t varies with the parameters. The color indicates the maximum value of t that can be engineered while keeping J perturba- tively small. This ensures gauge invariance at intermediate timescales. White indicates that there is no valid solution to the resonance condition for these parameters.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper proposes using coupled cavity arrays in cavity QED to analog quantum simulate a 1D U(1) Lattice Gauge Theory. By engineering the Jaynes-Cummings-Hubbard model, the authors successfully map the system's dynamics onto the target gauge theory, confirming its gauge-invariant evolution. This establishes a novel, scalable platform for studying complex many-body physics beyond classical simulation capabilities.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it uses advanced cavity QED techniques (a core AMO topic) to tackle a fundamental problem in high-energy/condensed matter theory (LGTs), providing a concrete, experimentally viable quantum simulation blueprint.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of developing a quantum simulation platform to study the real-time dynamics of a (1+1)-Dimensional U(1) Lattice Gauge Theory (LGT) coupled to dynamical matter.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the dynamics of the target LGT can be accurately replicated by engineering the Jaynes-Cummings-Hubbard (JCH) model in a coupled cavity array, confirming its potential for simulating gauge-invariant physics.

**Method.** The simulation relies on mapping the LGT onto the JCH model using resonant engineering of polaritonic excitations, followed by verifying the dynamics using exact diagonalization.

**Model / system.** The physical system is an array of interacting cavities operating in the strong-coupling regime of Cavity Quantum Electrodynamics (QED). The underlying model is the JCH Hamiltonian, which is engineered to mimic the spin-1/2 Quantum Link Model (QLM) Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Gauge invariance (monitored via Gauss's law expectation value $\langle G_l angle$) and the confinement of the evolution to the physical subspace are key diagnostics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The simulation requires operating in the strong-coupling regime ($g \gg J$) and precise tuning of cavity detunings ($\Delta_i$) to achieve resonant coupling.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the system can be effectively described by the JCH model and relies on deriving an effective Hamiltonian via second-order perturbation theory.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the mapping of the U(1) gauge theory onto a bosonic chain and schematically depict the mapping between the JCH model and the spin-1/2 QLM in a coupled cavity array.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the LGT problem, details the mapping from the QLM to the JCH model using cavity QED principles, shows the successful replication of real-time dynamics via numerical simulation, and concludes by discussing scalable implementations in photonic and superconducting platforms.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a photonic scheme for analog quantum simulation of a $U(1)$ Lattice Gauge Theory (LGT) with dynamical matter based on the Jaynes-Cummings-Hubbard (JCH) model. Here, an array of interacting cavities in the strong-coupling regime of cavity Quantum Electrodynamics is mapped onto the alternating matter and gauge-field sites of the spin-1/2 Quantum Link Model. In contrast to other analog LGT quantum simulation methods, our approach implements the desired gauge-invariant dynamics through the hopping of polaritonic excitations among the array sites. The hopping is mapped to the gauge theory via precise tuning of polaritonic resonances in individual cavities. Using exact diagonalization, we show that the real-time evolution of the JCH model accurately replicates that of a Quantum Link Model. Finally, we discuss feasible routes to the beyond-classical simulation capability with scalable implementations in photonic and superconducting systems. This provides a novel route towards understanding the real-time dynamics of lattice gauge theories with matter in higher dimensions.

</details>

**Summary.** This work experimentally validates the use of high-gap superconductors (Pb) in InAs tetron devices to enhance topological protection. By measuring the parity switching time, the authors report a lifetime of $\sim 20$ seconds, significantly exceeding current qubit timescales. This demonstrates that increasing the superconducting gap effectively suppresses quasiparticle poisoning, a key requirement for scalable topological quantum computation.

**Why it may be interesting.** The demonstrated long parity lifetime directly addresses a major decoherence bottleneck in topological quantum computing, providing a crucial experimental benchmark for fault-tolerant quantum information processing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To experimentally validate that increasing the superconducting excitation gap improves the robustness and lifetime of topological superconducting phases used for quantum computing.

**Main result.** The authors achieved an extremely long parity lifetime of $\sim 20$ seconds in an InAs--Pb tetron device, which is orders of magnitude longer than typical qubit operation times.

**Method.** The study employs interferometric single-shot parity measurements and develops a novel RF measurement technique to probe low-energy wire-end states.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is an InAs--Pb tetron device, a superconductor-semiconductor hybrid structure designed to host Majorana zero modes (MZMs) for topological quantum computation. The system's robustness is enhanced by using the higher-gap superconductor Lead (Pb) instead of Aluminum (Al).

**Key observables.** Parity switching time ($	au_Z$), quantum capacitance shifts, and the topological gap ($\Delta_T$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Parity lifetime ($\sim 20$ s), Topological Gap ($\Delta_T \sim 70 \mu	ext{eV}$), Majorana hybridization splitting ($E_M < 1 \mu	ext{eV}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The improvement in robustness is directly attributed to the increased superconducting gap, which suppresses quasiparticle poisoning effects.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the device architecture, material characterization (SdH oscillations, mobility), the RF spectroscopy setup, and time-resolved measurements showing the exponential decay of the parity state over seconds.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from demonstrating improved material quality and gap size using Pb, to developing high-precision RF spectroscopy to measure low-energy states, and finally culminates in time-resolved measurements to quantify the dramatically extended parity lifetime.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A central promise of topological quantum computing is that increasing the excitation gap improves device performance significantly. Here, we experimentally validate this principle in an InAs--Pb tetron device via interferometric single-shot parity measurements. By replacing aluminum with the higher-gap superconductor lead in our superconductor-semiconductor hybrid devices, we have improved the robustness of our topological phase. In addition, to enable fast and precise bring-up at scale, we have developed an rf measurement technique that resolves low-energy wire-end states and directly measures their energy splitting with $μ\text{eV}$ precision. We employ this technique to bring up a device in a multi-tetron array and perform parity measurements of one of the tetron's hybrid nanowires (NWs). By controllably switching the wire parity, we observe $h/2e$-periodic bimodal shifts in the quantum capacitance of a quantum dot coupled to the hybrid nanowire in an interference loop. Further time-resolved measurements reveal a characteristic parity switching time of $\sim 20$ s with some instances reaching minute-scale. Such extremely long parity lifetimes are orders of magnitude longer than typical qubit operation times, which are on the order of $μ\text{s}$. Finally, we discuss potential implications for the fidelity of Pauli measurements.

</details>

### [Post-Selection Free Generation of Multi-Photon Added Coherent States](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03167v1)

**Authors:** Mariano Uria, Ricardo Gutiérrez-Jáuregui, Carla Hermann-Avigliano, Pablo Solano  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03167v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper presents a deterministic method to generate multi-photon added coherent states (PACS) using a driven Kerr nonlinear resonator. By leveraging the photon blockade effect, the protocol avoids probabilistic measurement steps, achieving high fidelity even when considering realistic cavity dissipation. This provides a robust platform for creating complex non-classical resources for quantum technologies.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly applies advanced concepts from cavity QED and nonlinear quantum optics (Kerr media, photon blockade) to create a deterministic source of complex non-classical states, which is a major goal in quantum information science.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of deterministically generating high-fidelity multi-photon added coherent states (PACS) without relying on probabilistic post-selection schemes.

**Main result.** A protocol is proposed and demonstrated that achieves fidelities exceeding 99% for generating PACS using a driven Kerr nonlinear resonator, even under realistic dissipative conditions.

**Method.** The generation relies on exploiting the photon blockade effect within the cavity dynamics, monitored using a frame-independent witness called the remnant photon number ($n_r$).

**Model / system.** The system is a single-mode cavity containing a Kerr nonlinear medium, modeled by a Hamiltonian $H_R$ in the rotating frame. The dynamics are analyzed using both unitary evolution and the Lindblad master equation to account for dissipation.

**Key observables.** Remnant Photon Number ($n_r$), Fidelity ($F(t)$), Mean Photon Number ($\langle\hat{n}(t)angle$), and the time evolution of the state in phase space.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The Kerr nonlinearity ($\chi$), cavity detuning ($\delta$), external drive power ($E$), and cavity decay rate ($\kappa$). The condition $\delta = 2\chi$ is noted as central for certain analyses.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The initial state is assumed to be the vacuum state. The analysis involves heuristic approximations and the assumption that $n_r$ is a superior, displacement-independent witness for non-classicality.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the protocol schematic, the photon number distribution showing blockade effects, and quantitative results showing fidelity vs. time/parameters for PACS generation.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theoretical framework by defining the Hamiltonian and the remnant photon number witness. It then details the protocol, showing how the system dynamics naturally lead to the desired state, followed by numerical and analytical confirmation of high fidelity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Non-Gaussian quantum states are essential resources for continuous-variable quantum information processing and for metrology. Among these, multi-photon added coherent states bridge classical and non-classical behaviors; however, their generation typically relies on small photon numbers and probabilistic heralding schemes. Here, we propose a protocol for the post-selection free generation of high fidelity multi-photon added coherent states using the photon blockade effect in a driven Kerr nonlinear resonator, where such states emerge naturally during the dynamics. We demonstrate that high-fidelity states can be prepared by optimizing the external drive power and the interaction time. Furthermore, we show that the protocol is robust under realistic experimental conditions, achieving fidelities of $\approx 99\%$ with current state-of-the-art parameters. Our results unlock a deterministic route to complex non-classical states using well-established quantum optical platforms.

</details>

### [Populating topologically protected edge states of a Chern insulator with the cold-atom elevator scheme and measurements](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03438v1)

**Summary.** This paper analyzes a method to populate the chiral edge states of a Chern insulator simulated with ultracold atoms. By introducing an instantaneous projective measurement on the particle reservoir, the system state can be projected into a pure state. The key finding is that postselecting on specific measurement outcomes allows researchers to significantly enhance the population of the desired edge states compared to standard preparation methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly bridges topological physics (Chern insulators) with advanced experimental platforms (cold atoms) and complex quantum measurement theory, providing concrete, calculable protocols for state preparation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how to populate topologically protected, chiral edge states in a Chern insulator using the cold-atom elevator scheme.

**Main result.** Performing an instantaneous, projective measurement on the reservoir allows for the preparation of pure system states and can enhance the population of chiral edge states through postselection.

**Method.** The authors developed a numerical method to calculate measurement probabilities and expectation values for the post-measurement state, which scales linearly with lattice sites.

**Model / system.** The system models a Chern insulator using spinless fermions in the Harper-Hofstadter model on a 2D lattice, coupled to a reservoir via an 'elevator' mechanism using ultracold atoms in optical lattices.

**Key observables.** Particle populations in clockwise ($R_C$) and counterclockwise ($R_{CC}$) edge states, system purity ($	ext{Tr}(ho_S^2)$), and the probability distribution $P_n$ of measuring $n$ particles in the reservoir.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling time $T$, the number of particles measured in the reservoir ($n$), and the magnetic flux $\phi$. Optimal transfer occurs around $T \approx \pi/(2J)$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The measurement is assumed to be instantaneous, and the reservoir is assumed to be topologically trivial. The method is derived for noninteracting, fermionic models conserving particle number.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the energy spectrum, the experimental scheme involving coupling and measurement, and show how particle populations ($R_C, R_{CC}$) and measurement probabilities ($P_n$) change as a function of coupling time $T$ and measurement outcome $n$.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the physical setup and the challenge of measurement. It then details the theoretical framework for calculating post-measurement observables, showing how the measurement step leads to pure states. Finally, it presents results demonstrating that postselection can optimize the population of desired edge states.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Two-dimensional Chern insulators support topologically protected, chiral edge currents, and these can be detected in experiments with ultracold atoms in optical lattices. It has previously been shown that one can populate a selected group of edge states of a Chern insulator by transferring particles from a reservoir. Here, we numerically investigate the effect of performing an instantaneous, projective measurement on the reservoir before the reservoir is discarded. In this way, the final state of the system is pure and described by a wavefunction. We also show that quite likely measurement outcomes can help to increase the final number or percentage of particles in the chiral edge states through postselection. Without the measurement step, the physics can be described in terms of single-particle physics. The measurement significantly complicates the description. By appropriately rewriting the analytical expressions, we show that measurement probabilities, expectation values, averages of expectation values, and purity can nevertheless be computed from the state before the measurement in a way that scales only linearly with the number of lattice sites for a fixed number of particles. This enables us to investigate a setup with, for instance, 14 particles and 198 lattice sites numerically. The approach applies generally to noninteracting, fermionic models that conserve the number of particles.

</details>

### [A mean-field description of strong-to-weak symmetry breaking in the monitored three-dimensional Bose-Hubbard model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02713v1)

**Summary.** This paper develops a mean-field theory to study symmetry breaking in monitored Bose-Hubbard systems. By analyzing local order parameters, the authors establish a local diagnostic for strong-to-weak symmetry breaking. They find that this transition is critically linked to the charge-sharpening transition, suggesting a unified critical physics in open quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, local diagnostic tool (the local order parameter) to study a complex non-equilibrium phenomenon (SWSSB) in a system relevant to quantum gas experiments, linking it to established critical phenomena.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to characterize strong-to-weak spontaneous symmetry breaking (SWSSB) in open quantum systems and establish its connection to the charge-sharpening transition.

**Main result.** The local order parameter characterizing SWSSB is found to be critical near the same measurement strength as the charge-sharpening transition, suggesting they share a common underlying critical point.

**Method.** The authors develop and apply a Gutzwiller mean-field framework to simulate the stochastic measurement dynamics of the Bose-Hubbard model.

**Model / system.** The system is the monitored three-dimensional Bose-Hubbard model, whose dynamics are governed by a combination of the Bose-Hubbard Hamiltonian, Lindbladian dissipation, and local density measurements.

**Key observables.** Local order parameter, charge variance ($\delta n^2$), and the Rényi-2 correlator ($F$), which serve as diagnostics for SWSSB and charge sharpening, respectively.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Measurement strength ($\gamma$), dissipation rate ($D$), and the critical measurement rate ($\gamma_c$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary approximation is the use of the Gutzwiller Mean-Field Theory (GMFT), which assumes the state remains unentangled, simplifying the many-body problem to local single-site dynamics.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the Bose-Hubbard model dynamics across different measurement rates ($\gamma$), showing the evolution of the superfluid order parameter ($\Psi_i$) from coherent to number-eigenstate dominated regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the model and the challenge of characterizing SWSSB. It develops the GMFT approach, applies it to analyze the local order parameter and charge variance, and concludes by establishing the critical connection between the two transitions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Strong-to-weak spontaneous symmetry breaking has emerged as a novel form of ordering in monitored and open quantum systems, yet its characterization has so far primarily relied on nonlocal diagnostics. Here, we develop a Gutzwiller mean-field framework for monitored bosonic lattice systems, enabling the direct simulation of stochastic measurement dynamics in three spatial dimensions. Applying this approach to the monitored Bose-Hubbard model with local density measurements and Lindbladian dissipation, we identify strong-to-weak symmetry breaking through a trajectory-averaged local order parameter. We find that this local order parameter becomes critical near the same measurement strength as the charge-sharpening transition and exhibits Lorentz invariance with a correlation-length exponent, $ν\simeq 1.2$, comparable to that of the charge-sharpening transition, suggesting that the two phenomena may originate from a common underlying critical point. Our work establishes a local characterization of strong-to-weak symmetry breaking, reveals its connection to charge sharpening, and provides concrete predictions for future experiments on the monitored Bose-Hubbard model.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03124"></a>
### [Many-Body Non-Hermitian Physics in the Generalized Brillouin Zone](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03124v1)

<img src="2606.03124_figures/2606.03124_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. (a) Charge-density-wave structure factor SRL(π) in the t1–V plane for a fixed lattice size L = 20. (b) SRL(π) as a function of the interaction V at t1 = 2 for various lattice sizes L. (c) Finite-size extrapolation of Vmax as a function of 1/L2, where Vmax denotes the point at which the derivative dSRL(π)/dV is maximized. The inset shows the derivative dSRL(π)/dV corresponding to the data in panel (b). (d) Zak phase γZ of ˜ H under PBC. The black line indicates the topo- logical phase boundary, with γZ = π in the topological region and γZ = 0 in the trivial region. Other parameters are t2 = 1 and γ = 2/3.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03124_figures/2606.03124_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Degeneracy structure D(n) of the entanglement spec- trum of the quasi-reciprocal Hamiltonian ˜ H in the t1–V plane. (a) D(4). (b) D(2). Common parameters: L = 20, N = 10, t2 = 1, and γ = 2/3.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper extends the Generalized Brillouin Zone theory to interacting non-Hermitian systems by constructing a quasi-reciprocal Hamiltonian. The authors demonstrate that the topological properties of this complex system are robustly characterized by analyzing the entanglement spectrum degeneracy of the derived quasi-reciprocal model. This establishes a crucial theoretical pathway for studying many-body non-Hermitian physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, generalized theoretical tool (GBZ formalism) to analyze complex, interacting, non-Hermitian quantum systems, which is highly relevant for understanding open quantum systems and topological phases in condensed matter.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge addressed is extending the Generalized Brillouin Zone (GBZ) theory, which resolves bulk-boundary correspondence breakdown in non-Hermitian systems, to interacting many-body systems.

**Main result.** The authors successfully construct a quasi-reciprocal many-body Hamiltonian whose topological properties are encoded in the entanglement spectrum of the quasi-reciprocal model, providing a route to study interacting non-Hermitian physics.

**Method.** The study employs a GBZ transformation to map the original non-Hermitian Hamiltonian (under OBC) to a quasi-reciprocal Hamiltonian (under PBC). Phase diagrams are analyzed using exact diagonalization, calculating the Zak phase and the entanglement spectrum degeneracy.

**Model / system.** The model is an interacting non-Hermitian SSH-type model. The theory relies on constructing a quasi-reciprocal Hamiltonian ($	ilde{H}$) that mimics the physics of the original model ($H$) under different boundary conditions.

**Key observables.** Zak phase, structure factor of the charge-density-wave (CDW) phase ($S_{RL}(k)$), and the degeneracy of the low-lying entanglement spectrum (ES).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength ($V$), hopping parameters ($t_1, t_2$), and the topological transition point ($t_{1c}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the applicability of the GBZ transformation to interacting systems and that the quasi-reciprocal Hamiltonian accurately captures the physics of the original model.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the mapping between the original and quasi-reciprocal models, show the phase diagram in the $t_1-V$ plane identifying different CDW and topological phases, and plot the dependence of the structure factor and ES degeneracy on system parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the theoretical framework using the GBZ to derive the quasi-reciprocal Hamiltonian. It then applies this formalism to the interacting SSH model, using multiple diagnostics (CDW structure factor, Zak phase, ES) to map out the phase diagram and confirm the topological nature of the system.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The breakdown of conventional bulk-boundary correspondence (BBC) in   non-Hermitian system can be resolved by the generalized Brillouin   zone (GBZ) theory. However, extending the GBZ theory to interacting   many-body systems remains an open problem. Here, we consider an   interacting non-Hermitian model characterized by a circular GBZ. We   show that, based on a GBZ transformation, a quasi-reciprocal   many-body Hamiltonian can be constructed which, under periodic   boundary conditions (PBC), captures the physics of the original   non-Hermitian model under open boundary conditions (OBC). Using   exact diagonalization (ED), we determine the phase diagram for the   quasi-reciprocal many-body Hamiltonian by computing the Zak phase   and the structure factor of the charge-density-wave (CDW) phase. We   further investigate the entanglement properties and find that the   degeneracy of the low-lying entanglement spectrum characterizes each   phase in the phase diagram. These findings demonstrate that the   topological properties in interacting non-Hermitian system is encoded in   the entanglement spectrum of the quasi-reciprocal model. Our work   establishes a route to studying many-body non-Hermitian physics   within the GBZ formalism.

</details>

### [Geometric Decoherence Time in Lindbladian Dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02743v1)

**Authors:** Rishabh Jha, Stephan Haas, Abhinav Prem

<sub>Figure 5: Time evolution of exact logarithmic negativity EN and von Neumann mutual information I(A:B) for the interacting XXZ chain under two dissipative protocols. (a) Under local z-dephasing (which preserves a strong U(1) symmetry), EN →0 while I(A:B) saturates to a nonzero plateau, signaling a separable but non-product NESS. **Summary.** This paper introduces the geometric decoherence time ($	au_{g}^{	ext{d}}$) as a precise measure for the onset of decoherence in open quantum systems. By analyzing the breakdown of monotonicity between entanglement measures and entropy, the authors demonstrate that this timescale is sensitive to underlying physics, such as topological order in the Kitaev chain. It provides a powerful tool for characterizing the transition from coherent quantum dynamics to a classical steady state.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous, operational measure ($	au_{g}^{	ext{d}}$) to quantify when a quantum system effectively loses its quantum coherence due to environmental coupling, which is crucial for understanding quantum computation and thermalization.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of defining a clear dynamical timescale for the onset of decoherence in open many-body systems, where entanglement loss and entropy growth occur concurrently.

**Main result.** The geometric decoherence time ($	au_{g}^{	ext{d}}$), defined by the breakdown of a monotone relation between logarithmic negativity and Rényi entropy, is established as a robust diagnostic scale for decoherence onset.

**Method.** The authors analyze the time evolution of quantum correlations using the Lindblad master equation, deriving specific criteria based on the monotonicity of entanglement measures.

**Model / system.** The study applies to open many-body systems, including the Kitaev chain and the interacting XXZ spin chain, evolving under Lindbladian dynamics characterized by gain and loss jump operators.

**Key observables.** Logarithmic Negativity ($	ext{EN}$), Rényi-1/2 Entropy ($S_{1/2}$), Quantum Mutual Information ($I(A:B)$), and the geometric decoherence time ($	au_{g}^{	ext{d}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dissipation rates ($\gamma$), system parameters ($\mu, J, \Delta$), and the comparison between topological and trivial phases.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The geometric criterion is assumed to hold for both single-particle Gaussian dynamics and interacting Lindbladian evolution, though symmetry can cause tracking failures.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare $	au_{g}^{	ext{d}}$ against $	au_{	ext{peak}}^{	ext{d}}$ for the Kitaev chain, showing topological protection, and illustrate the time evolution of $	ext{EN}$ and $I(A:B)$ for different dissipation protocols in the XXZ chain.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the geometric criterion ($	au_{g}^{	ext{d}}$) by analyzing the entropy-negativity trajectory, applies this to the Kitaev chain to show topological protection, and extends the analysis to the interacting XXZ chain to distinguish between symmetry-protected classical correlations and true decoherence.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The onset of decoherence in open many-body systems lacks a dynamical timescale grounded in the loss of bipartite entanglement. Here, we introduce the $geometric$ $decoherence$ $time$, defined as the earliest moment the monotone relation between logarithmic negativity and Rényi-$\tfrac{1}{2}$ entropy -- exactly equal across any bipartition for pure states -- breaks down under open-system evolution, signaling entropy growth without accompanying entanglement growth. We establish this criterion in both single-particle Gaussian dynamics and many-body Lindbladian evolution. We show that quantum mutual information provides a complementary long-time diagnostic: its asymptotic vanishing is equivalent to factorization of the steady state across the bipartition, a condition strictly stronger than separability, and whenever a product steady state is approached exponentially in trace norm, negativity and mutual information share the same decay rate. In the presence of a strong symmetry, this tracking can fail -- residual classical correlations can survive after entanglement has vanished. In the Kitaev chain with balanced gain and loss, we derive a closed-form solution and show that the topological phase sustains longer coherence times than the trivial phase at identical dissipation, with a local minimum at the chiral-symmetric point. In the interacting XXZ chain, exact many-body evolution shows that local $Z$-dephasing preserves residual classical correlations, whereas gain and loss restore the mutual-information tracking of negativity. Our results establish the geometric decoherence time as a dynamical scale tracking the onset of decoherence.

</details>

**Authors:** Lara Ulčakar, Jernej Mravlje, Gal Lemut, Tomaž Rejec

**Summary.** This work analyzes the topological Anderson transition in a disordered Chern insulator, finding critical exponents consistent with the Integer Quantum Hall Effect. Crucially, the study examines non-equilibrium dynamics during disorder quenches, revealing that the excitation generation and the associated length scale deviate from standard Kibble-Zurek scaling, depending on the quench direction.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study bridges topological physics, Anderson localization, and non-equilibrium dynamics, providing insights into how topological protection breaks down under disorder and quench protocols, which is relevant for understanding quantum phase transitions in engineered materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the critical properties and non-equilibrium dynamics across the topological Anderson phase transition in a strongly disordered Chern insulator.

**Main result.** The transition is characterized by critical exponents consistent with the Integer Quantum Hall Effect, but the non-equilibrium excitation density does not follow standard Kibble-Zurek scaling, showing direction-dependent behavior.

**Method.** The authors employ finite-size scaling, analysis of the Local Chern Marker (LCM) profile, and quench dynamics simulations to extract critical exponents and non-equilibrium scaling laws.

**Model / system.** The system is a disordered Chern insulator modeled by the half-filled disordered Qi-Wu-Zhang (QWZ) model, which exhibits a transition between a topological and a trivial Anderson insulating phase.

**Key observables.** Chern number, electrical conductance, Local Chern Marker (LCM) profile, correlation length ($\xi$), excitation density, and critical exponents ($
u, z$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Disorder strength ($W$), system size ($N$), and quench time ($	au$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on comparing results to established universality classes (IQHE) and the Kibble-Zurek mechanism, while acknowledging potential limitations regarding convergence in the thermodynamic limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the phase diagram (Chern number vs. conductance vs. disorder), finite-size scaling plots for critical observables, and real-space profiles of the LCM at different disorder strengths.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the critical nature of the transition using ground-state properties (conductance, Chern number) and extracts critical exponents. It then moves to non-equilibrium dynamics, studying the quench evolution of excitation density and the characteristic length scale, comparing these findings to the Kibble-Zurek prediction.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the critical properties of the topological Anderson phase transition in a strongly disordered Chern insulator, separating the topological phase from a trivial Anderson insulator. We show that the transition is characterized by a non-zero electrical conductance and by the emergence of a critical length scale in the real-space profile of the local Chern marker. From this, we extract the correlation-length and the dynamical critical exponents, which are consistent with those of non-interacting models of the integer quantum Hall effect. We then ramp the disorder strength across the transition and study the ensuing dynamics. In contrast to clean topological systems, we find that the excitation density does not follow the Kibble-Zurek scaling. The non-equilibrium length scale associated with the local Chern marker is decoupled from the generation of excitations. For studied system sizes, we find it to be close to the Kibble-Zurek prediction for the topological-to-trivial quench, while it deviates from it for the reverse direction.

</details>

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `analog quantum simulation` **4/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper reveals a drag-induced Non-Hermitian Skin Effect in Bose-Fermi mixtures, where strong interactions enable fermions to exhibit boundary accumulation even though their isolated dynamics are Hermitian. The effect is robustly demonstrated through theoretical modeling and is proposed to be realized in ultracold atomic systems using Floquet engineering.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges non-Hermitian physics (NHSE) with interacting quantum gases, providing a concrete, interaction-mediated mechanism for emergent non-Hermitian behavior in ultracold atomic systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates a novel mechanism where strong interactions allow fermions, which are otherwise Hermitian, to inherit the Non-Hermitian Skin Effect (NHSE) from bosons in a Bose-Fermi mixture.

**Main result.** A drag-induced NHSE is demonstrated, showing that the fermionic boundary accumulation is dynamically stable and requires strong, non-Hermitian bosonic hopping to be induced.

**Method.** The analysis uses a strong-coupling expansion to derive an effective Hamiltonian for the bound-state subspace, followed by time-evolution simulations.

**Model / system.** The system is a one-dimensional Bose-Fermi mixture modeled by a Hamiltonian incorporating bosonic asymmetric hopping ($t_L 
eq t_R$) and inter-species interaction ($U_{bf}$). The platform is proposed for realization using ultracold atoms in optical lattices.

**Key observables.** Fermion density imbalance ($\Delta n_f$), correlation function ($C_n$), and time-evolved density profiles.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Strong interaction coupling ($|U_{bf}|$), non-reciprocal hopping ratio ($t_R/t_L$), and the ratio of interaction to hopping scales.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the strong-coupling limit for deriving the effective Hamiltonian, though the effect is shown to persist beyond this regime.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the energy spectrum and correlation for bound vs. scattering states, time evolution confirming localization, and parameter maps showing $\Delta n_f$ dependence on interaction and hopping rates.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the basic NHSE in bosons, to deriving the effective Hamiltonian for the interacting bound states, demonstrating the drag-induced transfer of the skin effect, and finally mapping the parameter dependence and stability of this interaction-mediated localization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The non-Hermitian skin effect (NHSE) represents one of the most distinctive phenomena in non-Hermitian physics. Here, we uncover a new drag-induced NHSE mechanism in interacting Bose--Fermi mixtures where only bosons and not fermions experience asymmetric hoppings. %While bosons exhibit intrinsic skin localization due to asymmetric hopping, fermions remain Hermitian in isolation and do not independently support NHSE. We show that strong Bose--Fermi interactions enable fermions to inherit boundary accumulation through correlated bound states. %In the few-body regime, The interplay of interactions, quantum statistics, and non-Hermitian dynamics gives rise to an interaction-induced blockade mechanism, leading to highly asymmetric fermionic transport. We demonstrate that the drag-induced NHSE is dynamically stable and propose a feasible realization in ultracold Bose--Fermi mixtures with Floquet-engineered asymmetric tunneling. Our results establish a general interaction-mediated mechanism for emergent non-Hermitian localization in hybrid quantum matter.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03343"></a>
### [Observation of residual entanglement in chip-based entanglement purification](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03343v1)

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03343_figures/2606.03343_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Experimental demonstration of the residual entangle- ment as a function of the polarization-state fidelity Fp under different circuit configurations. For Fp &lt; Fs, the original purification circuit is applied (light blue arrow), whereas for Fp &gt; Fs, the reconfigured circuit indicated by the light red arrows is used. The experimental results for the residual en- tanglement after purification also show good agreement with the theoretical calculation.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper reports the experimental observation of residual entanglement in a chip-based entanglement purification system utilizing hyperentanglement. By leveraging the reconfigurability of integrated photonics, the authors show that usable residual entanglement can always be obtained, even when noise affects the different quantum degrees of freedom unequally. This advances the feasibility of building robust, on-chip quantum repeaters.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a highly practical, integrated platform demonstration for quantum networking, showing how to salvage valuable quantum information (residual entanglement) that would otherwise be lost in noisy channels.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of maximizing the utility of quantum resources by observing and utilizing residual entanglement generated during entanglement purification processes.

**Main result.** The authors experimentally demonstrate a chip-based scheme that reliably obtains residual entanglement suitable for further purification, even when the error rates across different degrees of freedom are unbalanced.

**Method.** They implemented a single-copy entanglement purification scheme using integrated silicon photonics, analyzing coincidence detection events to distinguish between successfully distilled and residual entangled states.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is chip-based quantum optics using silicon photonics, which supports hyperentanglement encoding across multiple degrees of freedom (e.g., polarization and spatial modes). The system is designed to simulate and mitigate bit-flip and phase-flip noise.

**Key observables.** Fidelity of distilled entanglement and residual entanglement; coincidence rates; and the ability to maintain residual fidelity above a critical threshold (e.g., 0.5).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The relative fidelities of the two degrees of freedom ($F_p$ vs $F_s$); noise types (BF, PF, white noise); and the reconfigurability of the integrated photonic circuits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the initial state is a hyperentangled state and relies on the reconfigurability of integrated photonics to manage unequal error rates between DOFs.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the chip layout, experimental setup, and crucial results showing the fidelity of both distilled and residual entanglement under various noise conditions (BF, PF, white noise).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the need for purification, detailing the chip-based hyperentanglement setup, presenting the methodology for observing residual entanglement from failed events, and finally quantifying the success of the scheme under different noise models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement purification is an essential component of quantum repeaters, as it can improve the fidelity of the distributed entangled states and mitigate the effects of the noisy channel. Successful purification yields entangled states with increased fidelity, whereas failed events can still retain residual entanglement that remains usable for further purification when the error rates of the two degrees of freedom (DOFs) are unbalanced. In this paper, we demonstrate a single-copy entanglement purification scheme based on hyperentanglement using silicon chips and experimentally observe the presence of residual entanglement. Leveraging the reconfigurability of integrated photonics, our scheme ensures that, under bit-flip noise acting on the two DOFs, residual entanglement suitable for further purification can always be obtained, regardless of which DOF has the higher error rate. Our results demonstrate the advantages of integrated photonics for quantum information processing and provide guidance for the optimized utilization of entanglement resources in on-chip entanglement purification and future quantum repeater systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.02942"></a>
### [Maximizing Information Flow in Three-Coin Quantum Walk: from Initial Entanglement to Integrated Photonic Implementation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02942v1)

**Authors:** Seyed Mohsen Moosavi Khansari  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02942v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **3/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.02942_figures/2606.02942_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Mutual information 𝐼(𝐶; 𝑃; 𝑡) versus step number 𝑡 for initial states ∣000⟩ (green dashed), ∣ 111⟩ (red dashed), and GHZ (blue solid) with 𝛼= 1, 𝐿= 5. The GHZ state starts lower (1.62 bits) but surpasses the separable states from 𝑡= 3 onward, reaching a value of 3.00 bits at 𝑡= 10, which is approximately 18% higher than the value for the ∣000⟩ state (2.54 bits) and 12% higher than for ∣ 111⟩ (2.68 bits). The non-monotonic behaviour stems from quantum interference due to initial entanglement.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.02942_figures/2606.02942_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Mutual information 𝐼(𝐶: 𝑃) after 𝑡= 5 steps as a function of the staying parameter 𝛼 for the initial states ∣000⟩ (green), ∣111⟩ (red), and GHZ (blue). The separable states exhibit a monotonic increase with 𝛼, while the GHZ state shows a distinct maximum at 𝛼≈0.71. The inset provides a magnified view of the maximum region.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work investigates how to maximize information flow in a three-coin quantum walk by controlling the initial entanglement and the probability of non-displacement. The key finding is that initial three-partite entanglement is a powerful resource, and optimal performance is achieved by tuning a specific control parameter $\alpha$. The authors conclude by proposing a scalable implementation using integrated photonics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It directly addresses the use of entanglement as a quantifiable resource to enhance quantum transport and information processing, linking abstract quantum information measures to concrete physical implementations in photonics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to maximize the rate of information flow in a quantum walk system by controlling the initial entanglement and the dynamics of the walk.

**Main result.** Initial three-partite entanglement significantly accelerates the growth of mutual information, and the optimal information flow is achieved by tuning a non-displacement amplitude parameter $\alpha$ to a specific intermediate value ($\alpha \approx 0.71$).

**Method.** The authors use numerical simulation of the quantum state vector and compute the quantum mutual information $I(C; P; t)$ between the coin and position subsystems.

**Model / system.** The model is a Three-Coin Quantum Walk (TCQW) on a 1D lattice, where movement is conditionally coupled to the state of three entangled coins. The system is proposed to be implemented using integrated photonics utilizing polarization, spatial modes, and time bins.

**Key observables.** Mutual Information $I(C; P; t)$, Position Entropy $S(\rho_p(t))$, and the tunable parameter $\alpha$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The initial state (separable vs. GHZ-type entanglement) and the non-displacement amplitude $\alpha$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the feasibility of implementing the walk using integrated photonics and models the dynamics based on the conditional movement rule.

**Figures summary.** Figures plot the mutual information $I(C: P; t)$ versus time $t$ for different initial states, and also plot $I(C: P)$ versus the tuning parameter $\alpha$ to identify the optimal control point.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the TCQW model, analyzes the effect of initial entanglement on mutual information growth, determines the optimal control parameter $\alpha$ via entropy analysis, and concludes with a detailed proposal for an integrated photonic realization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Discrete-time quantum walks are powerful platforms for simulating quantum transport and information processing. Here we introduce a walker on a one-dimensional lattice whose motion is controlled by three entangled coins, each initialized with the Hadamard gate, aiming to maximize information flow. The walker moves only when all three coins yield the same outcome (HHH or TTT), thus coupling the 8-dimensional coin Hilbert space to the position degree of freedom. By analyzing fully separable, fully entangled (GHZ-type) and intermediate initial states, and using the von Neumann entropy of reduced subsystems, we compute the mutual information $I(C;P;t)$ between coin and position. The results show that initial three-partite entanglement accelerates the growth of mutual information by up to 18\% after ten steps (when compared to the lower of the two separable states), although it exhibits short-term non-monotonic dynamics due to quantum interference. For the first time, we introduce a tunable parameter $α$ (amplitude of non-displacement states) and show that the GHZ state reaches a maximum of mutual information at $α\approx 0.71$ - a key finding for optimal control of information flow. Finally, an integrated photonic implementation using polarization, spatial modes and time bins is proposed, where $α$ can be tuned with nonlinear or electro-optic elements. A scalable numerical framework (Python code) for simulations up to $t = 5$ steps is provided. Our findings establish three-partite entanglement as a dynamical resource for maximizing information flow and spatial spreading, with direct applications in quantum state transfer, entanglement-assisted sensing and programmable photonic quantum processors.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a novel architecture for creating high-coherence quantum memories by coupling a superconducting quantum circuit to a solid-state spin ensemble. By using a parametric pump, the researchers induced dynamic strong coupling, enabling fast and controllable quantum state transfer between the two disparate quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the hardware challenge of quantum memory integration by coupling superconducting circuits (a major QI platform) to long-coherence solid-state spins, which is a key area in quantum networking and computation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to achieve efficient, on-demand, and tunable quantum state transfer between superconducting quantum circuits and solid-state spin ensembles to build high-coherence quantum memories.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated dynamic strong coupling using a parametric pump, enabling fast quantum state transfer between the superconducting circuit and the spin ensemble.

**Method.** The research employed microwave spectroscopy, measuring transmission and reflection, and analyzing avoided crossings induced by a parametric pump tone.

**Model / system.** The system is a hybrid device coupling a superconducting quantum circuit (SNAIL mixer) to a microwave cavity, which is dispersively coupled to a rare-earth spin ensemble ($	ext{}^{171}	ext{Yb}^{3+}:	ext{YSO}$). The dynamics are modeled by coupled Hamiltonian terms involving the circuit, cavity, and spin operators.

**Key observables.** Avoided crossings in spectroscopy, measured coupling strengths ($	ilde{g}_{mc}$, $	ilde{g}_{ms}$), and the resulting state transfer efficiency.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Operating temperature ($10 	ext{ mK}$), static couplings ($\approx 27 	ext{ MHz}$), and the pump amplitude ($\xi$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the dispersive regime approximation and the use of the Rotating-Wave Approximation (RWA) for modeling the coupled dynamics.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the experimental setup (Josephson element $ightarrow$ cavity $ightarrow$ spin ensemble), mode characterization via spectroscopy, and the measurement scheme for pump-activated interactions showing avoided crossings.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from characterizing the static couplings in the dispersive regime to demonstrating the dynamic strong coupling mechanism using a parametric pump, culminating in the prediction of fast state transfer.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Efficient quantum state transfer between superconducting circuits and solid-state spins would unlock high-coherence quantum memories for superconducting quantum processors. We demonstrate dynamically controlled strong coupling between a Josephson circuit and a rare-earth spin ensemble. Using a parametric pump, we realize on-demand coupling of several MHz, which will enable faithful state transfer between quantum circuits and spins. Our architecture enables quantum control of spin ensembles, and paves the way for hybrid memories with coherence far beyond those of superconducting circuits alone.

</details>

### [Two-mode collapse and revival of quantum coherent state in a tilted optical lattice](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03630v1)

**Summary.** This paper investigates the collapse and revival dynamics of quantum coherence in bosons trapped in a tilted optical lattice. The key finding is the emergence of a two-mode oscillation, where the revival frequency depends on both the particle interactions and the external tilt. By combining experiments and theory, the authors reveal universal scaling laws governing this novel collective dynamic feature.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides deep insight into non-equilibrium collective dynamics in interacting quantum systems, showing how external driving fields (tilt) can couple to intrinsic interaction dynamics, leading to predictable, universal scaling laws.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the collective dynamics, specifically the collapse and revival (CR) of phase coherence, in quantum coherent states of bosons confined in a tilted optical lattice.

**Main result.** The authors report the first observation of two-mode CR dynamics, showing that the revival frequencies are governed by both inter-site interactions and the external tilt, particularly when the tilt is weaker than the interaction.

**Method.** The research combines experimental measurements using band mapping to determine the coherent fraction with theoretical modeling and numerical simulations (DMRG/TEBD) to analyze the underlying physics.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled as one-dimensional (1D) ultracold bosons in an optical lattice subjected to a linear tilt potential, described by a Bose-Hubbard type Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Coherent fraction ($f_c$), its spectrum ($e_{fc}$), and the characteristic collapse and revival periods ($T_r$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Tunneling strength ($J$), on-site interaction ($U$), and the tilt energy ($E$). The study compares regimes where $U$ dominates versus where $E$ becomes comparable to $U$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes the system can be effectively reduced to 1D tubes, and theoretical derivations rely on perturbation theory and small parameter approximations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the experimental setup, the time evolution of the coherent fraction $f_c$ showing beating patterns, and plots demonstrating the universal linear scaling of the two-mode amplitudes around a critical crossing point.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical model and experimental measurements of CR dynamics to identifying the two distinct modes (U-mode and E-mode). It then develops analytic models and simulations to predict the crossover between these modes and demonstrates the universal linear scaling of their amplitudes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Collective dynamics is an important out-of-equilibrium feature of quantum coherent states and usually reflects the intrinsic properties of the state. Collapse and revival (CR) dynamics of phase coherence is a well-known example for bosonic coherent states, which is usually induced by applying a quench. Previous studies have shown that the CR frequency is governed solely by interactions, even in the presence of a tilt quench. However, whether such interaction-dominated oscillation is a universal feature remains unknown. In this work, we show that an ensemble of one-dimensional bosons can undergo two-mode CR, with frequencies set by both the interaction and the tilt, particularly when the tilt is weaker than the interaction. The newly discovered tilt mode is enabled by tunneling between lattice sites. When the two modes coexist, the amplitudes of both modes exhibit universal linear scaling for various tilts. These findings clarify the general features of CR dynamics in tilted lattice models and the underlying mechanism, and provide deeper insight into collective dynamics in correlated systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.03950"></a>
### [Coexistence of dipolar and quadrupolar higher-order topology](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03950v1)

**Authors:** Konstantin Rodionenko, Maxim Mazanov, Maxim A. Gorlach  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03950v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `analog quantum simulation` **4/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a physical realization, using coupled optical waveguides, that supports a mixed higher-order topological insulator exhibiting both dipolar and quadrupolar topological characteristics simultaneously. By analyzing the system's Hamiltonian and using advanced numerical techniques, the authors confirm the coexistence of these two distinct topological invariants in a single structure. This provides a crucial platform for engineering complex topological states in integrated photonics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO/Quantum Optics as it proposes a concrete, experimentally realizable platform (optical waveguides) to host and study complex topological phases beyond simple SSH models, linking fundamental topology to photonics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the theoretical tension in higher-order topological insulators (HOTIs) regarding the non-overlapping nature of dipolar and quadrupolar bulk moments, aiming to demonstrate a system exhibiting both simultaneously.

**Main result.** The authors propose and analyze a model that successfully hosts a mixed dipolar-quadrupolar higher-order topological phase, confirmed by observing both corner and edge localized states.

**Method.** The study employs Bloch Hamiltonian formulations, nested Wilson loop techniques, and full-wave numerical simulations to calculate topological invariants and analyze edge/corner spectra.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is suggested to be arrays of laser-written evanescently coupled optical waveguides. The model is based on a two-orbital or single-orbital meta-atom lattice, approximated by generalized Su-Schrieffer-Heeger (SSH) chains.

**Key observables.** Quantized bulk quadrupole moment ($q_{xy}$), bulk polarization ($\mathbf{P}$), corner-localized modes, and edge-localized modes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling strengths ($\kappa, \gamma, \Delta$), lattice geometry parameters ($J', \alpha$), and disorder strengths ($\deltaeta, \kappa, \gamma, \Delta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximating the system with effective two-band models and assumes that the topological character can be separated into dipolar and quadrupolar subsectors via Wannier center analysis.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the construction of the two-orbital model, band structures for different topological phases, Wannier bands showing polarization, and finite-size spectra highlighting spectrally separated corner and edge states.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the theoretical conflict to proposing a specific lattice model, deriving the Hamiltonian, calculating topological invariants using Wilson loops, and finally confirming the coexistence of both topologies via numerical simulations on a finite lattice.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Two-dimensional higher-order topological insulators are typically classified either as dipolar or quadrupolar depending on the relevant invariant. These two classes were previously considered non-overlapping. Here we put forward an example system exhibiting dipolar and quadrupolar higher-order topology simultaneously, suggest its implementation using the arrays of laser-written evanescently coupled optical waveguides and support our conclusions by the full-wave numerical simulations.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper presents a novel, fast protocol for preparing single atoms in optical tweezer arrays by leveraging intra-tweezer Rydberg blockade and autoionization. By operating on a microsecond timescale, the method significantly overcomes the slow cycle time limitations of current quantum circuit implementations. The research highlights the critical role of minimizing multi-atom loss to achieve scalable, high-fidelity quantum computation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the scalability bottleneck in neutral atom quantum computing platforms by providing a fast, high-fidelity method for preparing single-atom qubits in arrays.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary bottleneck in continuously replenished optical tweezer arrays for quantum circuits is the slow cycle time associated with removing excess atoms from multiply occupied tweezers.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrated a microsecond-scale protocol using intra-tweezer Rydberg blockade and autoionization, achieving single-atom filling fractions up to 74.8% and showing a significant speedup over conventional methods.

**Method.** They propose and experimentally demonstrate a selective 'remove-one' protocol by cycling Rydberg excitation and autoionization to reduce multi-atom occupancy rapidly.

**Model / system.** The system involves neutral atoms (specifically Yb) trapped in optical tweezer arrays. The physics relies on controlled Rydberg blockade interactions and autoionization dynamics.

**Key observables.** Single-atom filling fraction ($P^*(1)$), multi-atom probability ($P(>1)$), and the time scale required to reach a target occupancy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The protocol operates on the microsecond timescale, contrasting with millisecond timescales of previous methods; key regimes include two-photon vs. single-photon excitation.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The final filling fraction is limited by an unexplained two-body loss mechanism ($N 	o N-2$), which is the primary remaining challenge.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate collective Rabi oscillations for various atom numbers and show the time evolution of the filling fraction versus the target multi-atom probability threshold for both excitation regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theory and experimental demonstration by first establishing the need for fast cycling, then comparing two excitation regimes (two-photon vs. single-photon), and finally analyzing the limiting loss mechanisms and optimizing the pulse sequence.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Continuously replenished optical tweezer arrays will unlock unlimited-depth quantum circuits with neutral atom qubits. A key bottleneck limiting the cycle time of these systems is removing atoms from tweezers initially loaded with more than one atom. In the conventional technique of light-assisted collisions, slow collisional dynamics limit the timescale for removing excess atoms to several milliseconds. Here, we propose and demonstrate a scheme for selectively removing one atom at a time from multiply occupied tweezers on a microsecond timescale, using intra-tweezer Rydberg blockade and autoionization. We demonstrate the protocol in $^{171}$Yb in two complementary regimes. With two-photon Rydberg excitation from the ground state, we reduce multi-atom probability to 1% in 64.8 $μ$s, while retaining single atoms in 58.2(2)% of the tweezers, which is comparable to the filling fraction achieved with light-assisted collisions under the same experimental conditions, but over two orders of magnitude faster. With single-photon excitation from the metastable state $^3P_0$, reduced single-atom loss enables a higher filling fraction of 74.8(3)%, at the cost of additional temporal overhead to prepare the atoms in $^3P_0$. The final filling fraction is limited by an unexplained two-body loss mechanism, which, if solved, could enable fast, quasi-deterministic loading.

</details>

### [FPGA Based Feedforward System for Photonic Quantum Computing Applications](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03500v1)

**Authors:** Daniel Duggan, Simon Filgis, Axel B. Bregnsbo, Jürgen Saalmüller, Jonas S. Neergaard-Nielsen, Tobias Wintermantel, Ulrik L. Andersen

**Summary.** This paper presents an FPGA-based fast feedforward system designed to enable real-time signal processing for Continuous Variable Measurement-Based Quantum Information Processing. By integrating a high-efficiency homodyne detector with low-latency digital electronics, the system overcomes the limitations of post-processing, allowing for deterministic, real-time quantum gate operations in photonic quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it provides a concrete, high-speed engineering solution (FPGA) to a critical bottleneck—real-time control—in the experimental realization of photonic quantum computation protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is enabling low-latency, real-time signal processing required for Continuous Variables (CV) Measurement-Based Quantum Information Processing (MB-QIP) protocols.

**Main result.** The authors successfully implemented and demonstrated an FPGA-based fast feedforward system capable of performing necessary signal conditioning and logic operations in real-time with a total system latency of 196 ns.

**Method.** The system uses an FPGA to process continuous measurement outcomes from a homodyne detector, performing real-time feedforward corrections necessary to maintain the quantum state during measurement-based computation.

**Model / system.** The system is built around a high quantum efficiency, fully fibre-based homodyne detector coupled to an FPGA. This platform processes CV observables ($\hat{x}, \hat{p}$) derived from quantum harmonic oscillator models, which are central to CV MB-QIP.

**Key observables.** Quadrature measurements ($\hat{x}, \hat{p}$), system latency (196 ns), detector clearance (15 dB at 1 GHz), and phase output oscillation patterns.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Quantum efficiencies $> 95\%$, total system latency of 196 ns, and the need to correct deterministic displacements in phase space.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the required feedforward corrections can be calculated and applied faster than the measurement rate, bridging the gap between theory and physical implementation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the system architecture (FPGA components, data flow), the performance of the homodyne detector (clearance vs. frequency), and simulated/measured phase output oscillations confirming real-time operation.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the theoretical need for real-time feedforward in CV MB-QIP, details the physical components (homodyne detector, FPGA), describes the implementation of the feedforward calculation (inner products, CORDIC), and concludes with experimental verification of the low-latency performance.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Field-programmable gate arrays provide a high-performance solution for real-time signal processing in emerging quantum and photonic technologies. We present an FPGA-based fast feedforward system, that incorporates a high quantum efficiency fully fibre based homodyne detector, to enable low-latency signal processing critical for continuous variables (CV) measurement-based quantum information processing (MB-QIP) protocols. CV MB-QIP typically relies on adaptive measurements and/or displacements via feedforward to achieve scalability and universality, but existing implementations typically handle these operations in post-processing, limiting real-time applicability. Our system performs signal acquisition, conditioning, and logic operations in real-time, meeting the tight latency requirements of photonic quantum computing protocols. The detector exhibits a large clearance of 15 dB at 1 GHz with 4 mW linear oscillator and quantum efficiencies of >95% with a total system latency of 196 ns. This work highlights the role of FPGAs in bridging the gap between theoretical models and physical implementations in photonics-based technologies

</details>

**Authors:** Yuqing Wang, Zhongchi Zhang, Tao Zhang, Yuxuan Liao, Hanteng Wang, Ye Tian, Binjie Ji, Yujia Wu, Luming Ma, Chen Qing, Chengshu Li, Wei Zhang, Yidong Huang, Wenjun Zhang, Xue Feng, Wenlan Chen, Hui Zhai  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02715v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `analog quantum simulation` **4/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **3/5** · `Rydberg arrays` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.02715_figures/2606.02715_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

<sub>FIG. 2. Large-scale single-atom trapping performance in the 18,225-site array. Each bright spot represents a detected single atom; this single shot shows 11400 trapped atoms. (b) Statistical distribution of the filling fraction over 100 consecutive experimental iterations. **Summary.** This paper demonstrates a breakthrough in scaling atom-based quantum computers by trapping 11,000 atoms using a tweezer array generated by a single metasurface. This approach overcomes limitations of traditional optics, enabling the realization of large-scale quantum processors. Furthermore, the authors analyze the array's connectivity using percolation theory, providing both a physical platform and a theoretical characterization of the system's disorder.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges advanced optical engineering (metasurfaces) with fundamental quantum simulation challenges (scaling and disorder), providing a robust platform for studying many-body physics in controllable, large-scale arrays.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central challenge in quantum computing is scaling the number of physical qubits, which is limited by the number of controllable trapped atoms.

**Main result.** The authors successfully trapped 11,000 individual atoms in a tweezer array generated by a single metasurface, reaching the tens-of-thousands scale for qubit resources.

**Method.** They utilized a single metasurface to generate the entire tweezer array, avoiding complex in-vacuum optics, and characterized the resulting random array using percolation theory.

**Model / system.** The system uses neutral atoms (e.g., Rb) trapped in an optical tweezer array generated by a metasurface. The array size is large (18,225 sites), and the collective properties are analyzed using percolation theory.

**Key observables.** Average trapped atom count ($\sim 11,022$), site-resolved loading probability ($\sim 60\%$), percolation probability $P(p, L)$, and trap depth ($\sim 0.3$ mK).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Metasurface diameter ($\sim 2$ cm), working distance ($\sim 1.5$ cm), and the critical percolation probability ($p_c \approx 0.59$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The metasurface is placed outside the vacuum cell to avoid technical complications, and the analysis accounts for propagation through air and glass.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the experimental setup, raw single-shot fluorescence images confirming $\sim 11,000$ atoms, and plots demonstrating the percolation transition using finite-size scaling.

**Paper structure.** The paper details the experimental setup using the metasurface, quantifies the trapping of a large number of atoms, and then applies statistical theory (percolation) to characterize the connectivity and disorder within the randomly loaded array.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The scalability of physical qubit numbers is a central challenge toward a universal fault-tolerant quantum computer. The inherent scalability of atom array quantum computers stems from the identical nature of atomic qubits, so the available qubit resource is primarily limited by the number of atoms that can be trapped and controlled. Here, we robustly trap 11,000 individual atoms in a tweezer array, thereby enabling the available qubit resource to reach the tens-of-thousands scale for the first time among all quantum computation platforms. This advance is enabled by a single metasurface, approximately 2 cm in diameter, that generates the entire tweezer array without the need for microscope objectives, thereby maximizing laser-power efficiency. The large aperture ensures a working distance of about 1.5 cm, allowing the metasurface to be placed outside the vacuum cell and avoiding the technical complications of in-vacuum operation. We further characterize the randomly loaded atom array using the statistical theory of percolation phase transitions. This work takes an important first step toward a quantum computer at the 10,000-qubit scale.

</details>
