**Summary.** This tutorial serves as an essential experimental guide for characterizing superconducting transmon qubits. It walks the reader through the entire process, covering everything from initial setup and single-qubit spectroscopy to advanced measurements of anharmonicity, readout fidelity, and qubit-qubit coupling. The paper is crucial for experimentalists aiming to bring up and operate transmon-based quantum processors.

**Why it may be interesting.** For a theoretical physicist, this paper provides a rich experimental realization of open quantum systems dynamics, offering concrete measurements of decoherence rates ($T_1, T_2$) and the practical application of error mitigation protocols like dynamical decoupling and composite pulses.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper provides a comprehensive, practical, and step-by-step guide for experimentalists to characterize and optimize tunable transmon qubits.

**Main result.** It details the entire workflow, from cryogenic setup and flux sweet-spot identification to single-shot readout and qubit-qubit coupling characterization.

**Method.** The methodology involves a combination of microwave spectroscopy (one-tone/two-tone), dispersive readout measurements, and advanced error characterization techniques like dynamical decoupling and composite pulses.

**Model / system.** The physical system is superconducting transmon qubits, whose dynamics are modeled using Hamiltonians that approximate weakly anharmonic quantum oscillators. The characterization relies on mapping bosonic operators to Pauli operators in the two-level approximation.

**Key observables.** Qubit transition frequency ($\omega_q$), anharmonicity ($\alpha$), readout fidelity ($f$), effective coupling strength ($J$), and coherence times ($T_1, T_2$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Operating regimes include the dispersive regime ($g \ll \Delta$), and key parameters involve flux bias ($\Phi$), detuning ($\Omega$), and pulse parameters (amplitude, duration).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The procedures are highly dependent on the specific chip layout, fabrication details, and measurement hardware, and many advanced techniques rely on the qubit being well-described by the two-level approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the qubit energy level structure, Bloch sphere representations, resonator response vs. probe power (bare vs. dressed frequency), qubit spectroscopy vs. flux bias (showing the sweet spot), and fidelity decay curves comparing standard vs. composite pulses.

**Paper structure.** The tutorial progresses logically: first covering basic Hamiltonian models and setup; then detailing single-qubit control (Rabi oscillations, anharmonicity); followed by readout optimization (dispersive coupling, heterodyne detection); and finally addressing multi-qubit interactions and error mitigation (coupling strength $J$, composite pulses, DD).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Superconducting transmon qubits are a leading technology for quantum information processing, yet their reliable operation rests on meticulous calibration and characterization routines. These processes have been fine-tuned and are relatively well understood by the quantum computing community. Nevertheless, it is often challenging for newcomers to compile all the available information into a practical experimental flow. In this tutorial, we present a comprehensive walkthrough for the characterization and optimization of tunable transmon qubits, demonstrated on a commercial five-qubit processor. Moving beyond theoretical description, we detail in a straightforward manner the complete workflow, from cryogenic setup and wiring to parametric amplifier optimum operation, flux sweet-spot identification, pulse calibration, and readout optimization. We also demonstrate the characterization of qubit-qubit coupling, covering all steps before multiqubit operations. This guide serves as a reference for experimentalists seeking to efficiently bring up transmon-based quantum devices.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper experimentally investigates Thouless pumping in an atomic Bose-Einstein condensate, demonstrating that interactions can induce fast, non-adiabatic topological transport of matter-wave solitons. By modulating the lattice and tuning interactions, the researchers achieved quantized pumping regimes inaccessible under purely adiabatic conditions. This opens new avenues for engineering fast topological quantum devices using ultracold atoms.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly bridges concepts from topological physics (Thouless pumping) with experimental quantum simulation using ultracold atoms, providing a clean platform to study nonlinear, interaction-driven quantum phenomena.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how interparticle interactions influence topological transport, specifically Thouless pumping, in periodically driven quantum systems, aiming to operate beyond the limitations of adiabatic driving.

**Main result.** The authors observed fast, interaction-induced Thouless pumping of matter-wave solitons at intermediate interaction strengths, and demonstrated nonadiabatic quantized pumping at high modulation frequencies.

**Method.** The research uses experimental realization in an atomic Bose-Einstein condensate, measuring the center-of-mass displacement and participation ratio to diagnose topological transport.

**Model / system.** The physical system is an atomic Bose-Einstein condensate (BEC) of ${}^{133}	ext{Cs}$ atoms trapped in a periodically modulated lattice in momentum space. The dynamics are modeled by a nonlinear Schrödinger equation incorporating both lattice hopping and mean-field interaction terms.

**Key observables.** Center-of-mass displacement ($\delta D$), participation ratio ($r$), and the Chern number (quantized pumping value).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength ($U$), modulation frequency ($\Omega$), and modulation amplitude ($\Delta J/J_m$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the mean-field approximation, and initial state preparation is noted as an approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the experimental setup, the dependence of the Chern number on interaction, and measurements of $\delta D$ and $r$ over pump cycles for varying interaction and modulation parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by introducing the physical platform and model, presenting experimental measurements of key observables ($\delta D, r$) across different interaction regimes, and finally developing the theoretical understanding of the nonadiabatic pumping mechanism.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Thouless pumping provides a paradigmatic platform for studying the effects of interactions on topological transport in periodically driven systems. However, most studies have been constrained by adiabatic conditions, which preclude exploration of interaction-driven novel topological states at high driving frequencies. Here, we experimentally investigate the interplay between interaction and modulation frequency in Thouless pumping realized in a periodically modulated lattice in momentum space of atomic Bose-Einstein condensate. We observe fast Thouless pumping of matterwave solitons at intermediate interactions, with no counterpart in the non- or weakly interacting regimes. Beyond the boundary of topological phase transition induced by interaction, nonadiabatic quantized pumping of solitons emerges at high modulation frequencies over a broad interaction range, in good agreement with theoretical calculations, while the solitons remain trapped in the low-frequency adiabatic pumping regime. Our work opens new avenues for accelerating topological transport in driven quantum systems and engineering fast topological devices.

</details>

### [Fast Tensor Network Imaginary Time Evolution by Implicit Stepping on Logarithmic Grids](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02930v1)

**Authors:** John P. Zima, E. Miles Stoudenmire, Steven R. White, Olivier Parcollet, Jason Kaye  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02930v1>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a novel, highly efficient technique for simulating imaginary time evolution of quantum many-body systems using Tensor Networks. By combining logarithmic time grids with A-stable implicit solvers, the method achieves a dramatic reduction in computational cost, scaling logarithmically with the required evolution time. This breakthrough allows for accurate simulations of complex models like the Anderson impurity model to very low temperatures.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, scalable numerical tool for simulating thermal and ground-state properties of strongly correlated materials, directly impacting research in high-Tc superconductivity and quantum impurity physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to achieve an efficient and stable method for performing imaginary time evolution of quantum many-body wavefunctions represented by Matrix Product States (MPS).

**Main result.** The combination of using a logarithmic time grid and A-stable implicit time-stepping methods allows for an exponential reduction in the required number of time steps compared to standard uniform-step approaches.

**Method.** The authors employ A-stable implicit time-stepping methods (like the trapezoid rule) applied to the imaginary time evolution ODE, utilizing a logarithmic time grid to resolve long-time dynamics efficiently.

**Model / system.** The method is tested on the Heisenberg spin chain and the single-site Anderson impurity model with a metallic bath, which are canonical models in strongly correlated electron physics.

**Key observables.** Overlap error, Kondo temperature dependence, and the double occupancy $D(T)$ in the Anderson model.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The maximum imaginary time $	au_{\max}$ (or inverse temperature $eta$), and the bond dimension $\chi$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes the Hamiltonian is positive semi-definite and relies on the stability properties of A-stable implicit solvers, which overcome the stability constraints of explicit methods.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare CPU time and overlap error showing logarithmic scaling for advanced methods on the Heisenberg chain, and demonstrate the collapse of $D(T)/D(0)$ vs. $T/T_K(U)$ for the Anderson model.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem, details the logarithmic grid and implicit solver combination, validates the approach on the Heisenberg chain (showing speedup), and applies it to the Anderson impurity model to observe physical phenomena like the Kondo temperature.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a new method for the efficient imaginary time evolution of quantum many-body wavefunctions represented by matrix product states (MPS). We first show that logarithmic time grids are sufficient to resolve long imaginary time dynamics, yielding an exponential reduction in the number of time steps compared with standard approaches. We then show that A-stable implicit time-stepping methods for ordinary differential equations allow stable propagation for any time step size. The resulting scheme requires only matrix-vector products and linear solves, standard operations in the MPS toolbox. We validate our approach with two examples: a Heisenberg spin chain, which we use to demonstrate a speedup of several orders of magnitude over the standard time-dependent variational principle method with uniform time steps, and a single-site Anderson impurity model with a metallic bath, for which propagation to large imaginary times allows one to observe the exponential dependence of the Kondo temperature on the interaction strength.

</details>

### [Efficient Quantum Error Mitigation for Unitary k-Designs](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03891v1)

(c) Depolarizing error channels commute, so we can gather the two-qubit depolariz- ing elements in blocks of fully-connected motifs; likewise, we gather single-qubit depolarizing elements in complete layers of gates. (c) By permuting the qubit labels in the register (which is simply a form of layout selection for an all-to-all connected device), we enforce similar total local depolarization across qubit pairs. (d) Using the relationships determined through benchmarking...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a novel, overhead-free error mitigation protocol for quantum circuits approximating unitary k-designs. It combines 'circuit balancing' with Pauli twirling to estimate and invert global depolarization caused by both depolarizing and coherent noise. The technique demonstrates significant fidelity improvements on superconducting quantum hardware, making complex simulations more feasible on noisy devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** The focus on mitigating errors in circuits designed to mimic complex physical dynamics (like quantum chaos) is highly relevant for simulating strongly correlated or non-equilibrium quantum systems using current NISQ hardware.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing efficient quantum error mitigation techniques for quantum circuits representing unitary k-designs, especially in the presence of both depolarizing and coherent errors, without incurring significant quantum overhead.

**Main result.** The proposed method significantly reduces average random circuit infidelity on both simulated and real superconducting quantum hardware (IBM Fez) without adding two-qubit gate overhead.

**Method.** The authors introduce 'circuit balancing' to estimate global depolarization by leveraging the structure of k-design Pauli support distributions, followed by inverting the noise model using Pauli twirling.

**Model / system.** The work analyzes quantum circuits approximating unitary k-designs, relevant for simulating quantum chaos (e.g., near black holes). Testing is performed on superconducting quantum computers (IBM Fez) and simulated for various connectivity graphs.

**Key observables.** Mean Hellinger distance, Loschmidt echo survival probability, and estimated global depolarizing parameters ($\lambda_{	ext{avg}, 2Q}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Depolarizing parameter ($\lambda$), coherent error angle ($	heta$), circuit depth, and the number of twirls ($t$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes the availability of regular device benchmarking data and relies on asymptotic scaling arguments for the required number of twirls.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare Hellinger distance reduction across different error models (depolarizing only vs. depolarizing + coherent) and show that circuit balancing and twirling stabilize fidelity on real hardware.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the need for mitigation in k-designs, deriving theoretical bounds for global depolarization on structured graphs, introducing circuit balancing to optimize local error variance, and finally applying Pauli twirling to invert the noise model for practical error suppression.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum circuit ensembles that have the properties of unitary k-designs represent applications where there is no obvious bias toward any particular Pauli support, as is the case in simulating systems exhibiting ''quantum chaos,'' which range from quantum dynamics near black holes to gapless spin fluid analysis. However, noisy hardware makes quantum circuits prone to a myriad of error sources, of which depolarizing and coherent error can be particularly destructive. To combat depolarizing error, popular techniques typically involve circuit or gate folding, which can be time-intensive procedures due to increased circuit depth and shot overhead. Other tensor-network-based mitigation techniques suffer from intractability in high-entanglement regimes. In this work, we leverage the structure of unitary k-design Pauli support distributions by introducing a technique we name ''circuit balancing,'' along with gate benchmarking data, in order to estimate circuit-wide depolarization. We describe how to invert the diagnosed circuit depolarization even in the presence of coherent error, via Pauli twirling. We provide asymptotics to estimate the number of twirls needed to maintain a desired output fidelity. We test our method numerically in a variety of simulation settings and find that it can significantly reduce average random circuit infidelity. Further, we employ our methods to find significant infidelity reductions when running a random circuit ensemble on a contemporary superconducting quantum computer, IBM Fez. Overall, we show that the method effectively reduces gate-based error for unitary k-designs without incurring any two-qubit gate overhead.

</details>

### [Exact Solutions for Spin Conserving Models and the Wigner-Araki-Yanase Theorem](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02861v1)

**Authors:** Michael Steiner, Ronald Rendell  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02861v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **3/5**

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

**Summary.** This paper re-examines the Wigner-Araki-Yanase theorem by developing a general model based on exact total angular momentum conservation. Using both density matrix tracing and Kraus operators, the authors show that measurement outcomes are constrained by apparatus uncertainties, providing a deeper physical understanding of measurement limitations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to open quantum systems and quantum measurement theory, providing a rigorous, generalized framework for understanding how conservation laws constrain measurement outcomes beyond standard assumptions.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the fundamental limitations of quantum measurement in the presence of additive conservation laws, specifically addressing the Wigner-Araki-Yanase (WAY) theorem.

**Main result.** By developing a general angular momentum conserving model of measurement, the authors provide a simple explanation for the major implications of the WAY theorem, showing that measurement errors depend on apparatus uncertainty.

**Method.** The analysis uses both tracing out the apparatus from the density matrix and developing a system-only channel model based on Kraus operators to analyze the measurement process.

**Model / system.** The model involves a quantum system (e.g., spin) interacting with an apparatus (like a Stern-Gerlach device) under the assumption of exact total angular momentum conservation.

**Key observables.** Expected values of angular momenta ($E(L_{i,z,SA})$), the final system density matrix ($ho_{f,S}$), and the correlation parameters ($eta_x, eta_z$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The correlation parameters $eta_x$ and $eta_z$ characterizing the non-orthogonality of apparatus states.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is the exact conservation of total angular momentum ($L_T$) during measurement, moving beyond the standard von Neumann model assumptions.

**Paper structure.** The paper first reviews the WAY theorem and then develops a general angular momentum conserving model. It proceeds by calculating the evolution using unitary operators and then deriving the system-only channel model via Kraus operators to analyze the resulting state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Wigner-Araki-Yanase (WAY) theorem is a well-known theorem regarding limitations of quantum measurement in the presence of additive conservation laws. Under the assumptions of the von Neumann measurement model, for which the system conserved quantity $L_{S}$ is bounded, given a conserved total additive system plus apparatus quantity $L_{SA}$, the measurement operator $E_{S}$ must commute with $L_{S}$. Prior proofs have exploited the properties of unitary evolution constrained by momentum conserving operations that tend to obscure the physical nature of the WAY theorem and as well lead to bounds on performance. As it is generally agreed that momentum is always exactly conserved in measurement, we instead develop a general angular momentum conserving model of measurement. This model is shown to lead to a simple explanation of the major implications of the WAY theorem and provides exact results of the effects of measurement based on the apparatus model. This is shown by both tracing the apparatus from the density matrix and also via a system-only channel model based on Kraus operators.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03964"></a>
### [Informational completeness of qubit measurements and IC preservability of qubit channels: Characterization and Quantification](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03964v1)

**Authors:** Jatin Ghai, Arindam Mitra  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03964v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03964_figures/2606.03964_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03964_figures/2606.03964_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03964_figures/2606.03964_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03964_figures/2606.03964_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.03964_figures/2606.03964_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops quantitative measures for informational completeness of quantum measurements and the ability of quantum channels to preserve this property. Using qubit systems, the authors establish that SIC measurements saturate the upper bound for IC. Crucially, they derive bounds linking this preservation measure to the channel's absolute output coherence, providing a deep conceptual link between information theory and quantum coherence.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides rigorous, quantitative tools to measure how much information a quantum channel preserves about the initial state, linking fundamental concepts like measurement distinguishability (IC) to coherence measures.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to quantify the informational completeness (IC) of quantum measurements and characterize the ability of quantum channels to preserve this property (IC preservability).

**Main result.** The authors establish a quantitative framework showing that the IC of qubit SIC measurements is $1/\sqrt{6}$, and they derive bounds relating IC preservability ($	ilde{D}(\Lambda)$) to the channel's absolute output coherence ($C(\Lambda)$).

**Method.** The work involves defining and characterizing faithful measures for informational completeness, analyzing the action of quantum channels in the Heisenberg picture, and using singular value decompositions of the channel map.

**Model / system.** The primary focus is on qubit systems ($d=2$), analyzing general quantum measurements (POVMs) and arbitrary quantum channels ($\Lambda$) acting on these qubits.

**Key observables.** Informational Completeness ($D(A)$), Informational Completeness-Preservability ($	ilde{D}(\Lambda)$), and Absolute Output Coherence ($C(\Lambda)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension $d=2$ (qubits); the singular values ($\lambda_i$) of the channel $\Lambda$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is heavily focused on the qubit case; the derivation of bounds for $	ilde{D}(\Lambda)$ relies on assuming the measurement $A'$ is a qubit SIC measurement.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first quantifying IC for measurements (establishing the $1/\sqrt{6}$ bound via SIC measurements), then defining and analyzing IC preservability ($	ilde{D}(\Lambda)$) for channels, and finally establishing the relationship between $	ilde{D}(\Lambda)$ and the absolute output coherence $C(\Lambda)$.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Informationally complete (IC) measurements are a useful class of measurements, as their outcome statistics uniquely determine an unknown quantum state. Hence, they are important for certain tasks such as quantum state tomography, quantum process tomography, etc. In this work, we study the quantification of informational completeness for arbitrary quantum measurements by introducing and characterizing a faithful measure for it. We explicitly evaluate the informational completeness of qubit symmetric informationally complete (SIC) measurements and show that it is an upper bound for all qubit minimal informationally complete measurements. Furthermore, by introducing a faithful measure, we try to quantify and characterize the ability of an arbitrary quantum channel to preserve informational completeness of any IC measurement when the channel acts on it in the Heisenberg picture. We call this measure informational completeness-preservability (IC preservability) of quantum channels. After studying its properties, we finally establish its relation to another quantity, namely, the absolute output coherence of a quantum channel, which quantifies the minimum amount of coherence (w.r.t. an arbitrary incoherent basis) that can always be obtained from the output of that channel. Thus, in this work, not only do we try to provide a quantitative framework for studying both the informational completeness of quantum measurements and the ability of quantum channels to preserve it, but we also try to offer key insight into the conceptual relation between informational completeness and quantum coherence.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<sub>FIG. 5: Butterfly velocity for a SO−1 circuit at q = 2. The pa- rameter space of the circuit is one-dimensional and can be param- eterized by P3, which in turn determines other parameters P1 and P2. The data points represent the results of a direct numerical sim- ulation conducted on a system of size L = 320 for 200 time steps, with averaging over N = 5000 independent realizations. The curves indicate analytical predictions derived from a truncation scheme at successive orders n = 0, 2, and 4. Due to the rapid convergence of the truncation scheme, the analytical results for orders n = 2 and 4 are indistinguishable.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.03956_figures/2606.03956_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Same as Fig. 3, but for the diffusion constant D for a Brow- nian special orthogonal circuit. The small fluctuations of the data points around the theoretical curve are attributed to statistical noise from the large, but finite, number of realizations N in the numerical simulations. Due to the rapid convergence of the truncation scheme, the analytical results for orders n = 2 and 4 are indistinguishable in the upper panel.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work analyzes how symmetry constraints (orthogonal or symplectic) modify operator spreading in random quantum circuits compared to the standard unitary case. It shows that these symmetries force the system's state representation into a ternary structure and predict specific, symmetry-dependent values for key dynamical parameters like the butterfly velocity. This is vital for understanding quantum thermalization in physical systems with underlying symmetries.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a detailed, mathematically rigorous framework for understanding how fundamental symmetries (like time-reversal or particle-hole symmetry) constrain the scrambling dynamics in quantum many-body systems, which is crucial for understanding thermalization and quantum chaos.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates operator spreading in random quantum circuits when the gates are constrained by orthogonal or symplectic symmetries, contrasting these results with the standard unitary-invariant case.

**Main result.** For orthogonal/symplectic circuits, the Pauli-string weights relax to a ternary-valued structure, and the domain wall separating trivial and scrambled regions has a finite width, unlike the sharp wall in the unitary case.

**Method.** The analysis uses the Heisenberg picture evolution, deriving closed Markovian equations for projected ternary-string distributions and modeling the dynamics via drift-diffusion equations.

**Model / system.** The system is a random quantum circuit acting on qudits ($q$-dimensional degrees of freedom) arranged in a 1D lattice, with gates drawn from orthogonal or symplectic invariant ensembles.

**Key observables.** Operator spreading, butterfly velocity ($v_B$), diffusion constant ($D$), and the structure of the steady-state Pauli-string weights ($ar{ho}_{ar{p}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Qudit dimension ($q$), symmetry type (orthogonal/symplectic), and the degree of randomness (Haar vs. Brownian).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on projecting the full state space onto a simplified binary or ternary string distribution to derive tractable Markovian evolution equations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the front position and width of operator spreading for random unitary circuits, comparing analytical approximations with numerical simulations.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the general problem and the difference between symmetry classes, deriving the Markovian evolution equations for the projected ternary distribution, and finally deriving the drift-diffusion equation governing the long-time dynamics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate operator spreading in random quantum circuits with gates drawn from orthogonal-invariant or symplectic-invariant ensembles, revealing several key distinctions from the well-studied unitary-invariant case. We find that the ensemble-averaged Pauli-string weights relax to a ternary-valued structure, instead of the binary structure of unitary-invariant circuits. For orthogonal- or symplectic-invariant circuits, the domain wall separating trivial and scrambled regions has a finite width even for Haar-random gates, whereas domain walls are sharp for Haar-distributed random unitary circuits. We further find a fundamental dichotomy between random circuits with two-qubit gates from the two disconnected components of the orthogonal group: While the butterfly velocity for the special orthogonal ensemble lies between zero and the Haar value, the negative-determinant sector exhibits a non-zero lower bound for any gate distribution. Moreover, for qudit size $q=2$, the butterfly velocity can exceed that of the Haar-random ensemble.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03977"></a>
### [Pure states for subregions in gravity and their entanglement entropy](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03977v1)

**Authors:** Zixia Wei  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03977v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03977_figures/2606.03977_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Sketch of the computation of the entanglement en- tropy using (16). Shown is a spatial slice Σ with nontrivial gravitating boundary ∂Σ. The frozen subregion F is shown in blue, and the ambient gravitating region G is shown in gray. Given a subregion A ⊂F, a representative eA satis- fying the frozen condition (17) is shown in yellow. The red surface γ|F (eA) is the codimension-2 surface defined in (18).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes that spatial subregions in quantum gravity can be described by pure quantum states, not mixed ones. It introduces a novel holographic formula for calculating the entanglement entropy of these subregions. The key achievement is demonstrating that this formula is mathematically consistent with core principles of quantum information theory, such as strong subadditivity.

**Why it may be interesting.** While highly theoretical, the rigorous derivation of entanglement entropy in a gravitational context provides deep insights into how quantum information principles (like SSA) must constrain the structure of spacetime itself, which is relevant for understanding quantum correlations in complex many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses whether spatial subregions in quantum gravity can be assigned pure quantum states, moving beyond the standard mixed reduced density matrix description.

**Main result.** It proposes a holographic prescription for the entanglement entropy that satisfies fundamental consistency conditions like strong subadditivity and complementarity, and reduces to known formulas like Ryu-Takayanagi.

**Method.** The authors use a partially frozen gravitational path integral (GPI) to prepare the state and conjecture a holographic formula involving minimizing over surfaces constrained by the frozen region.

**Model / system.** The system is quantum gravity, modeled using a gravitational path integral coupled to a QFT, focusing on the entanglement structure of spatial subregions within a larger, fixed gravitational background.

**Key observables.** Entanglement Entropy ($S_{|F}A$), the pure state $|\Psi(	ext{MF} / \mathbb{Z}_2)angle_{	ext{F} \cup \partial 	ext{F}}$, and the area of minimal surfaces ($	ext{Area}(\gamma_{|F}(eA))$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The gravitational constant ($G_N$), the geometry of the frozen region ($	ext{F}$), and the specific subregions ($	ext{A}, 	ext{B}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the partially frozen GPI is dominated by a saddle geometry, and the derivation relies on specific constraints related to the frozen subregion.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the pure state nature of the subregion using the GPI, then conjectures the holographic EE formula, and finally verifies its consistency by checking fundamental properties like SSA and complementarity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

It is proposed that spatial subregions in quantum gravity can be assigned pure states, rather than mixed reduced density matrices. The state is prepared by a partially frozen gravitational path integral, in which a spacetime subregion containing the spatial subregion is fixed while the field configurations and ambient geometry are summed over. In the semiclassical regime, we further propose a holographic prescription for the entanglement entropy of bipartitions of this state, with a frozen-region analogue of the homology constraint. The prescription satisfies nontrivial self-consistency conditions, including strong subadditivity, complementarity, and entanglement wedge nesting, and reproduces several known entropy formulas in holography and gravity as special cases. The construction suggests an observer-dependent entanglement wedge labeled by the frozen subregion.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.02943"></a>
### [Testing the ${\rm ER=EPR}$ conjecture with entangled photons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02943v1)

**Authors:** Kimet Jusufi, Francisco S. N. Lobo, Douglas Singleton  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02943v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.02943_figures/2606.02943_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. An entangled e+ + e−→2γ results in a pair of entangled photons. There is an ER bridge connecting the particles. A schematic plot inspired from [29] but with a difference that our wormhole is non-traversable.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work provides a rigorous theoretical framework to test the ER=EPR conjecture using entangled photons. By calculating the gravitational self-energy within a regularized wormhole geometry, the authors show that the energy scales match predictions derived from entanglement entropy. The results suggest that quantum gravity effects for photons are extremely weak, leading to an immunity from collapse over cosmological timescales.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a concrete, quantitative link between quantum entanglement (information theory) and spacetime geometry (general relativity) using ultra-relativistic quanta, which is a major frontier in theoretical physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper tests the ER=EPR conjecture by investigating the gravitational self-energy associated with entangled photon pairs.

**Main result.** The gravitational self-energy is found to be suppressed by the photon's wavelength ($1/L$), and the entanglement entropy calculation successfully reproduces this scaling, confirming the conjecture.

**Method.** The authors use a regularized Aichelburg-Sexl shock-wave metric to model the geometry and calculate the self-energy, complementing this with an entanglement-entropy interpretation derived from CFT.

**Model / system.** The system involves entangled photon pairs generated in $e^+e^- 	o 2\gamma$, modeled using a regularized shock-wave metric that forms a zero-throat Einstein-Rosen wormhole.

**Key observables.** Gravitational Self-Energy (EGSE), Entanglement Entropy (SEE), and the collapse time ($	au_{	ext{collapse}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Longitudinal extent ($L$), transverse separation ($d$), and the string-inspired smearing length scale ($l_0$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on linearizing the metric and using a T-duality-inspired smearing scale ($l_0$) to regularize divergences.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the regularized geometry, calculates the EGSE for separated pairs, and then provides a complementary calculation using entanglement entropy to confirm the scaling and normalization of the self-energy.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We regularize the Aichelburg-Sexl shock-wave metric for massless particles by smearing the point-like source over a string-inspired length scale $l_0$, obtaining a singularity-free gravitational potential. A coordinate transformation reveals that the transverse geometry is a zero-throat Einstein-Rosen wormhole, providing an explicit geometric realization of the ER=EPR conjecture for entangled photons. Crucially, we show that the gravitational self-energy depends on the photon's longitudinal extent $L$ (its wavelength) and, for a transversely separated photon pair, is suppressed by a factor $1/L$, giving $E^{\rm GSE}\sim 4G(\hbarω)^2/(c^4 L)\ln(d^2/l_0^2)$. For the coincident back-to-back pair created in $e^{+} e^{-}\to2γ$, the wormhole carries no additional binding energy; the logarithmic interaction energy emerges only after the entangled photons separate to a distance $d$, stretching the ER bridge. We further provide an entanglement-entropy interpretation: by computing the entanglement entropy of null intervals in the shock-wave geometry and introducing an effective entanglement temperature $k_B T_{\rm ent}\sim\hbar c/(2πL)$, we recover the same scaling and normalization of the gravitational self-energy. For optical photons the corresponding collapse time exceeds $10^{30}$ years, making isolated photons immune to gravity-induced wave-function collapse. These findings establish a rigorous playground for testing ER=EPR and reveal a deep suppression of quantum-gravity effects for ultra-relativistic quanta.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This work explores how disorder can drive a time-reversal-broken quantum spin Hall state into a topological Anderson Chern insulator (TACI) in MnBi4Te7. Using advanced theoretical methods like NEGF and SCBA, the authors construct a phase diagram revealing multiple disorder-induced phases. The key finding is the stabilization of the TACI and the prediction of a unique zero Hall plateau insulating state at strong disorder.

**Why it may be interesting.** The mechanism by which disorder stabilizes a topological phase (TACI) by controlling band inversions is a rich topic relevant to understanding robustness in engineered quantum materials, which shares conceptual links with disorder effects in quantum computation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the complex interplay between disorder and topology, specifically demonstrating that disorder can actively induce topological phases rather than merely destroying them.

**Main result.** The research identifies a novel topological Anderson Chern insulator (TACI) phase, which coexists with a zero Hall plateau insulating state at strong disorder, providing a distinct signature from conventional Chern insulators.

**Method.** The authors employ Density Functional Theory (DFT), Nonequilibrium Green's Function (NEGF) calculations, and the Self-Consistent Born Approximation (SCBA) to map out a comprehensive phase diagram.

**Model / system.** The system under study is the ferromagnetic (FM) monolayer MnBi4Te7, which is modeled using an effective Hamiltonian subjected to Anderson disorder.

**Key observables.** Longitudinal and Hall resistances ($R_{xx}, R_{xy}$), density of states (via SCBA), and the local Chern marker ($\langle 	ext{C}_{	ext{bulk}} angle_{	ext{dis}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Disorder strength ($W$), Fermi energy ($E_f$), and critical disorder strengths ($W_{c1}, W_{c2}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on finite-size scaling analysis and assumes the initial state is a T-broken QSHE state.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase diagrams mapping $R_{xx}$ and $R_{xy}$ against disorder strength and Fermi energy, and show the evolution of edge transport signatures and local density of states under varying disorder.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing the theoretical framework (DFT/NEGF/SCBA), mapping the phase diagram across different disorder regimes (T-broken TAI $	o$ TACI $	o$ NI), and finally proposing and analyzing the unique zero Hall plateau state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The interplay between disorder and topology has become a central theme in condensed matter physics. Disorder can not only destroy topological phases but also induce them, as exemplified by the topological Anderson insulator (TAI). Here we show that, in close analogy, disorder can drive the clean-limit, time-reversal-broken(T-broken) quantum spin Hall state of ferromagnetic(FM) monolayer MnBi4Te7 into a quantum anomalous Hall phase, which was called topological Anderson Chern insulator (TACI). Using density functional theory (DFT) and nonequilibrium Green's func tion (NEGF) calculations in the presence of disorder, we identify disorder induced phases-including T-broken TAI, TACI, Normal insulator, etc., then construct a comprehensive phase diagram. To discriminate multiple phases in the strong disorder regime, we further use the density of states computed within the self-consistent Born approximation (SCBA), which in particular distinguishes gapped and ungapped topological phases. We find that the two effective band inversions of Hamiltonian are suppressed at distinct critical disorder strengths; the survival of a single inversion over a finite disorder window stabilizes the TACI. Remarkably, at strong disorder, we further propose a zero Hall plateau insulating state characterized by an insulating bulk and edge channels subject to diffusive scattering that can coexist with the TACI. This behavior is distinct from a conventional band-gap Chern insulator and provides a clear experimental signature.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes using a levitated ferromagnet to generate macroscopic spin GHZ states by exploiting strong coupling between the collective spin and the mechanical rotor. By analyzing the constrained dynamics, the authors demonstrate that the system can achieve Heisenberg-limited precision for quantum sensing. The work is significant for both fundamental tests of quantum mechanics and developing ultra-sensitive quantum sensors.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it merges concepts from cavity QED (trapping/levitation), quantum sensing (QFI scaling), and open quantum systems (Lindblad master equations) to realize a macroscopic quantum system.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The work focuses on generating and characterizing macroscopic quantum states, specifically GHZ states, using a levitated ferromagnet to study quantum-to-classical transitions and test fundamental physics.

**Main result.** The strong spin-lattice coupling allows the mechanical degree of freedom to coherently drive the spin system into a non-classical regime, achieving near-Heisenberg-limited metrological scaling.

**Method.** The authors employ a top-down approach, analyzing the system dynamics by constraining the Hilbert space based on the spin-lattice locking condition and calculating the Quantum Fisher Information.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a levitated ferromagnet whose collective spin is coupled to its mechanical rotation (lattice). The dynamics are governed by a Hamiltonian involving spin-lattice coupling, and the evolution is analyzed using master equations and Wigner quasiprobability distributions.

**Key observables.** Quantum Fisher Information (QFI), macroscopic spin superposition state signatures (GHZ state), and the spin-lattice locking correlator $\langle \hat{s} \cdot \hat{n} angle$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Decoherence rates ($\Gamma_{	ext{dec}}$), required ultrahigh vacuum pressures ($\sim 10^{-9} 	ext{ Pa}$), and the spin number $N$ (up to $10^7$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies heavily on the strong spin-lattice locking condition to define a constrained subspace, and approximations are made for decoherence modeling (e.g., high-temperature limits).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the spin-lattice coupling schematic, the double-well potential for GHZ state generation, the phase space representation of the macrospin GHZ states, and the scaling of QFI with spin number.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical platform and the strong coupling mechanism, to deriving the effective dynamics and constrained Hilbert space, quantifying the metrological advantage via QFI, and finally analyzing decoherence from environmental noise sources.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The generation of macroscopic quantum states can drive both fundamental physics and quantum technologies. This work proposes a top-down approach to the generation of macroscopic spin GHZ states using a levitated ferromagnet, where a strong locking between the collective spin and the lattice rotation enables mechanical control of the collective spin. We quantify the metrological advantage of the resulting macrospin superposition state by showing that Heisenberg scaling of the quantum Fisher information is achievable. Roles of symmetry and geometry are analyzed in terms of decoherence due to gas collisions, identifying accessible conditions for experimental realization. The usefulness of a macrospin superposition state of a levitated cylindrical ferromagnet in testing spin-dependent wavefunction collapse models is also discussed.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03848"></a>
### [Generating quantum ensembles via reverse-time quantum diffusions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03848v1)

**Authors:** Maël Bompais, Mădălin Guţă, Juan P. Garrahan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03848v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `measurement-induced transitions` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03848_figures/2606.03848_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: (a) Forward (noising) dynamics for the 2-level example for samples from an initial measure over pure states µ0(ψ) corresponding to a great circle in the Bloch sphere. Under the noising dynamics the samples evolve towards a uniform µinv = 1/4π. (b) Backward denoising dynamics starting from uniformly distributed samples, for various levels ℓmax of approximation of the score, see [13] for details. At long times the initial µ0(ψ) is reconstructed, thus transforming noisy initial pure states to samples of the distribution of interest.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03848_figures/2606.03848_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Norm of the average reverse Hamiltonian in Eq.(20) as a function of denoising time in the two-level example, for several variational cut-offs ℓmax. Inset: Wasserstein distance of the learned distribution of ˜ψ to the target distribution, as a function of denoising time.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a rigorous theory for generating complex quantum state ensembles by implementing a reverse-time quantum diffusion process. It shows that the reverse dynamics is a controlled quantum diffusion whose Hamiltonian acts as a quantum score function. This provides a general, principled method for quantum state preparation from simple noise distributions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, general mathematical tool for quantum state preparation and tomography, linking modern machine learning generative models (diffusion models) directly to fundamental open quantum system dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper establishes a principled framework to generate complex quantum state ensembles by reversing a quantum diffusion process, analogous to classical score-based generative models.

**Main result.** It derives the exact reverse-time dynamics, showing it is a feedback-controlled quantum diffusion governed by a state-dependent Hamiltonian that acts as the quantum analogue of the score function.

**Method.** The derivation involves analyzing the reverse-time transition kernel using Bayes' formula and solving the corresponding backward Kolmogorov equation to find the required stochastic differential equation (SDE).

**Model / system.** The system involves quantum states evolving via continuous measurement, modeled by a forward Stochastic Schrödinger Equation (SSE) leading to an invariant distribution. The reverse process is a controlled quantum diffusion.

**Key observables.** The Wasserstein distance $W_1(	ilde{\mu}_t, \mu_0)$ is used to confirm convergence to the target distribution $\mu_0$. The mean Frobenius norm of the reverse Hamiltonian $\mathbb{E}[\|	ilde{H}_t(	ilde{\psi}_t)\|^2]$ is also monitored.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The time parameter $t$ (or $T-t$), the initial distribution $\mu_0$, and the invariant distribution $\mu_{	ext{inv}}$ are key. The Hamiltonian $	ilde{H}_t(\psi)$ depends explicitly on the instantaneous state $\psi$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes suitable regularity conditions for the transition kernel and relies on the Markov property. For specific examples, rotational symmetry around an axis $\hat{n}$ is assumed.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the forward noising dynamics spreading samples from $\mu_0$ towards $\mu_{	ext{inv}}$ and the reverse-time dynamics reconstructing $\mu_0$ from $\mu_{	ext{inv}}$. Figure 2 shows the convergence of the Wasserstein distance and the Hamiltonian norm for a qubit example.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the general theory by deriving the reverse-time SDE from the forward process's transition kernel. It then proves necessary algebraic conditions for the reverse Hamiltonian and applies these results to specific, solvable models (like the qubit) to explicitly calculate the score function and the final Hamiltonian.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We establish a reverse-time denoising theory for quantum diffusions of continuously measured quantum systems. Starting from the stochastic Schrödinger equation of a forward noising dynamics, we derive the exact reverse-time dynamics for quantum trajectories, whose law coincides with the time-reversal of the original process. We prove that the denoising dynamics is a physically admissible quantum diffusion, with the same measurement-induced noise but a state-dependent feedback Hamiltonian, a direct analogue of the "score function" of generative classical diffusion models. This provides a principled framework for converting samples of a simple distribution into those of a more complex ensemble of quantum states. We show how the denoising dynamics can be directly learnt from forward trajectory data, and how to exploit purification to initialise the denoising process.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.03789"></a>
### [Triple exceptional point with unitary paths of unfolding in a three-site fermionic Swanson-like model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03789v1)

**Authors:** Bijan Bagchi, Aritra Ghosh, Miloslav Znojil  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03789v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<img src="2606.03789_figures/2606.03789_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03789_figures/2606.03789_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper studies a three-site fermionic model to analyze the complex physics occurring at a Triple Exceptional Point (EP3) singularity. Using advanced techniques from quasi-Hermitian quantum mechanics, the authors map out the conditions under which the system's energy spectrum remains real and observable during the singularity's unfolding. The findings highlight the necessity of fine-tuning parameters to maintain physical stability near such degeneracies.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed, exactly solvable model to study non-Hermitian quantum dynamics, which is crucial for understanding open quantum systems, parity-time symmetry breaking, and spectral singularities in quantum optics and AMO physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the quantum-mechanical unitary evolution process leading to the loss of observability at a Triple Exceptional Point (EP3) singularity.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the EP3 singularity exists in a three-site fermionic model and that the system's stability (real spectrum) near this point requires fine-tuning of parameters, avoiding naive level crossings.

**Method.** The analysis employs quasi-Hermitian quantum mechanics (QHQM), perturbation theory, and the construction of transition matrices to analyze the spectral flow near the singularity.

**Model / system.** The system is a three-site fermionic generalization of the bosonic Swanson model, described by a five-parametric Hamiltonian. The analysis extends known two-site results to this three-site structure.

**Key observables.** Energy eigenvalues (E), the degree of degeneracy (EP3), and the reality/complexity of the energy spectrum during unfolding.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The five Hamiltonian parameters (a, A, B, d, u), and the small perturbation parameter $\lambda$ (or $ho = |\lambda|^{1/3}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the mathematical framework of QHQM and assumes that the system's physical interpretation is maintained by restricting the parameters to a specific 'channel of unitarity'.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the avoided crossing of energy levels near the EP3 singularity, showing that the expected real-level crossing is avoided, and the spectrum exhibits complex (resonant) branches for certain parameter ranges.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from analyzing the two-site model exhibiting an EP2, to generalizing the Hamiltonian to three sites, establishing the five-parametric form, and finally analyzing the unfolding dynamics near the EP3 singularity using perturbation theory.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A fermionic three-site generalization of the popular bosonic Swanson model is studied as providing an exactly solvable five-parametric example of the quantum-mechanical unitary-evolution process leading to an ultimate loss of the observability and fall in an exceptional-point singularity (EP3). The instant of degeneracy is found to have an explicit one-parametric form. Its unitarity-compatible vicinity (i.e., the corridor of access to EP3) is also specified in closed form. The exact, numerical-error-independent solvability is found essential due to another, avoided, false energy-level crossing which is found to occur not too far from the true EP3 singularity.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03205"></a>
### [Geometric Bounds on the Finite-Time Performance of Active Machines](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03205v1)

**Authors:** Geng Li, Z. C. Tu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03205v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03205_figures/2606.03205_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Optimal driving cycles in parameter space. Throughout the simulations, we set γ = 1, T = 1, and v = 1. The circular markers denote the initial points of the cycles, while the arrows indicate the driving directions. The protocol duration is fixed at τ = 20. As τp increases, the optimal cycles progressively shrink toward weakly dissipative regions, reflecting the increasing energetic cost associated with irreversible driving. Panels (b) and (d) display the corresponding optimal power and maximal efficiency. Increasing persistence enhances the...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work introduces a geometric thermodynamic framework to analyze how active matter machines convert energy over finite time scales. It shows that the system's performance is governed by a decomposition of work into a curvature term (work extraction) and a metric term (dissipation). This establishes a fundamental, geometric link between active matter physics and established concepts like thermoelectric efficiency.

**Why it may be interesting.** The geometric framework linking work extraction to curvature, and the direct analogy drawn to thermoelectric devices, provides powerful, general tools for analyzing non-equilibrium energy conversion in complex systems, which is relevant to open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops a unified thermodynamic framework to characterize the finite-time performance of interacting active machines operating far from equilibrium.

**Main result.** It establishes that the performance bounds (efficiency and power) of active machines share the functional form of thermoelectric devices with broken time-reversal symmetry, linking active matter geometry to energy conversion limits.

**Method.** The analysis uses stochastic thermodynamics, linear response theory, and geometric decomposition, showing that work decomposes into an antisymmetric curvature term and a symmetric dissipation metric.

**Model / system.** The model involves $N$ interacting overdamped self-propelled particles (active matter) driven by external protocols ($\lambda(t)$) and subject to thermal noise. The dynamics are analyzed using Langevin equations.

**Key observables.** Cyclic work ($W$), heat ($Q$), power ($P$), efficiency ($\varepsilon$), asymmetry parameter ($r$), and the figure of merit ($\phi$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The asymmetry parameter ($r$), the protocol shape ($C$), and the time scale ($	au$) are crucial for determining performance bounds.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies heavily on the slow-driving and weak-driving limits, utilizing linear response theory approximations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate optimal driving cycles in parameter space, showing how increasing persistence shrinks optimal cycles toward weakly dissipative regions.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first defining the thermodynamic framework, decomposing work into geometric components, deriving scaling relations for work and heat using linear response, and finally applying these results to determine maximal efficiency and power output.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Optimizing energy conversion in active matter remains a central challenge in nonequilibrium physics. Here, we develop a unified thermodynamic framework that characterizes the finite-time performance of interacting active machines. We show that cyclic work admits a geometric decomposition into an antisymmetric thermodynamic curvature, governing work extraction, and a symmetric metric, controlling dissipation. Minimal-dissipation protocols follow geodesics in parameter space, while optimal work extraction deviates from them due to a curvature-induced, Lorentz-like effect. This geometric structure directly determines the finite-time scaling of work and dissipation, enabling a mapping onto Onsager-type quasi-linear current--force relations. We show that both the maximal efficiency and the efficiency at maximum power are governed by an asymmetry parameter and a figure of merit, establishing a formal correspondence between active machines and thermoelectric devices with broken time-reversal symmetry. Our results reveal a fundamental geometric origin of energy-conversion performance and provide a general framework for optimizing active machines.

</details>

**Summary.** This review surveys the state-of-the-art in using quantum hardware to simulate condensed matter physics, covering platforms from superconducting qubits to ultracold atoms. It details the methodologies for simulating complex phenomena like strong correlations and topological phases using both analog and digital quantum simulation techniques. The paper emphasizes that current noisy experiments are vital for prototyping the error control necessary for future fault-tolerant quantum simulation.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a comprehensive roadmap connecting the theoretical necessity of simulating strongly correlated matter to the practical, hardware-specific implementations and necessary error-correction protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is simulating the complex, many-body collective behavior of quantum materials, such as those exhibiting strong electronic correlations, topological order, or non-equilibrium dynamics.

**Main result.** The review surveys the progress across multiple quantum hardware platforms, demonstrating that current noisy simulations serve as crucial prototypes for developing the error control and encoding strategies needed for future fault-tolerant quantum simulation.

**Method.** The paper reviews both analog simulation (engineering continuous dynamics) and digital simulation (implementing evolution via programmable quantum gates) across various hardware architectures.

**Model / system.** The scope covers diverse physical models including the Hubbard model, topological phases, lattice gauge theories, and non-equilibrium systems. Hardware platforms discussed include superconducting qubits, trapped ions, ultracold atoms in optical lattices, and Rydberg arrays.

**Key observables.** Entanglement entropy, topological invariants, ground-state energies, and real-time dynamics are key diagnostics used to characterize quantum phases.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coherence times, gate fidelity, interaction strengths (e.g., $E_J/E_C$), and lattice geometry (1D, 2D, 3D) are critical parameters defining platform capabilities.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that quantum simulation is a viable path forward, viewing current NISQ devices as necessary stepping stones toward fault-tolerant computation.

**Figures summary.** The figures illustrate the diverse quantum hardware platforms available for simulation, including superconducting lattices, trapped-ion processors, Rydberg arrays, and optical lattices.

**Paper structure.** The review is structured by first surveying major hardware platforms, then introducing the basic building blocks of digital quantum simulation, and finally discussing representative applications across ground-state, topological, and non-equilibrium physics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum hardware platforms are getting increasingly sophisticated in their ability to simulate condensed matter, including but not limited to strongly-correlated, topological, and non-equilibrium phenomena. This review surveys recent progress in quantum-hardware-based simulations of condensed matter, primarily emphasizing gate-based digital quantum computer simulation, with analog experiments discussed as complementary benchmarks. We first review major hardware platforms, including superconducting qubits, trapped-ions, ultracold atoms, Rydberg arrays, photonic systems, and moire quantum materials. We then introduce the basic ingredients of digital quantum simulation. Building on this foundation, we discuss representative applications to condensed-matter physics, spanning ground-state problems, strongly correlated matter, topological phases, non-equilibrium dynamics, open-system physics, and high-energy-physics-inspired simulations. Finally, we summarize key methodological tools used in state-of-the-art quantum-simulation workflows. We emphasize that present noisy quantum simulations serve not only as near-term demonstrations, but also as prototypes for the encodings, diagnostic protocols and error-control strategies required for future fault-tolerant quantum simulation.

</details>

### [Deterministic generation of cat states with more than $100$ photons under dissipation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03293v1)

**Authors:** Zhu-yao Jin, Jun Jing  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03293v1>

**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper presents a theoretical protocol for deterministically generating large-amplitude Schrödinger cat states in hybrid qubit-bosonic systems, even when dissipation is present. By leveraging the dynamical invariant within the framework of Universal Quantum Control, the authors achieve high-fidelity generation of states with over 100 photons. This advances the control theory for hybrid quantum systems, making large-scale quantum state preparation more feasible.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful theoretical extension of UQC theory to hybrid discrete-continuous variable systems, offering a robust method to create macroscopic quantum states essential for quantum metrology and computation despite environmental decoherence.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of deterministically generating large-size Schrödinger cat states (with over 100 photons) while operating under the influence of dissipation.

**Main result.** A protocol was developed that deterministically generates these large cat states with high fidelity, achieving a mean photon number exceeding 120 in both Hermitian and non-Hermitian regimes.

**Method.** The methodology utilizes Universal Quantum Control (UQC) theory by analyzing the dynamical invariant of the hybrid qubit-bosonic system under time-dependent, potentially non-Hermitian Hamiltonians.

**Model / system.** The system modeled is a hybrid qubit-bosonic system, whose dynamics are governed by a time-dependent Hamiltonian, analyzed through the dynamical invariant $\mu(t)$ in the ancillary picture.

**Key observables.** Fidelity ($F$), mean photon number ($ar{n}$), and the evolution of the dynamical invariant $\mu(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mean photon number $ar{n} > 100$; fidelity $>0.962$ (non-Hermitian); cat state amplitude $|\alpha|$; time evolution parameters $eta(t)$ and $	heta(t)$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on transforming the system dynamics into a rotating frame and constraining the Hamiltonian such that the dynamical invariant can be controlled.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the Wigner functions showing the interference pattern of the generated cat states, and plots track the fidelity, mean photon number, and trace of the density matrix across different stages of the generation protocol.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from the general theory of UQC for hybrid systems, details the Hamiltonian and transformation into the rotating frame, derives the evolution of the dynamical invariant, and finally applies this framework to generate specific types of cat states (intrinsic and four-legged) in both closed and open systems.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Large-size cat states are especially meaningful and fundamental for exploring the quantum-to-classical transition, as well as promising resources for quantum metrology and fault-tolerant quantum computation. However, amplifying the magnitude of cat states remains challenging because of the growing fragility under decoherence. We propose to generate large cat states by using the dynamical invariant of hybrid qubit-bosonic systems under Hermitian or non-Hermitian time-dependent Hamiltonian. It is a study with the universal quantum control (UQC) theory, in which the system dynamics is analyzed in the ancillary picture via a unitary transformation conditional on the qubit state. The controllable dynamics that can be encoded in the evolution of the dynamical invariant is presented by the Heisenberg equation, which imposes constrains on the Hamiltonian. When the qubit is prepared in a balanced superposed state, the bosonic mode can evolve deterministically from the vacuum state to the cat state of a mean photon number over $120$. In the Hermitian case, the generation is perfect; and in the non-Hermitian case, the fidelity is over $0.962$. Our protocol can also be applied to the generation of the intrinsic cat states and the four-component cat states of large size. Through the preparation of macroscopic quantum states, our work essentially advances UQC to hybrid discrete-continuous variable systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03722"></a>
### [Characterizing quantum channels from local-unitary invariants](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03722v1)

**Authors:** Salwa Shaglel, Satoya Imai  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03722v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

**Summary.** This work introduces averaged local-unitary invariants, or 'moments,' to systematically characterize how two-qubit quantum channels affect entanglement. By calculating these moments for $t=2$ and $t=4$, the authors establish computable criteria to distinguish between channels that create, preserve, or destroy bipartite entanglement, extending methods beyond purely unitary dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The development of moment-based diagnostics provides powerful, computable tools to quantify non-local correlations and entanglement changes in open quantum systems, which is crucial for understanding decoherence and quantum resource theories.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops systematic frameworks to characterize the entanglement properties (creation, preservation, or destruction) of two-qubit quantum channels, extending beyond the limitations of unitary settings.

**Main result.** The authors introduce averaged local-unitary invariants, or 'moments,' which provide computable criteria (e.g., $C(t)(\Lambda) \le 1$) to detect entanglement-creating and entanglement-preserving channels.

**Method.** The core method involves calculating moments, $C(t)(\Lambda)$, by taking Haar integrals of local-unitary invariants over input states or unitaries, allowing for analysis of general, non-unitary channels.

**Model / system.** The system under study is a two-qubit quantum channel ($\Lambda$) acting on a two-particle system AB. The analysis is generalized to include non-unitary channels, comparing them to the known behavior of unitary channels.

**Key observables.** Averaged local-unitary invariants, or 'moments,' denoted $C(t)(\Lambda)$ and $P(t)(\Gamma)$, for $t=2$ and $t=4$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The order of the moment ($t=2, 4$) and the specific structure of the channel (e.g., mixtures of unitaries and depolarization).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the assumption that these moments provide sufficient information to characterize entanglement properties, though limitations are noted regarding full distinguishability.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the moment space spanned by $(C(2), C(4))$ and $(P(2), P(4))$, mapping out the boundaries between entanglement-creating, entanglement-preserving, and non-entangling regions for various channel types.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops criteria by first establishing moment definitions for general channels, then applying these moments to characterize entanglement creation in two-qubit systems, and finally extending the analysis to induced single-qubit channels ($\Gamma$) to distinguish unitaries.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop systematic frameworks for characterizing the entanglement properties of two-qubit channels beyond unitary settings. We introduce averaged local-unitary invariants, referred to as moments, obtained from Haar integrals over input states or unitaries. These moments provide computable descriptions of how a quantum channel can create, preserve, or destroy bipartite entanglement. We first show that second-order moments yield criteria for non-entangling and entanglement-breaking channels, which allow us to detect entanglement-creating and entanglement-preserving channels. We then demonstrate that higher-order moments can capture additional information and distinguish channels beyond second-order moments alone. Finally, we show that combinations of moments associated with different channel families improve the discrimination of locally inequivalent two-qubit unitaries.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.02824"></a>
### [Apparent Fermionic Spectra for Bosonic Radiation: Accelerated Charge Kinematics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02824v1)

**Authors:** Michael R. R. Good, Evgenii Ievlev, Arsen Almaskhan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02824v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.02824_figures/2606.02824_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Instantaneous Larmor–Li`enard radiated power PLL(t), Eq. (10), plotted on a logarithmic vertical scale. The logarithmic scale makes visible the third local maximum, Eq.
