**Summary.** This theoretical work shows that the electromagnetic radiation from an accelerating point charge can mimic a Fermi-Dirac spectrum using only classical electrodynamics. This spectral resemblance arises purely from the specific, non-equilibrium kinematics of the charge's motion, independent of quantum statistics or thermal equilibrium assumptions. The paper is significant because it reveals a 'thermodynamic illusion' generated purely by classical dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a purely classical, kinematic mechanism to generate spectral signatures (like the FD spectrum) typically associated with quantum statistical ensembles (like Bose-Einstein or Fermi-Dirac distributions), which is highly relevant for understanding non-equilibrium quantum optics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper demonstrates that bosonic radiation emitted by an accelerated point charge can exhibit a Fermi-Dirac spectrum without invoking quantum statistics, thermal equilibrium, or the presence of event horizons.

**Main result.** The spectral shape matches the Fermi-Dirac form purely due to the specific, out-of-equilibrium kinematics of the accelerating charge, suggesting a kinematic origin for thermal-like spectra.

**Method.** The analysis uses semi-classical electrodynamics, relating the spectral distribution to the Fourier transform of the charge's trajectory, and analyzing the monodromy properties of the associated wave functions.

**Model / system.** The system is a classical point charge undergoing accelerated motion, analyzed in the non-relativistic limit. The mathematical machinery involves the Lambert W-function to define the specific trajectory $z(t)$ that yields the target spectrum.

**Key observables.** Fermi-Dirac spectrum $E_{FD}(\omega)$, radiated power $P(t)$, and the effective energy density $ho_{eff}(T)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The energy scale $\kappa$ (related to effective temperature $T$), and the non-relativistic limit assumption ($|\dot{z}| \ll 1$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the non-relativistic approximation and assumes the radiation field propagates freely to infinity; the spectral match is shown to be a kinematic illusion.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the instantaneous Larmor–Liénard radiated power $P_{LL}(t)$ exhibiting an asymmetric triple-peak structure, confirming the nonstationary, burst-like nature of the emission.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the connection between the spectrum and the trajectory's Fourier transform, deriving the required trajectory using the Lambert W-function, and then analyzing the resulting energy scaling to show recovery of standard thermodynamic laws despite the non-equilibrium process.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

An accelerated point charge can emit photons with an apparent Fermi-Dirac spectrum, even though the radiation is bosonic and its occupation numbers are not constrained to 0 or 1. The effect arises from a special class of acceleration kinematics and does not rely on thermal equilibrium, horizons, or statistical ensembles.

</details>

### [Mott transition of photons: quantum Monte Carlo study of Gross-Neveu criticality in a cavity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03733v1)

**Authors:** João C. Inácio, Natanael C. Costa, Fakher F. Assaad  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03733v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03733_figures/2606.03733_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper analyzes the coupling between a Mott-insulating electronic system (Hubbard model) and a cavity photon mode. Using advanced quantum simulation techniques, the authors demonstrate that while the coupling does not alter the fundamental quantum critical point, the photon's spectral response becomes directly coupled to the electronic optical conductivity. This establishes the cavity photon as a powerful, non-invasive diagnostic tool for observing metal-to-insulator transitions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges condensed matter theory (Mott physics, criticality) with quantum optics (cavity QED), showing how cavity modes can serve as sensitive, non-perturbative probes of intrinsic electronic quantum phases.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether coupling a Mott-transitioning electronic system (Hubbard model) to a cavity photon mode alters the quantum critical point associated with the Mott transition.

**Main result.** The coupling to the cavity photons is found to be irrelevant at the critical point, but the photon spectral function acts as a non-invasive probe for the electronic Mott transition via its coupling to the optical conductivity.

**Method.** The research employs Quantum Monte Carlo (QMC) simulations and advanced field-theoretic techniques, including path integrals and Random Phase Approximation (RPA), to analyze the coupled electron-photon dynamics.

**Model / system.** The model consists of the Hubbard model on a honeycomb lattice, representing the electronic system, coupled to a single, linearly polarized cavity photon mode. The coupling is analyzed within the context of Gross-Neveu (GN) criticality.

**Key observables.** Photon spectral function, electronic spectral function A(K, ω), optical conductivity ($\sigma_{dc}$), and the polariton spectral function.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength ($U$), coupling strength ($g$), and the lattice geometry (honeycomb lattice).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the long-wavelength limit for the cavity and that the electron-photon coupling is intensive in the thermodynamic limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the stability of the critical point against coupling strength, the electronic spectral function near the Dirac point, and the emergence/suppression of polariton modes as the system crosses the Mott insulating phase.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the coupled Hamiltonian, then using QMC and field theory to calculate spectral functions, demonstrating that the photon coupling only probes uniform charge fluctuations, and finally concluding that the photon spectrum tracks the Mott transition via polariton formation/disappearance.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Hubbard model on the honeycomb lattice is a pristine realisation of a semimetal-to-insulator Mott transition belonging to the Gross-Neveu O(3) universality class. We couple this system to a single linearly polarised cavity photon mode. The light-matter coupling is such that the photon number remains an intensive quantity as is the case for an empty cavity. For this interacting light-matter model, we formulate a negative-sign-free fermion quantum Monte Carlo algorithm that allows for bias-free results on finite system sizes. Our numerical results show that the coupling to the cavity is irrelevant at criticality, even at strong electron-photon coupling. On the other hand, we observe, and show analytically, that the photon spectral function couples to the optical conductivity of the electronic system. The cavity photons thereby undergo a Mott transition, and the photon spectral function acts as a contact-free non-invasive probe for Mott criticality.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper details a method to control the motion of a heavy ion (piston) coupled to a light ion (quantum medium) in a two-ion quantum device. By using an inverse-engineering approach, the required time-dependent trapping potential for the light ion is calculated to achieve precise piston trajectories. The results confirm that this control scheme works robustly across classical, hybrid, and full quantum theoretical treatments.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a clean, controllable, and experimentally relevant model for studying quantum work extraction and controlled coupling dynamics using trapped ions, which is a cornerstone of modern AMO physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper proposes and analyzes a scheme for achieving controlled 'piston dynamics' in a two-ion quantum device.

**Main result.** An inverse-engineered control protocol for modulating the trapping potential of one ion successfully controls the motion of the other ion, showing high fidelity across multiple theoretical approximations.

**Method.** The study employs a hybrid quantum-classical approach, treating the heavy ion classically while solving the Schrödinger equation for the light ion, and uses inverse engineering to design the required time-dependent control fields.

**Model / system.** The system consists of two interacting ions confined in orthogonal harmonic traps, where the interaction is governed by the Coulomb potential. The lighter ion acts as a quantum working medium, and the heavier ion acts as a classical piston.

**Key observables.** Final-state fidelity (F), final excitation energy (Q), and the time-dependent mean position of the piston ($\langle \hat{X} angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The time-dependent trapping frequency ($\omega_L(t)$) of the light ion, and the ratio of ion masses ($M/m$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The heavy ion is treated classically due to its large mass, and the analysis relies on hybrid quantum-classical approximations which are shown to agree well with the full quantum treatment.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the schematic setup, the comparison of stationary state regimes (single vs. double minimum), and crucially, compare the final excitation energy (Q) and fidelity (F) achieved by the inverse-engineered control versus an adiabatic reference protocol.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the Hamiltonian and the hybrid quantum-classical framework. It then analyzes the stationary states by identifying distinct quantum regimes based on trap frequencies. Finally, it focuses on time-dependent control, using inverse engineering to design the control pulse $\omega_L(t)$ and comparing its performance against other protocols.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a scheme for piston control in a two-ion quantum device with motion confined to orthogonal axes. In this system, one ion plays the role of a ''classical'' piston driven by the Coulomb interaction with the other ion, whose quantum motion is controlled through modulation of its trapping potential. The stationary state is determined self-consistently, taking quantum effects into account. We identify a narrow quantum regime of the ground state connecting two broad classical regimes. We further design inverse-engineering protocols to control the motion of the ''classical'' ion. The proposed control scheme provides a useful route toward controlled piston dynamics in microscopic quantum devices.

</details>

### [Quantum Erasure Imaging: Complementary Modalities from Delayed-Choice Erasure](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03914v1)

**Authors:** Sean D Huver, Sanjaya Lohani  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03914v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `interference shaping light` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03914_figures/2606.03914_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. QEI schematic. The imaging photon A traverses a modified Mach–Zehnder: PBS1 encodes which–path on polariza- tion; the object in Arm 1 imparts T(x, y), ϕ(x, y); a HWP equalizes polarization; and BS2 recombines to two ports (D1,D2). The ancilla photon B is measured in a random/delayed analyzer (H/V, D/A, or a rotated orthonormal basis {|e±(θ)⟩}). All events are time–tagged and retrospectively sorted by B’s outcome to reconstruct co–registered absorption and phase images from a single run. Two–port normalization makes the denominator analyzer–independent (completeness/no–signaling).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03914_figures/2606.03914_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Illustration of the reconstruction of T(x, y) using H/V polarization bases. (a) Image representing True T(x, y). (b) A vortex phase map associated with the sample. Measur- ing B: (c) Reconstructed T(x, y). (d) Estimated uncertainty in the reconstructed T(x, y).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03914_figures/2606.03914_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Measuring B on H/V bases. (a) T(x, y) Distribu- tion: brown and blue histograms, respectively, represent the true T(x, y) and reconstructed values of T(x, y). (b) Correla- tion coefficient between the true T and reconstructed T at a simulated 1000 photons per pixel.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03914_figures/2606.03914_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Measuring B on D/A bases: (a) Two-port normalized differential corresponding to the given image T(x, y). (b) Es- timated uncertainty in the visibility derived from coincidence counting statistics. Measuring B on rotational |e±(θ)⟩bases: (c) Visibility evaluated at θ = π/8. (d) Reconstruction of phase ϕ (modulo π) based on the visibility shown in panel (c).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces Quantum Erasure Imaging (QEI), a novel protocol that uses polarization-entangled photons to simultaneously measure two complementary properties of an object: absorption and phase. By analyzing coincidence counts from a single run, the authors derive robust estimators and prove that the information gained is theoretically equivalent to time-division measurements, offering significant operational advantages for quantum imaging.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems, providing a concrete, advanced protocol for quantum state tomography and imaging that leverages entanglement and delayed choice principles.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop Quantum Erasure Imaging (QEI) as a practical protocol to reconstruct two complementary classical modalities—absorption $T(x, y)$ and phase $\phi(x, y)$—from a single run of time-tagged coincidence measurements.

**Main result.** QEI enables single-run acquisition of co-registered complementary observables, and its information-theoretic performance (Fisher Information) is shown to be equivalent to time-division measurements under labeled randomization.

**Method.** The authors derive balanced two-port estimators and calculate the Fisher Information Matrix (FIM) and Cramér-Rao Bounds (CRBs) based on coincidence counting statistics from polarization-entangled photon pairs.

**Model / system.** The system uses polarization-entangled photon pairs generated via Type-II SPDC. The imaging process involves the imaging photon passing through an object imparting $T(x, y)$ and $\phi(x, y)$, while the ancilla photon measurement dictates the reconstruction modality.

**Key observables.** Absorption $T(x, y)$, phase $\phi(x, y)$, interference visibility $V(x, y)$, and the generalized visibility $V_	heta(x, y)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analyzer angle $	heta$ which continuously trades between path information and interference contrast.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The FIM equivalence proof assumes equal detection efficiencies across ancilla outcomes and negligible system drift.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the QEI setup; other figures show the estimation of the phase $\phi$ using specific analyzer angles and quantify the performance using the FIM and CRBs.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces QEI, details the reconstruction formulas for $T$ and $\phi$ based on different ancilla measurements (H/V vs D/A), derives the robust estimators, calculates the FIM, and finally establishes the operational advantages by comparing it to time-division methods.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum Erasure Imaging (QEI) turns delayed-choice erasure into a practical imaging protocol. Entangled photon pairs encode two classical modalities, absorption $T(x,y)$ and a phase-sensitive cosine quadrature of $φ(x,y)$, reconstructed from a single run of time-tagged coincidences by retrospective sorting on a remote ancilla. Measuring the ancilla in H/V yields $T$ via which-path information; D/A yields interference visibility $\propto \frac{2\sqrt{T}}{T+1}\cosφ$; and a rotated orthonormal analyzer continuously trades between them. We derive balanced two-port estimators whose denominators are analyzer independent (completeness / no signaling), together with Fisher information (FI) and Cramér--Rao bounds (CRBs) that establish an equivalence to time division under labeled randomization. The advantages of QEI are operational: single-run acquisition, perfect co-registration, and remote / delayed mode choice. We illustrate the protocol with Monte-Carlo simulations and open source our code.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** The authors successfully demonstrated single-electron trapping in a novel spherical Penning trap geometry. They characterized the trap's microwave resonance spectrum, finding three distinct, measurable modes. This work validates the trap's suitability for next-generation precision measurements, such as determining the electron magnetic moment or searching for exotic particles like dark photons.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO physics, specifically in the areas of cavity QED and precision measurements. The characterization of the mode structure and the use of the trap for fundamental constant measurements (g-factor) are core topics in the field.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper demonstrates single-electron trapping in a spherical Penning trap and characterizes its microwave resonance structure.

**Main result.** The measured cavity spectrum revealed three dominant resonance peaks (at ~38, 85, and 130 GHz), successfully characterizing the trap's microwave mode structure.

**Method.** The resonance structure was characterized by sweeping the superconducting magnet field and fitting the resulting spectral peaks to a Lorentzian function.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a spherical Penning trap containing a single electron, designed for high-precision measurements like electron g-factor determination or searches for dark photons/axions.

**Key observables.** Three dominant microwave resonance frequencies ($f_{TM\pm 11p}$), the quality factors ($Q$), and the derived cold-cavity radius ($r_0$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The required relative precision for g-factor measurement ($<10^{-12}$), and the enhancement factor ($\kappa_2$) for dark-photon searches.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theoretical mode calculation assumes a perfectly conducting spherical boundary, and the analysis relies on fitting resonance peaks to Lorentzian profiles.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 demonstrates single-electron detection via oscillation amplitude decay, while Figure 3 maps the cavity spectrum showing the three dominant resonance peaks.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by detailing the trap geometry and its advantages, demonstrating single-electron trapping, characterizing the microwave resonances through field sweeps, and finally presenting the extracted mode frequencies and derived physical parameters.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A spherical Penning trap has well-separated, clean microwave resonances, making it attractive for precision measurements of the electron magnetic moment and for dark-photon and axion searches with trapped electrons. We demonstrate single-electron trapping in a spherical Penning trap and characterize its microwave resonance structure. The design, single-electron detection, microwave mode characterization, and advantages of this geometry are presented.

<a id="paper-2606.02761"></a>
### [Enhanced qubit performance by integrating altermagnets into superconducting qubit designs](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02761v1)

**Authors:** Johanne Bratland Tjernshaugen, Morten Amundsen, Jacob Linder  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02761v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

**Summary.** This theoretical study proposes using altermagnets to enhance superconducting qubits by modifying the Josephson junction properties. By calculating the resulting energy-phase relation, the authors show that these junctions can yield qubits with superior anharmonicity and reduced decoherence. This integration offers a pathway to building more robust and controllable quantum processors.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the materials science challenge in quantum computing by proposing a novel magnetic material class (altermagnets) to engineer superconducting qubit parameters, which is highly relevant to open quantum systems and superconducting circuit QED.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary hurdle in realizing practical quantum computation is finding qubit materials that are both tunable and resilient to environmental noise.

**Main result.** Integrating altermagnets into superconducting qubits significantly improves performance by offering superior anharmonicity and robust protection against decoherence near specific operational regimes.

**Method.** The study uses microscopic calculations, generalized Blonder-Tinkham-Klapwijk (BTK) theory, and Hamiltonian analysis to model the energy-phase relation of altermagnetic Josephson junctions.

**Model / system.** The system involves superconducting qubits, particularly the transmon design, interfaced with altermagnetic Josephson junctions. The analysis models the resulting Josephson potential $U_{AM}(\varphi)$ and the full qubit Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Qubit splitting, anharmonicity ($\alpha$), decoherence rates ($\Gamma_1, \Gamma_\varphi$), and single/coupled-qubit gate operation times ($T_\pi$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Strength of the Néel field, crystallographic orientation ($\gamma$), and the superconducting phase difference ($\varphi$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations like truncating the energy-phase relation to a Fourier series and assuming zero temperature for some free energy calculations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the Josephson potential $U_{AM}(\varphi)$ dependence on altermagnet strength, the harmonic content of the potential, and the resulting decoherence rates and $\pi$-pulse times as a function of material parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the need for new qubit materials, deriving the Josephson energy via microscopic calculations, analyzing the resulting potential features ($\phi$-states, $0-\pi$ transitions), and finally applying these findings to calculate key performance metrics like decoherence and gate times for various qubit types.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Identifying a materials platform for creating qubits that are both tunable and resilient towards environmental noise is one of the main hurdles that need to be overcome to realize quantum computation that is practically useful. One pursued avenue to this end is to use superconducting qubits with intrinsic spin-dependent interactions, such as spin-orbit coupling or magnetism. However, the recently discovered class of materials known as altermagnets remain largely unexplored in this context. We here use microscopic calculations to determine how the properties of superconducting qubits are modified when altermagnetic Josephson junctions are included. The key qubit performance parameters, including splitting, anharmonicity, decoherence, and single/coupled-qubit gate operation times, display rich behavior depending on the characteristic properties of the altermagnetic material, such as the strength of the Néel field and the crystallographic orientation of the altermagnetic relative interfaces in the system. We focus in particular on the transmon design and show that the qubit is very well protected against decoherence and simultaneously shows superior anharmonicity both near 0-$π$ transition points and when it is in a $φ$-state. We propose that by using strain, the altermagnetic qubit can be moved out of its protected regime to enable faster gate-operation times, and then moved back to its protected state. We also discuss how the altermagnetic properties influence flux qubits and fluxonium. Our results suggest that integration of altermagnetic materials into existing superconducting qubit design can substantially improve their performance due to the unique properties of the altermagnetic band-structure.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a Global Adiabatic Criterion (GAC) to explain fast topological photon transfer in Fock-state lattices. It proves that the key to speed is minimizing the variance of the nonadiabatic coupling, not just maintaining a constant energy gap. The optimal protocol uses a sinusoidal coupling profile, predicting significantly faster and more efficient transfer times than previously reported.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a quantitative, general theoretical framework for optimizing quantum light transport, moving beyond simple gap-closing arguments to focus on the uniformity of nonadiabatic dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To provide a rigorous explanation for the fast topological photon transfer observed in Fock-state lattices and establish a general framework for fast topological photonics engineering.

**Main result.** The essential condition for fast transfer is the vanishing temporal variance of the nonadiabatic coupling factor ($\sigma_Q^2 = 0$), which is achieved optimally using a sinusoidal coupling profile ($\alpha=1$).

**Method.** The authors developed a Global Adiabatic Criterion (GAC) to bound infidelity using the mean and variance of the nonadiabatic factor, analyzing power-law coupling profiles.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled using a two-mode Jaynes-Cummings (JC) model applied to Fock-state lattices (FSLs), which simulates topological photon transfer.

**Key observables.** Transferred photon number ($ar{n}_2$), infidelity ($\epsilon$), and the nonadiabaticity variance ($\sigma_Q^2$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Optimal transfer duration for a five-photon state is predicted to be 161 ns, showing a linear scaling with photon number $N$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis incorporates experimental decoherence parameters and relies on the validity of the Global Adiabatic Criterion.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the setup (JC model, FSL), compare fidelity/photon number vs. coupling exponent ($\alpha$), and show the variance $\sigma_Q^2$ vanishing only for the sinusoidal case ($\alpha=1$).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical model and the GAC, analyzes power-law coupling profiles, determines the optimal profile by minimizing nonadiabaticity variance, and finally predicts significantly faster transfer times compared to previous experimental benchmarks.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Topological state transfer in Fock-state lattices has been demonstrated with high speed using sinusoidal profiles of coupling, yet the underlying reason has remained unclear. A global adiabatic criterion (GAC) is developed to bound the infidelity by the mean and variance of the nonadiabatic factor. The GAC reveals that the key to fast transfer is not a constant energy gap but the vanishing nonadiabaticity variance. For power-law coupling profiles, the variance vanishes only for the sinusoidal shape, which is thus globally optimal. Incorporating experimental decoherence parameters, it is predicted that the optimal transfer duration for a five-photon state is 161 ns, far shorter than 600 ns used in the experiment, reducing time by over 73% while increasing transferred photons by 29%. The optimal duration follow a simple linear scaling with photon number, providing a practical guideline. Through constructing an alternative constant-gap coupling family, it is confirmed that a constant gap alone is not sufficient for fast topological photon transfer. The essential condition is uniformity of nonadiabaticity. This work offers a rigorous explanation for the observed speed and a general framework for fast topological photonics engineering.

</details>

### [Scaling Laws for Neural-Network Quantum States](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02794v1)

**Authors:** Riccardo Rende, Alessandro Sinibaldi, Luciano Loris Viteritti, Roeland Wiersema, Antoine Georges, Giuseppe Carleo

**Summary.** This work applies scaling laws from deep learning to variational quantum simulations of spin models. By analyzing how the approximation error decays with training compute, the authors establish a quantitative measure ($\alpha$) that characterizes the intrinsic difficulty of the target quantum ground state. This suggests scaling laws can serve as a general benchmark for variational quantum algorithms.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel, quantitative framework—the scaling exponent $\alpha$—to benchmark the difficulty of approximating quantum ground states, linking machine learning theory directly to condensed matter physics complexity.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether the scaling laws observed in modern neural networks can be used to characterize the computational complexity of solving physical problems using Neural-Network Quantum States (NQS).

**Main result.** The accuracy measure ($V$-score) decays as a power law in training compute, and the scaling exponent systematically decreases with physical frustration, suggesting it quantifies the ground state's representational difficulty.

**Method.** The authors use a variational approach with transformer wave functions to approximate ground states, fitting the resulting error metric ($V$-score) to a power law dependent on training compute and system size.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on the $J_1$-$J_2$ Heisenberg model on 2D lattices (square and triangular) to generate target ground states spanning different levels of magnetic frustration.

**Key observables.** The $V$-score (a measure of variational accuracy), and the scaling exponents ($\alpha, eta$) derived from the power-law fit of the $V$-score vs. compute.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Frustration level ($J_2/J_1$ ratio), system size ($L 	imes L$ up to $20 	imes 20$), and training compute ($f$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the scaling behavior follows a power law, and that the training compute scales linearly with system size ($f \approx aN$).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the scaling collapse of the $V$-score for various spin Hamiltonians against rescaled compute. Figure 2 illustrates the data collapse procedure for the square $J_1-J_2$ model.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of applying ML scaling laws to physics, details the use of transformer wave functions for the $J_1-J_2$ model, presents the power-law fitting and data collapse, and concludes by interpreting the scaling exponent as a measure of physical complexity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Scaling laws, the power-law relations between loss, architecture size, and compute observed in modern neural networks, offer a quantitative way to characterize the complexity of a learning problem, with the exponent governing the decay of the loss reflecting how rapidly additional resources translate into improved accuracy, and thus how hard the target is to learn. Whether an analogous framework can characterize the complexity of physical problems remains open. We address this question for Neural-Network Quantum States, a leading variational approach for strongly correlated quantum many-body systems. Using transformer wave functions to approximate ground states of the $J_1$-$J_2$ Heisenberg model on triangular and square lattices with up to $20\times 20$ sites, we find that the $V$-score, a measure of accuracy of a variational state, decays as a power law in training compute. Under an appropriate rescaling of compute, results for different system sizes collapse onto a single curve, analogous to scaling collapse in critical phenomena. The resulting power law is, to a good approximation, independent of the number of sites, showing that the transformer Ansatz is size-consistent for the systems considered. The exponent decreases systematically with frustration, identifying it as a quantitative measure of representational difficulty of the ground state and establishing scaling laws as a general framework for benchmarking variational ansätze.

</details>

### [Vector Magnetometry with Broadband Microwave Fields in Nitrogen-Vacancy Centers in Diamond](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02749v1)

**Summary.** This paper presents a breakthrough method for full vector magnetometry using Nitrogen-Vacancy centers in diamond. It overcomes the limitations of single-polarization measurements by employing two orthogonal microwave pulses and analyzing the resulting signals with deep neural networks. The technique achieves high sensitivity, enabling accurate magnetic field measurements down to the microtesla range without needing an external bias field.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges quantum sensing (NV centers) with advanced machine learning techniques (NNs) to solve a complex inverse problem in condensed matter physics, providing a robust, high-precision measurement tool.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of performing full vector magnetometry ($	ext{B}_x, 	ext{B}_y, 	ext{B}_z$) using Nitrogen-Vacancy (NV) centers in diamond.

**Main result.** A novel method utilizing two orthogonally polarized microwave pulses and deep neural networks achieves high sensitivity ($\sim 5-100 	ext{ pT}/\sqrt{	ext{Hz}}$) and accuracy ($	ext{nT}$) for measuring the full magnetic field vector.

**Method.** The technique involves measuring the transmission of broadband microwave pulses through the NV ensemble, which is then analyzed using deep neural networks trained on simulated data.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the NV center in diamond, whose spin Hamiltonian governs the magnetic response. The measurement relies on analyzing the spectral line splitting induced by the Zeeman effect using two distinct, orthogonally polarized microwave fields.

**Key observables.** Measured microwave signal intensities ($I_{MW1}(t), I_{MW2}(t)$) and the estimated magnetic field components ($	ext{B}_x, 	ext{B}_y, 	ext{B}_z$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sensitivities ranging from $5 	ext{ pT}/\sqrt{	ext{Hz}}$ to $100 	ext{ pT}/\sqrt{	ext{Hz}}$; measurement down to $25 	ext{ }\mu	ext{T}$; $	ext{SNR}$ of $70 	ext{ dB}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method requires using two orthogonal microwave polarizations to resolve all three magnetic field components independently; the analysis relies on training deep neural networks on simulated data.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the frequency-domain response of NV ensembles, the dependence of estimation statistics ($\sigma_{i,est}$ and Bias) on the true field magnitude $|\mathbf{B}_{true}|$, and the improvement gained by using two microwave polarizations over one.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theory for vector magnetometry using two MW polarizations, details the inverse problem solving via deep neural networks, analyzes the performance metrics (sensitivity, bias) as a function of the true field strength, and discusses limitations and potential improvements.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a novel method for full vector magnetometry using nitrogen-vacancy (NV) centers. In contrast to conventional optically detected magnetic resonance techniques, our method employs two distinct broadband microwave pulses and measures them after transmission through the NV sensor medium, thus capturing the line splitting of the ground state triplet due to the Zeeman effect. Two orthogonally polarized microwave pulses allow resolving all magnetic field components independently by reading out differently oriented NV centers. Simulated data is analyzed using deep neural networks, whose efficacy we expect to translate very well to experiments. Our method yields sensitivities between $5~\mathrm{pT}/\sqrt{\mathrm{Hz}}$ and $100~\mathrm{pT}/\sqrt{\mathrm{Hz}}$ across different magnetic field vector components, while achieving approximately $\mathrm{nT}$ accuracy at a signal-to-noise (SNR) ratio of $70~\mathrm{dB}$. By being capable of accurately measuring magnetic fields down to $25~\mathrm{μT}$, the need for a bias field beyond Earth's magnetic field is eliminated.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.03380"></a>
### [Energy-selective quantum search with Ising Hamiltonian phase oracles](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03380v1)

**Authors:** A. S. Plyashechnik, A. A. Zhukov, A. V. Lebedev, W. V. Pogosov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03380v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03380_figures/2606.03380_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Amplification profile for an annealed Gaussian spectrum near the principal phase resonance ET = π. The horizontal axis is the dimensionless phase ET/π, and the three panels show the amplitude magnitude after K = 2, 8, 16 iterations. Solid curves are obtained from the exact spectral recurrence, while dashed curves show the local resonant approximation. The parameters are chosen such that ΣT ≃0.72. The agreement near ET/π = 1 illustrates that the local response captures the growth and narrowing of the Gaussian resonance.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>FIG. 2. Energy-space amplification profile for a single zero-field random Ising instance compared with the annealed Gaussian prediction. The Ising instance has n = 24, hi = 0, and independent couplings Jij ∼N(0, 1). The red curve shows the response of the discrete Ising spectrum, while the blue curve shows the annealed Gaussian model with the same energy variance. Both profiles develop resonances near ET = ±π, but the discrete Ising response is shifted and distorted by spectral correlations.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03380_figures/2606.03380_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Zoom of the positive-energy resonance for zero-field random Ising spectra at n = 24. Blue curves show the annealed Gaussian response, red points show the amplitudes on the discrete Ising energy levels, and the vertical dashed line marks the Gaussian target energy E∗= π/T. Panel (a) corresponds to a denser spectral tail, approximately µ ≃0.6, with a broader and lower peak. Panel (b) corresponds to a sparser tail, approximately µ ≃1.2, with a narrower and higher peak. In both cases the discrete Ising peak is displaced relative to the Gaussian target, illustrating the realization-dependent phase shift φ0.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03380_figures/2606.03380_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Scaling of the correlation-induced resonance displacement in the zero-field random Ising ensemble. The simulations use σJ = 1, Jij ∼N(0, 1), hi = 0, µ = 3/2, and the truncated characteristic function with m ≤15. Panel (a) shows the disorder-averaged second moment ⟨φ2 0⟩for n = 18, . . . , 36. </details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a theory for an energy-selective quantum search using the natural phase accumulation from an Ising Hamiltonian's time evolution. It shows that this method achieves Grover-like speedups, while also providing crucial theoretical tools to correct for spectral shifts caused by real-world Hamiltonian correlations. This advances the understanding of quantum computation on disordered physical systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides a deep theoretical link between quantum search algorithms and the spectral properties of disordered Hamiltonians, offering insights into how physical constraints (like spectral correlations) affect quantum computational scaling.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates an energy-selective quantum search primitive that uses the physical evolution of an Ising Hamiltonian as a continuous phase oracle, contrasting it with standard Boolean oracles.

**Main result.** The algorithm successfully achieves a Grover-type amplification peak, and the required oracle calls scale according to a square-root law in the annealed Gaussian limit.

**Method.** The analysis employs spectral theory, generating functions, and recurrence relations to model the quantum amplitude evolution in energy space.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled by the Ising Hamiltonian, $H(\mathbf{s})$, which describes disordered magnets and is used in optimization problems. The oracle is the time evolution operator $U_T = \exp(-iTH)$, which imparts a phase proportional to the state's energy $E_s$.

**Key observables.** The primary observables are the resonance peak position, width, and height, which determine the success probability of the search.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Key parameters include the evolution time $T$, the target energy $E^*$, and the correlation-induced spectral shift $\varphi_0 = O(n^{-3})$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations such as the annealed Gaussian density of states and the ability to correct for spectral correlations using spectral symmetrization or iterative calibration.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the discrete random Ising spectrum response against the annealed Gaussian prediction, illustrating the measurable shift and distortion caused by spectral correlations.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the energy-selective oracle to deriving the recurrence relations, analyzing the idealized annealed Gaussian case, and finally tackling the complexities introduced by spectral correlations through calibration techniques.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Ising Hamiltonians are basic models of disordered magnets and a standard language for quantum and classical optimization. We study an energy-selective quantum search primitive in which the physical evolution \(\exp(-\mathrm{i} T H)\) is used directly as a Hamiltonian phase oracle. Unlike a Boolean oracle, this oracle marks configurations continuously by their phases and selects a finite resonance band rather than a preassigned marked set. We show that alternating it with the Grover diffusion operator nevertheless produces a Grover-type amplification peak. An exact spectral recurrence and a generating-function representation determine the peak position, width, and height. For an annealed Gaussian density of states, target energies in a high-density tail require \(Θ(\sqrt{2^n/M})\) oracle calls when the resonance contains \(M\) configurations. For random Ising spectra, overlap-induced correlations shift and distort the peak; spectral symmetrization and iterative calibration remove this detuning for prescribed-energy targeting.

</details>

### [Multiscale Phase Separation in Chemophoretic Active Matter](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03474v1)

**Authors:** Manisha Jhajhria, Subir K. Das, Snigdha Thakur

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>FIG. 3. Two-point equal time correlation function, C(r, t), is plotted against r. In (a) and (b) we show results for the chemoattractive case with k = 10 and k = 3, respectively. The insets of these figures show the scaling plots of C(r, t), where the distance is divided by the time-dependent domain lengths ℓ(t). **Summary.** This work analyzes phase separation in active matter driven by chemical gradients, contrasting chemoattractive and chemorepulsive coupling. The key finding is that attraction leads to continuous, macroscopic domain growth, whereas repulsion causes growth to arrest at a microscale due to dynamical trapping. The study uses advanced diagnostics like growth exponents and transition matrices to distinguish these fundamentally different non-equilibrium pathways.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides a detailed, non-equilibrium characterization of phase separation driven by active, non-reciprocal forces, offering insights into pattern formation far from equilibrium that can inform models of active matter dynamics relevant to AMO systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how nonreciprocal interactions in chemophoretic active matter lead to multiscale phase separation, comparing the kinetics in chemoattractive versus chemorepulsive coupling regimes.

**Main result.** Chemoattraction drives sustained, macrophase separation via cluster coalescence, while chemorepulsion leads to arrested, microscale phase separation due to strong caging and fragmentation.

**Method.** The research employs simulations and scaling analysis using the two-point equal-time correlation function, cluster transition matrices, and Mean-Squared Displacement (MSD) to characterize growth dynamics.

**Model / system.** The system models binary mixtures of chemically active source (S) and phoretic (P) particles whose motion is governed by Langevin dynamics and asymmetric chemophoretic forces. The interaction strength is controlled by the parameter k, determining attraction or repulsion.

**Key observables.** Growth exponents ($\alpha$), fractal dimension ($d_f$), cluster transition matrices, and Mean-Squared Displacements (MSD).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Phoretic strength parameter $k$ (sign determines attraction/repulsion), particle density ($\phi$), and temperature ($k_B T$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis involves mapping continuum systems to a 2D square lattice and applying coarse-graining techniques to analyze domain morphology.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate simulation snapshots comparing aggregation in both regimes, phase coexistence curves, and the evolution of cluster size and MSD, showing distinct scaling behaviors.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically compares the phase-ordering kinetics by analyzing the structural evolution (correlation functions), the cluster size dynamics (transition matrices and growth/fragmentation bias), and the particle transport (MSD) across the chemoattractive ($k>0$) and chemorepulsive ($k<0$) regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nonreciprocal interactions in active matter provide interesting structure and dynamics. Here we investigate chemophoretic systems in which nonreciprocity arises from the asymmetric coupling between agents: first species produces certain chemicals and the other phoretically responds to it. This leads to phase separation at varying scales. Our study uncovers a re-entrant steady-state phase diagram as the nature of the coupling changes from chemoattractive to chemorepulsive character. Chemoattraction provides sustained domain growth, leading to macrophase separation via cluster coalescence. Aggregation in the chemorepulsive case, on the other hand, leads to a steady-state situation that displays phase separation only at a microscale, owing to strong caging effect and frequent fragmentation. The overall far-from-steady-state dynamics is quantified via calculations of growth exponents, cluster transition matrices, and mean-squared displacements.

</details>

### [Modification of Charge and Spin Textures by Light Chemical Substitution in Eu(Al$_{1-x}$Ga$_{x}$)$_4$ ($x=0.1$)](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03869v1)

**Authors:** Zétény Bacsó, Fellipe Carneiro, Kevin Allen, Aly H. Abdeldaim, Rebecca Scatena, Jaime M. Moya, Emilia Morosan, Alessandro Bombardi, Roger D. Johnson

**Summary.** This experimental study uses resonant X-ray diffraction to characterize the coupled charge and spin ordering in Eu(Al1-xGa_x)4. The researchers found evidence for a Charge Density Wave and a complex magnetic structure below the Néel temperature. The work highlights the strong coupling between these two orders and details the specific helical nature of the magnetic moments in different crystal domains.

**Why it may be interesting.** The strong coupling observed between charge and spin order in a topological magnet provides a rich platform for studying emergent quantum phenomena, which is relevant to understanding correlated electron physics in novel materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how light chemical substitution modifies the coupled charge and spin textures in the topological magnet Eu(Al1-xGa_x)4.

**Main result.** The system exhibits strong coupling between the Charge Density Wave (CDW) and magnetic order, with the magnetic structure characterized as a single helical arrangement in the k2 domain and a tilted helicoid in the k1 domain below the Néel temperature.

**Method.** Resonant X-ray diffraction experiments were performed at the Eu L3 edge, utilizing polarization analysis and azimuthal measurements to resolve both charge and spin modulations.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the intermetallic topological magnet Eu(Al1-xGa_x)4, where the substitution of Al by Ga ($x=0.1$) is used to tune the electronic and magnetic properties. The analysis focuses on the interplay between CDW formation and long-range magnetic ordering.

**Key observables.** CDW propagation vector (kCDW ~ (0, 0, 0.18)), magnetic satellite positions (k = (±δ_m, 0, 0) and (0, ±δ_m, 0)), and the ratio of intensities measured with linear vs. circularly polarized light.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (especially the transition temperatures $\approx 75 	ext{ K}$ for CDW and $T_N = 14.8 	ext{ K}$ for magnetism), and the substitution concentration ($x=0.1$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the observed magnetic scattering can be modeled using elliptical helix or helicoidal structures, and that the temperature invariance of the polarization ratio suggests a single magnetic phase below $T_N$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the temperature evolution of CDW intensity showing a sharp drop near 15 K, and show magnetic satellites in reciprocal space, with further analysis using azimuthal dependence to model the spin structure.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the presence of CDW and magnetic order, noting the coincidence of the CDW drop and $T_N$. It then uses azimuthal measurements to fit the magnetic satellite intensities, distinguishing between different types of helical spin arrangements in the two observed magnetic domains.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present the results of a resonant X-ray diffraction experiment, resolving both charge and spin textures in the intermetallic topological magnet Eu(Al$_{1-x}$Ga$_{x}$)$_4$, $x$ = 0.1. Below $\approx$ 75 K the system develops a charge density wave (CDW) with propagation vector kCDW ~ (0, 0, 0.18). The CDW order parameter grows monotonically on cooling until ~ 15 K, when a sudden decrease in the CDW amplitude occurs. Pairs of magnetic satellites of the (0, 0, 8) Bragg reflection corresponding to two distinct domains, k = ($\pm δ_\text{m}$, 0, 0), k2 = (0, $\pmδ_\text{m}$, 0), $δ_\text{m} = 0.2002(4)$ were studied at the Eu L3 edge, appearing below TN = 14.8 K. Our measurement of TN is exactly coincident with the sudden drop in the CDW amplitude, which suggests strong coupling between the charge and spin orders, as observed in other compounds of the Eu(Al$_{1-x}$Ga$_{x}$)$_4$ series. Azimuthal measurements revealed a single helical spin arrangement with an elliptical envelope of $μ_\text{Y}/μ_\text{Z}$ = 1.19(6) for the k2 domain, and a tilted helical (helicoidal) spin arrangement for the k1 domain, with $μ_\text{Y}/μ_\text{Z}$ = 1.14(4) and $μ_\text{X}/μ_\text{Z}$ = 0.20(2) that may be hidden for the k2 domain due to multiple subdomains. Temperature evolution of the magnetic satellite intensities in linear and circularly polarised light found the respective ratio to be invariant with temperature, suggesting a single magnetic phase below TN. This behaviour is unlike the x = 0 material, in which a spin density wave forms first, transitioning to a helical ground state on cooling through intermediate phases. Future theoretical work on the Eu electronic ground state, supported by related experiments, will help understand the effects of Ga substitution on the evolution of the magnetic structure.

</details>

### [Tailoring pure valley-Zeeman spin-orbit coupling in WSe$_2$-encapsulated monolayer graphene](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03278v1)

**Summary.** This research reports the successful experimental realization of pure valley-Zeeman spin-orbit coupling in graphene by sandwiching it between twisted WSe2 layers. By analyzing quantum oscillations and Landau level spectra, the authors demonstrate precise control over the SOC components, showing a transition between symmetry-broken and highly degenerate quantum Hall states. This work establishes a powerful platform for designing novel electronic properties in van der Waals heterostructures.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a clean, tunable platform for engineering fundamental spin-valley coupling in 2D materials, which is crucial for developing topological quantum devices and spintronics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to experimentally realize and control pure valley-Zeeman spin-orbit coupling (SOC) in monolayer graphene using a van der Waals heterostructure.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated pure valley-Zeeman SOC via WSe2/graphene/WSe2 encapsulation, evidenced by the reordering of Landau levels and the ability to tune the SOC strength and even quench it.

**Method.** The study combines magneto-transport measurements (Shubnikov-de Haas effect, $ho_{xx}$ vs $B$) with theoretical modeling (tight-binding) and Fast Fourier Transforms (FFT) analysis of quantum oscillations.

**Model / system.** The system is graphene encapsulated between two parallel twisted WSe2 monolayers. The physics is governed by the Dirac Hamiltonian perturbed by proximity-induced SOC terms, specifically the valley-Zeeman ($\lambda_{vZ}$) and Rashba ($\lambda_R$) components.

**Key observables.** Landau level reordering, symmetry-enforced transitions between twofold and fourfold degenerate quantum Hall states, frequency splitting ($\Delta f_
u$) in FFT spectra, and the effective SOC strength ($\lambda_{SOC}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angle ($\Delta	heta$), magnetic field ($B$), carrier density ($n$), and the relative strengths of the SOC components ($\lambda_{vZ}$ vs $\lambda_R$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The proximity effect is modeled by adding specific SOC terms to the graphene Hamiltonian, and the electronic structure is analyzed using tight-binding models.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the super-moiré lattice structure and calculated LL spectra for pure valley-Zeeman SOC, alongside $ho_{xx}$ vs $B$ measurements showing phase transitions between symmetry-broken and fourfold-degenerate states.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing the platform (WSe2/graphene/WSe2), theoretically modeling the SOC components, and then presenting experimental evidence from transport measurements (SdH, FFT) to confirm the selective realization and tunability of the pure valley-Zeeman SOC regime.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Engineering proximity effects in twisted van der Waals heterostructures offers a powerful platform for designing electronic properties. While theoretical predictions of quantum interference in transition metal dichalcogenide-encapsulated graphene can selectively control the spin-orbit coupling component, experimental realizations have remained elusive. Here, we report pure valley-Zeeman spin-orbit coupling in monolayer graphene, achieved by encapsulation between two parallel twisted WSe$_2$ monolayers. We observed a symmetry-enforced reordering of Landau levels, which is driven by the competition between the fixed valley-Zeeman energy and the magnetic-field-dependent cyclotron energy. This reordering is characterized by a transition from symmetry-broken states in the quantum Hall effect to a restored fourfold degeneracy with integer or half-integer quantum Hall sequences. We also demonstrate the ability to completely quench the proximity spin-orbit coupling by tuning the encapsulated geometry.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.02793"></a>
### [Skyrmion and meron phases induced by spin-phonon coupling](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02793v1)

**Authors:** E. Iroulart, F. A. Gómez Albarracín, H. Diego Rosales  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02793v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.02793_figures/2606.02793_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Illustration of the square lattice, indicating near- est neighbors exchange Jij between sites (i, j) (blue) and Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya interaction Dij. Purple dashed arrows indicate the orientation used to compute the scalar chirality (Eq. 9) from spins at sites (i, j, m).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work explores how coupling between magnetic spins and lattice vibrations (spin-phonon coupling) alters the magnetic ground state of chiral magnets. Using Monte Carlo simulations on two distinct coupling models (ESP and BP), the authors demonstrate that lattice effects can stabilize novel topological phases, such as meron-antimeron crystals, and tune the transition between different skyrmion lattice structures.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit inclusion of lattice degrees of freedom (phonons) provides a concrete, tunable mechanism for controlling topological magnetic order, which is highly relevant for understanding emergent quantum phenomena in solid-state systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how spin-phonon (SP) coupling modifies magnetic interactions and the resulting topological spin textures, such as skyrmions and merons, in chiral magnets.

**Main result.** Lattice effects provide a simple mechanism to tune topological magnetic phases, stabilizing exotic structures like M-aM crystals and distorting conventional skyrmion lattices into square arrangements.

**Method.** The research employs Monte Carlo simulations to analyze the effective Hamiltonians derived from two simplified spin-phonon coupling models: Einstein site-phonon (ESP) and bond-phonon (BP).

**Model / system.** The system is a two-dimensional skyrmion model on a square lattice, starting from a Heisenberg model Hamiltonian modified by SP coupling terms. The analysis compares the effects of site-based (ESP) versus bond-based (BP) phonon interactions.

**Key observables.** Local and total scalar chirality ($\chi$), topological charge ($Q$), and the structure factor ($S_{\perp}(\mathbf{q})$) are used to characterize the magnetic phases.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling strengths ($\alpha, eta$) and the external magnetic field ($B/J_0$) are the key tuning parameters explored in the phase diagrams.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that lattice distortions modify the magnetic interactions (Exchange and DM) linearly, and it compares results from two distinct, simplified models for SP coupling (ESP vs. BP).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase diagrams showing stabilization regions for various textures (e.g., 2q-SkX, M-aM) as a function of coupling parameters, and density plots visualizing chirality in different phases.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically compares the predictions of the ESP and BP models, detailing how the resulting effective Hamiltonians lead to different topological phases, culminating in a discussion of how lattice effects tune the magnetic ground state.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In chiral magnets, magnetic skyrmions are typically stabilized by the competition between exchange and Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya interactions under an external magnetic field, while the role of lattice degrees of freedom has received comparatively less attention. Here we study how spin-phonon (SP) coupling modifies magnetic interactions and the resulting spin textures in a two-dimensional skyrmion model in the square lattice. Using Monte Carlo simulations, we compare two simplified models describing the SP coupling: the Einstein site-phonon (ESP) and bond-phonon (BP) models. We find that ESP coupling stabilizes skyrmion crystals in field regimes that are topologically trivial in the uncoupled model and also induces additional textures, including meron-antimeron (M-aM) crystals and mixed skyrmion-bimeron (SkX-Bm) phases. Furthermore, for sufficiently strong phonon coupling, the conventional triple-$\textbf{q}$ hexagonal skyrmion lattice is distorted into a double-$\textbf{q}$ square skyrmion lattice. Overall, our results show that lattice effects provide a simple mechanism to tune topological magnetic phases.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work analyzes a complex spin model on diamond-decorated lattices exhibiting competing magnetic interactions. By exploiting local spin conservation, the authors map out a phase diagram featuring three distinct magnetic phases. The key findings highlight that the system achieves maximal ground state degeneracy and high residual entropy at a multicritical point, suggesting applications in quantum cooling technologies.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study of high residual entropy and macroscopic degeneracy at multicritical points is highly relevant to understanding quantum phase transitions and the potential for quantum thermal engines.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the ground state properties, magnetization, and low-temperature thermodynamics of a spin-$s$ model with competing ferromagnetic and antiferromagnetic interactions on diamond-decorated lattices.

**Main result.** The system exhibits rich phase diagrams (F, Ferri, MD) meeting at a multicritical point where the ground state degeneracy is maximal, leading to high residual entropy.

**Method.** The analysis combines exact diagonalization for the 1D chain with mapping the 2D/3D problem onto a bond percolation framework for calculating ground state degeneracy and magnetization.

**Model / system.** The model is a spin-$s$ Heisenberg model defined on diamond-decorated lattices, incorporating bilinear and higher-order exchange interactions. Local conservation of the composite spin on each diamond diagonal allows for the exact analysis of local energy minimization.

**Key observables.** Ground state degeneracy ($W$), residual entropy per spin ($S_0$), low-temperature magnetization ($m$), and the excitation spectrum (gapped/gapless).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The spin values ($s, \sigma$), the coupling ratios ($J, K$), and the lattice dimensionality (1D chain, 2D, 3D).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the local conservation of the composite spin on each diamond unit, and for higher dimensions, the problem is mapped onto a bond percolation framework.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the lattice structure and couplings (Fig. 1, 2). Phase diagrams show the three competing phases (F, Ferri, MD) meeting at a triple point (Fig. 3). Further figures detail magnetization curves and percolation exponents (Fig. 4, 5, 6).

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the general formalism using local spin conservation. It then analyzes the pure Heisenberg case, detailing $2\sigma$ transitions. The focus shifts to the $s=\sigma=1$ case, mapping the phase diagram and calculating degeneracy/entropy. Finally, it analyzes magnetization and specific heat across different lattice dimensions and critical lines.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the ground state properties, magnetization, and low-temperature thermodynamics of the ferromagnetic-antiferromagnetic spin-$s$ model on diamond-decorated lattices with ideal diamond units, incorporating bilinear Heisenberg and higher-order exchange interactions between diagonal spins-$σ$. Local conservation of the composite spin on each diamond diagonal enables exact analysis. For the pure Heisenberg case, the system undergoes a series of $2σ$ transitions between monomer-dimer (MD), ferrimagnetic (Ferri) and ferromagnetic (F) phases with different optimal composite spin values as the coupling ratio varies. In the presence of higher-order interactions, a multicritical point exists where the states with all possible values of composite spin are degenerate, leading to maximal ground state degeneracy. The case $s=σ=1$ with bilinear and biquadratic interactions is studied in detail. Its phase diagram comprises three phases - F, Ferri and MD, which meet at a triple point. On the phase boundaries, the ground state becomes macroscopically degenerate. For the diamond chain, we calculate the ground state degeneracy exactly; for higher dimensions, the problem maps onto a bond percolation framework, solved numerically. The residual entropy per spin reaches up to $60\%$ of the maximal value, peaking at the triple point. Low-temperature magnetization curves in external magnetic fields exhibit plateaus and jumps. The excitation spectrum is gapped in the MD phase, gapless in the F phase, and resembles that of the Lieb-Mattis ferrimagnet in the Ferri phase. The high residual entropy suggests potential applications in ultra-low-temperature cooling and quantum thermal machines.

</details>

**Summary.** This work reveals a previously unappreciated, direct spin-mediated coupling between conduction electrons and polar phonons in strontium titanate. Using magneto-optical measurements, the authors quantify this coupling, finding it is strong enough to explain the material's superconductivity. This suggests that spin-orbit coupling plays a crucial role in determining electronic properties in polar oxides.

**Why it may be interesting.** The discovery of a spin-mediated EPC mechanism provides a novel coupling channel that can be relevant to understanding superconductivity and spintronics in polar materials, bridging concepts from condensed matter magnetism and superconductivity.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the role of electronic spin in electron-phonon coupling (EPC), proposing a direct spin-mediated coupling mechanism contrary to conventional assumptions.

**Main result.** A strong spin-mediated EPC was observed in metallic strontium titanate, generating chiral phonon modes whose coupling strength is large enough to potentially explain superconductivity in the material.

**Method.** The study combines far-infrared light absorption measurements in a magnetic field (experiment) with theoretical modeling using Rashba-like coupling Hamiltonians (theory).

**Model / system.** The model system is Strontium Titanate (STO), studied in its metallic state via doping. The theoretical framework involves a Rashba-like EPC Hamiltonian interacting with transverse polar phonons.

**Key observables.** Spin-phonon hybridization signatures in optical absorption spectra, Magnetic Circular Dichroism (CD) response, and the electron-phonon coupling strength ($	au_R$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling strength ($	ilde{\lambda}_R$), carrier concentration (e.g., OVDb, 0.1% Nb), and the BCS coupling parameter ($\lambda_{BCS}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Approximations include assuming a simple antiphase oscillation for atomic displacement and simplifying the electronic dispersion (e.g., parabolic dispersion).

**Figures summary.** Figures show evidence of hybridized spin-phonon excitations in magneto-optical spectra, demonstrating polarization and doping dependence, and confirming the chiral nature of the coupling.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the existence of the spin-mediated EPC via optical measurements, then quantifying its dependence on carrier concentration, and finally relating the coupling strength to superconducting parameters.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Electron-phonon coupling (EPC) - the interaction between conduction electrons and quantized atomic vibrations - plays a central role in condensed matter physics and determines some of the most important properties of materials, such as electrical resistivity and superconductivity. Conventionally, EPC is assumed to be induced by the ionic electrostatic background, and electronic spin plays no role in the process. In stark contrast with this view, here we uncover a direct spin-mediated coupling mechanism between electrons and transverse polar phonons in a metal. Using far-infrared light absorption measurements of the model system SrTiO$_3$ in a magnetic field, we observe a strong spin-mediated EPC that is quantitatively consistent with recent theoretical predictions, and that generates chiral phonon modes with large effective magnetic moments. The extracted coupling strength is in good agreement with ab initio estimates and sufficiently high to explain superconductivity in SrTiO$_3$, thereby resolving a long-standing conundrum. Spin-chiral EPC should generically appear in all metals with polar phonons, and the present work could be of relevance to spintronics applications and to uncovering the origins of superconductivity in layered materials, metals with Dirac points in their electronic dispersions, and nearly ferroelectric superconductors.

</details>

**Summary.** This work derives a precise, closed-form upper bound for the rate of quantum randomness generation under semi-device-independent assumptions. By correctly solving the full POVM optimization, the authors correct previous overestimations and analyze how squeezing affects the achievable randomness rate. This is crucial for designing practical, high-security quantum random number generators.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides rigorous, quantifiable bounds on randomness extraction from quantum sources, which is a core topic in quantum information theory and quantum optics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the theoretical limit for generating quantum randomness in a semi-device-independent setting.

**Main result.** A closed-form, unconditional upper bound for the certified asymptotic Shannon rate is derived, which is shown to be tight on a numerically verified dual-feasibility region.

**Method.** The problem is formulated as a Linear Program (LP) over binary POVMs, requiring the inclusion of deterministic extreme points for an accurate rate calculation.

**Model / system.** The model uses a trusted binary pure-state source and an untrusted binary detector whose side information is classical, specifically tested with squeezed-coherent BPSK sources.

**Key observables.** Certified asymptotic i.i.d. Shannon rate ($H_{	ext{cert}}$), Gram overlap ($\delta$), and single-symbol error probability ($q$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Squeezing parameter ($r$), photon budget ($ar{n}$), and the error probability $q$ relative to critical thresholds ($q_b, q_z$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The adversary's side information ($\lambda$) is assumed to be classical; the rate formula is an upper bound, though tight in the verified region.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the comparison between the extended LP closed form and projective-only treatments, mapping the rate in the $(\delta, q)$ plane, and showing the trade-off between squeezing and randomness.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by deriving the rate expression via LP optimization, establishing the necessary and sufficient conditions for the rate to be achievable (the dual-feasibility region), and finally applying the result to the specific physical case of squeezed-coherent BPSK sources.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This paper investigates semi-device-independent quantum randomness generation with a trusted binary pure-state source and an untrusted binary detector whose side information is classical. We derive a closed-form Shannon-rate expression for this setting, depending only on the trusted Gram overlap of the two source states and the observed symmetric error probability. The key point is that the full binary-qubit POVM optimisation must include the two deterministic extreme points omitted by the projective-only treatment; including them gives a substantially lower, and correct, certified rate. The closed form is an unconditional upper bound on the certified asymptotic i.i.d.\ Shannon rate, and becomes tight on a numerically verified dual-feasibility region containing all operating points used in the paper. Outside this region the same expression remains an upper bound. We then apply the result to squeezed-coherent BPSK sources, showing how squeezing changes the trade-off between state distinguishability and certified randomness in the lossless and lossy regimes. Finally, we clarify the adversary model if the adversary is allowed to hold a detector-purification register that tags the outcome.

</details>

### [The quantum-gravitational imitation game](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03688v1)

**Authors:** Kristian Toccacelo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03688v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03688_figures/2606.03688_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: The quantum-gravitational imitation game. Alice and Bob feed some input state to</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03688_figures/2606.03688_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Gravitational teleportation. The gravitational interaction between two harmonic</sub>

**Summary.** This paper proposes using quantum information theory to test the quantum nature of gravity by framing the problem as a 'quantum-gravitational imitation game.' The authors analyze how gravitational interactions affect quantum state teleportation, establishing a measurable fidelity bound that can distinguish between quantum and classical gravitational predictions. This provides a theoretical roadmap for future tabletop experiments testing quantum gravity.

**Why it may be interesting.** It connects fundamental quantum information concepts (teleportation, LOCC) to the most challenging area of physics—quantum gravity—using measurable, tabletop-scale quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to empirically distinguish between quantum and classical descriptions of gravity by testing what quantum gravity can do that classical theories cannot.

**Main result.** The test involves comparing the measured fidelity ($\mathcal{F}$) against a classical threshold ($\mathcal{F}_{	ext{cl}}$); violation ($\mathcal{F} > \mathcal{F}_{	ext{cl}}$) would suggest quantum gravitational effects.

**Method.** The authors frame the test as a 'quantum-gravitational imitation game' involving four parties and analyzing the fidelity of quantum state teleportation mediated by gravitational interaction.

**Model / system.** The model uses mechanical oscillators (Alice and Bob) whose joint state undergoes evolution ($\Im_t$) representing the gravitational interaction, analogous to quantum teleportation.

**Key observables.** Fidelity ($\mathcal{F}$) and the classical threshold ($\mathcal{F}_{	ext{cl}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling constant $\gamma_g = G m / (\omega d^3)$ and the time $t$ relative to the full teleportation time $t_s$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The test assumes an effective description for the null hypothesis (perturbatively quantized gravity) and relies on comparing results against the LOCC framework.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the quantum-gravitational imitation game setup, and Figure 2 illustrates the dynamics of gravitational teleportation using beamsplitter analogies.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem, defines the imitation game setup, analyzes the dynamics of gravitational teleportation, derives the classical bounds ($\mathcal{F}_{	ext{cl}}$) for partial dynamics, and discusses the implications of violating the LOCC hypothesis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Gravity is the most apparent force in our everyday existence. Yet its fundamental nature remains the most opaque of the known interactions. This gap in our understanding is, in large part, due to the weakness of the gravitational interaction, which makes its empirical probing exceedingly hard. Nevertheless, on the backdrop of rapid advances in quantum technologies, hope has mounted that tests of the quantum nature of gravity could be realized in tabletop experiments. In this essay, we frame these recently proposed tests as quantum-gravitational imitation games. In particular, we examine how gravitational interactions among mechanical oscillators enable the teleportation of arbitrary quantum states and how this can inform fundamental tests of gravity.

</details>
