<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.02808_figures/2606.02808_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.02808_figures/2606.02808_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work models structural glasses by treating them as systems with quenched disorder within a field-theoretic framework. It derives the random first-order transition from the free energy landscape and successfully predicts the characteristic boson peak in the vibrational density of states. This connects the structural complexity of amorphous materials to their measurable low-frequency excitation spectrum.

**Why it may be interesting.** The connection drawn between the complex, multi-valley free energy landscape (a hallmark of disordered systems) and a specific, measurable excitation spectrum (the boson peak) provides a powerful theoretical link between static structural disorder and low-energy dynamics, relevant for understanding amorphous materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to theoretically explain the emergence of the boson peak in the vibrational density of states of structural glasses and to characterize the non-ergodic glassy phase.

**Main result.** The formalism naturally yields a description of the random first-order transition, establishing a link between the multiplicity of local ground states and the characteristic $\omega^4$ excess contribution to the spectral density (the boson peak).

**Method.** The authors employ a functional field-theoretic approach, utilizing ensemble averaging over quenched disorder realizations and analyzing the resulting free energy landscape via replica methods and zeta-function formalisms.

**Model / system.** The system is structural glasses (amorphous solids) modeled using a coarse-grained density field coupled to a multiplicative quenched disorder field. The analysis is performed in the context of continuum field theory.

**Key observables.** Average free energy functional, effective actions, spectral density $g(\omega)$, and the boson peak ($\delta g(\omega) \propto \omega^4$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Disorder strength (covariance $C$), temperature/inverse temperature ($eta$), and the dimensionality of the system ($d$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on a coarse-grained description, the use of the replica trick, and approximations such as the diagonal approximation in function space for simplifying the disorder average.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the functional framework for the average free energy in the presence of quenched disorder, to analyzing the resulting multivalley structure that characterizes the random first-order transition, and finally applying this to the dynamic problem to derive the low-frequency spectral density exhibiting the boson peak.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show the emergence of a contribution characteristic of the boson peak in the spectral density of structural glasses. To model the vitreous state, we consider static density-fluctuation fields coupled to a multiplicative quenched disorder. Performing an ensemble average over all disorder realizations, a functional series representation of the average free energy is obtained. In this series representation of the average free energy for the glassy state of matter, we identify in the function space effective actions. These effective actions present a large number of metastable states and ground states. Random first-order transition, widely discussed in the literature as a description of the transition from the supercooled liquid to the glassy state of matter, emerges naturally in our formalism. We establish the connection between the use of hyperbolic differential equations with random coefficients and the presence of many ground states in the average free energy. This connection allows us to study emergent excitations in such amorphous materials.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper investigates the effective scattering properties and cluster binding energies of heteronuclear ultracold atomic mixtures confined in quasi-low dimensions. By solving the scattering problem in 1D and 2D using advanced techniques like the Lippmann-Schwinger equation, the authors predict conditions favorable for observing universal few-body quantum phases in experiments.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the theoretical modeling of few-body physics in highly controllable quantum systems, providing quantitative predictions for the observation of universal many-body states in reduced dimensions.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to derive the effective s-wave scattering parameters and compute the binding energies of universal $(1+N)$ clusters in heteronuclear ultracold mixtures confined in quasi-low dimensions.

**Main result.** The calculations provide a theoretical pathway for practically observing universal clusters and their associated many-body phases in low-dimensional ultracold heteronuclear systems.

**Method.** The authors employ the Lippmann-Schwinger equation framework, solving coupled scattering problems in 2D and 1D geometries, and utilizing matrix diagonalization techniques to handle divergences.

**Model / system.** The system involves two heteronuclear atoms (unequal masses) harmonically confined in quasi-low dimensions (q1D and q2D), exemplified by Li-K and Li-Cr mixtures.

**Key observables.** Effective 2D/1D scattering lengths ($a_{2D}, a_{1D}$) and effective ranges ($R_{2D}, R_{1D}$), and the binding energies of $(1+N)$ clusters ($\Delta_{1+N,N}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Confinement strengths, 3D scattering parameters, and the ratio $R^*/a_s$ are critical parameters governing the effective low-dimensional physics.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations such as decoupling the transverse center-of-mass motion for 2D, and using specific regularization techniques (e.g., short-time/long-time cutoffs) to handle divergences.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the effective scattering parameters and the binding energies of two-body and few-body clusters for Li-Cr and Li-K systems in both quasi-2D and quasi-1D geometries.

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by first deriving the effective low-dimensional scattering parameters using the Lippmann-Schwinger equation for 2D, then extending the analysis to 1D, and finally applying these effective parameters to calculate the binding energies of universal clusters.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the effective s-wave scattering of two heteronuclear atoms harmonically confined in quasi-low dimensions, where the atoms have unequal masses and are subject to different confinement frequencies. The resulting effective scattering parameters in low dimensions, including scattering length and effective range, are derived as functions of three-dimensional scattering parameters and confinement strengths. Using realistic Li-K and Li-Cr mixtures as examples, we further compute the binding energies of universal $(1+N)$ clusters in quasi-low dimensions using the effective scattering parameters. Our findings suggest a promising pathway for practically observing universal clusters and their associated many-body phases in low-dimensional ultracold heteronuclear systems.

</details>

### [On the saturated cases of the distillability conjecture](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03561v1)

**Authors:** Saiqi Liu, Lin Chen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03561v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03561_figures/2606.03561_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>

<img src="2606.03561_figures/2606.03561_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03561_figures/2606.03561_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.03561_figures/2606.03561_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper tackles the distillability conjecture for Werner states by finding the exact conditions under which the associated inequality becomes an equality. Through extensive mathematical analysis involving singular values and optimization, the authors prove that saturation necessitates a highly constrained, $2 	imes 2$ block-diagonal structure for the defining matrices. This provides a major structural breakthrough in quantum information theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous mathematical characterization of saturation points for entanglement measures provides deep structural insights into the limits of quantum information processing protocols.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the saturation conditions for the distillability conjecture concerning two-copy four-by-four Werner states.

**Main result.** Saturation of the inequality $\sigma_1(X)^2 + \sigma_2(X)^2 \le 1/2$ forces the underlying matrices $A$ and $B$ to adopt a specific $2 	imes 2$ block-diagonal structure.

**Method.** The authors combine theoretical characterization of saturation cases with advanced mathematical tools, including manifold optimization and eigenvalue/singular value analysis.

**Model / system.** The problem is framed using matrices $A, B \in M_4(\mathbb{C})$ that define the state $X = A \otimes I + I \otimes B$, relating to the distillability of entangled quantum states.

**Key observables.** The primary observable is the sum of the squares of the two largest singular values, $\sigma_1(X)^2 + \sigma_2(X)^2$, which is compared against the bound of $1/2$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The matrices $A$ and $B$ are constrained by $	ext{Tr}(A) = 	ext{Tr}(B) = 0$ and $\|A\|_F^2 + \|B\|_F^2 = 1$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on assuming specific equalities hold (e.g., for optimization) or that restricting the analysis to invertible matrices is sufficient due to density arguments.

**Figures summary.** The notes do not describe specific figures, but mention numerical examples (e.g., Example 24) used to support the theoretical bounds.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically reviews known results, develops the main theorem by analyzing multiple structural cases (Case i, ii, iii), and uses optimization/eigenvalue analysis to reduce all possibilities to the common $2 	imes 2$ block-diagonal structure.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The distillability conjecture for two-copy four-by-four Werner states has been an open problem in quantum information for years. We investigate the conditions under which the conjectured inequality becomes an equality. For all known cases where the conjecture has been verified, we characterize the saturation conditions and show that equality forces the matrices $A$ and $B$ to be two-by-two block-diagonal. In particular, several previously obtained partial results, including the cases of one normal matrix, unitary similarity between $B$ and $-A$ or $-A^T$, and anti-diagonal block structures, are reduced to this common block-diagonal structure. We also employ a manifold optimization method, which provides numerical evidence that the two-by-two block-diagonal structure is essential for saturating the inequality. Furthermore, we prove that the identified saturation points are critical points of the objective function on the constraint manifold.

</details>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical study investigates the electronic, magnetic, and optical properties of $	ext{RE}_2	ext{MnNiO}_6$ double-perovskites using DFT+U. By systematically varying the rare earth element, the authors demonstrate how lattice contraction and $4f-d$ hybridization govern the material's potential for optoelectronic applications. The work provides a comprehensive picture of structure-property relationships in this class of correlated oxides.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit treatment of $4f$ electrons and the investigation of hybridization effects in transition metal oxides directly addresses strong electronic correlations, which is central to understanding many correlated materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To systematically study the optoelectronic and magnetic properties of the $	ext{RE}_2	ext{MnNiO}_6$ double-perovskite oxide family, focusing on the influence of the A-site ionic radius via lanthanide contraction.

**Main result.** The study establishes a unified picture showing that the magnetic and optoelectronic potential of these materials is determined by the interplay between $4f$ electron occupancy and octahedral distortion.

**Method.** The research employs first-principles calculations using the DFT+U framework, analyzing electronic structure, lattice dynamics, and optical properties.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the $	ext{RE}_2	ext{MnNiO}_6$ double-perovskite oxide, where $	ext{RE}$ ranges from $	ext{La}$ to $	ext{Lu}$. The calculations treat the challenging $4f$ electrons both as frozen core states and explicitly in the valence manifold.

**Key observables.** Electronic band structure, dielectric function, phonon dispersion, magnetic moments (up to $30 \mu_B$), and optical absorption edges.

**Important parameters / regimes.** A-site ionic radius (governing lanthanide contraction), DFT+U parameters ($U=4 	ext{ eV}, J=5 	ext{ eV}$), and temperature (for thermodynamic analysis).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calculations assume the monoclinic $P2_1/c$ phase is the most relevant structure, and the methodology must account for the strong localization challenge of $4f$ electrons.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the crystal structure ($	ext{LMNO}$), phonon band structures showing $	ext{Re}$ dependence, and comprehensive optical properties ($\varepsilon(\omega)$, $\alpha(\omega)$, $R(\omega)$) across the series.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a systematic computational approach: first establishing the structural and electronic ground state using DFT+U, then analyzing lattice dynamics and thermal properties, and finally detailing the optical response, all while tracking the systematic changes across the lanthanide series.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

RE2NiMnO6 (RE = La-Lu) family of ordered double-perovskite oxides hosts a corner-sharing network of alternating NiO6 and MnO6 octahedra whose electronic and magnetic ground states are systematically governed by the A-site ionic radius through the lanthanide contraction. The strong localisation of RE 4f electrons poses a fundamental challenge to density-functional treatments, yet the hybridisation between 4f and neighbouring 5d (RE) or 3d (Ni, Mn) states is central to the origin of exchange interactions and optoelectronic response across the series. We present a comprehensive first-principles study of the electronic structure, lattice dynamics, and optical properties of representative RE2NiMnO6 compounds within the DFT+U framework. To disentangle the role of Kondo-type 4f - d hybridisation, calculations are performed with the RE 4f electrons treated both as frozen core states and explicitly in the valence manifold, enabling a direct assessment of their contribution to the band structure, dielectric function, and phonon dispersion. Spin-polarised calculations reveal significant spin-channel asymmetry, with magnetic moments reaching up to 30 μB per formula unit for select members of the series. The results establish a unified picture of how 4f occupancy and octahedral distortion collectively determine the magnetic and optoelectronic potential of this double-perovskite family.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03517"></a>
### [Scalable On-Hardware Training of Quantum Neural Networks and Application to Clinical Data Imputation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03517v1)

**Authors:** Natansh Mathur, Panagiotis Kl. Barkoutsos, Masako Yamada, Martin Roetteler, Iordanis Kerenidis  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03517v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `QC/QI experiment` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03517_figures/2606.03517_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. The quantum component of the hybrid imputation pipeline, shown for n = 8 qubits, consists of three mod- ules. (i) Non-Gaussian state initialisation: each group of four qubits is prepared in the entangled state |ϕ⟩= (|0011⟩+ |1100⟩)/ √</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03517_figures/2606.03517_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Layer-wise training workflow for the Butterfly quan- tum neural network. Smaller n/2-qubit Butterfly subcircuits are trained first (classically or on quantum hardware) and then frozen. A final coupling layer is added to form an n- qubit circuit, and only this newly introduced layer is opti- mised on quantum hardware using parallel parameter-shift, substantially reducing measurement and optimisation cost.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03517_figures/2606.03517_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Overview of the hybrid classical-quantum imputation pipeline. Given a partially observed patient vector, observed features (green) are used as inputs and missing entries (orange) are predicted. The Quantum circuit has multiple components. The first orange box corresponds to the Magic State Loader (Section II A 1), the second blue box corresponds to the data loader (Section II A 2) and the final red box signifies the trainable PQC (Section II A 3).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03517_figures/2606.03517_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Downstream survival prediction AUC for a classi- cal 16 × 16 neural network trained classically and a hybrid classical-quantum neural network with a 16-qubit quantum layer trained directly on the IonQ Forte Enterprise trapped- ion processor (16-Qubit Hybrid DeepImputer). Both models share the same overall architecture; inference is performed on a classical simulator in both cases. The hybrid model achieves a higher median AUC with reduced variance across random seeds, demonstrating that on-hardware training via the par- allel parameter-shift rule yields models competitive with clas- sical counterparts.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.03517_figures/2606.03517_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Comparison of the 16-Qubit hybrid neural net- work model performance when the quantum neural network is trained using ideal simulation, noisy simulation, and direct execution on IonQ Forte Enterprise hardware. No statisti- cally significant degradation is observed when moving from simulation to hardware. The inference was performed on the classical simulator for all.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a novel, scalable training framework for QNNs designed to overcome the quadratic scaling bottleneck of gradient estimation on quantum hardware. By combining a structured Butterfly circuit with a layer-wise training strategy and parallel gradient evaluation, the authors reduce the cost to $O(\log n)$. They successfully demonstrate this approach by training hybrid QNNs on trapped-ion hardware for clinical data imputation, achieving performance comparable to state-of-the-art classical methods while exhibiting lower variance.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work directly addresses the practical scalability of quantum machine learning by providing a concrete, hardware-aware optimization technique that reduces resource scaling from polynomial to logarithmic.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Training Quantum Neural Networks (QNNs) on near-term quantum hardware is bottlenecked by the prohibitively high cost of gradient estimation using standard parameter-shift methods.

**Main result.** The authors introduce a framework that reduces the gradient estimation cost from $O(n^2)$ to $O(\log n)$ circuit evaluations per step, enabling scalable, hardware-feasible QNN training.

**Method.** The framework combines a structured Butterfly circuit architecture, a layer-wise training strategy, and a parallelized parameter-shift rule that exploits the commuting structure within each layer.

**Model / system.** The model is a hybrid classical-quantum QNN utilizing a Butterfly ansatz. The physical platform is demonstrated on IonQ Forte Enterprise trapped-ion hardware (16 qubits), with simulation up to 32 qubits.

**Key observables.** Area Under the ROC Curve (AUC) for downstream patient survival prediction, and the reduction in circuit evaluations per optimization step.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The complexity scales as $O(\log n)$ circuit evaluations per step, and the hardware training limit is demonstrated at 16 qubits.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The layer-wise training approach is a greedy approximation, and the framework relies on exploiting the commuting structure within the Butterfly layers.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the QNN architecture modules, the layer-wise training workflow, and performance comparisons showing hardware-trained models matching or exceeding classical baselines.

**Paper structure.** The paper first identifies the gradient estimation bottleneck, then proposes the three-part co-designed framework (Butterfly circuit, layer-wise training, parallel gradient rule). It validates this on clinical data imputation using MIMIC-III, showing hardware feasibility and competitive performance against classical methods.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Training quantum neural networks (QNNs) on quantum hardware is currently bottlenecked by the cost of gradient estimation: standard parameter-shift methods require a number of circuit evaluations that grows quadratically with the number of trainable parameters, making hardware-based optimisation impractical beyond small system sizes. In this work, we introduce a training framework that reduces this cost to logarithmic in the number of qubits, making gradient-based QNN optimisation feasible on near-term hardware at increasing scales.   Our framework combines three co-designed ingredients: (i) a structured, subspace-preserving Butterfly circuit architecture with $O(n \log n)$ parameters and logarithmic depth; (ii) a layer-wise training strategy that confines on-hardware optimisation to one small, well-structured layer at a time; and (iii) a parallelised parameter-shift rule that exploits the commuting structure within each Butterfly layer to extract all gradients in a constant number of circuit executions. Together these reduce the number of distinct circuit evaluations per optimisation step from $O(n^2)$ to $O(\log n)$.   We validate the framework on clinical data imputation using the MIMIC-III electronic health record dataset, a demanding benchmark sensitive to optimisation instability and model variance. Hybrid classical-quantum models are trained directly on IonQ Forte Enterprise trapped-ion hardware at 16 qubits without performance degradation relative to ideal or noisy simulation and via tensor-network simulation at 32 qubits, with 32-qubit inference executed on hardware. The resulting models match or exceed strong classical neural baselines in downstream patient survival prediction while exhibiting reduced variance across runs, demonstrating that the proposed framework enables practical, scalable QNN training under realistic hardware constraints.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.02987"></a>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.02987_figures/2606.02987_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Schematic illustration of the multichannel structure of the system. For clarity, only the lowest three CoM chan- nels are shown. The solid red lines represent the threshold energies of the channels, the red dashed lines denote scatter- ing states, and the blue dots indicate the bound states in the corresponding channels. The orange lines represent the inter- channel couplings induced by the term gzZ. For details, see Sec. III A.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.02987_figures/2606.02987_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Bound-state energies Eb of a 6Li-53Cr system with m1 : m2 = 6 : 53 and ω1 : ω2 = 6 : 1. Panels (a) and (b) show the results for R3D = 0 and R3D = 6ℓz, respectively. </details>

**Summary.** This paper provides an exact theoretical solution to the two-body problem for heteronuclear ultracold atoms in a quasi-2D trap. By analyzing the coupling between the center-of-mass and relative motion, the authors derive expressions for the effective 2D scattering parameters. The results demonstrate that these parameters can be rapidly tuned by adjusting the trap frequencies and the underlying 3D scattering interactions, offering a powerful tool for manipulating interatomic forces.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed theoretical framework for controlling effective interactions in highly confined quantum systems, which is crucial for designing quantum simulators and realizing novel quantum phenomena.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To solve the two-body scattering problem for heteronuclear ultracold atoms confined in a quasi-two-dimensional geometry.

**Main result.** The study derives the 2D scattering length ($a_{2D}$) and effective range ($R_{2D}$), showing that the coupling between longitudinal center-of-mass and relative motion can induce multiple 2D scattering resonances.

**Method.** The problem is solved exactly by treating it as a multichannel system, involving the separation of the center-of-mass motion and solving coupled integral equations for the scattering and bound states.

**Model / system.** The system consists of two heteronuclear ultracold atoms trapped in a quasi-2D harmonic potential, where the longitudinal confinement frequencies for the two atoms differ ($\omega_1 
eq \omega_2$). The interaction is modeled by a zero-range pseudopotential characterized by 3D scattering parameters.

**Key observables.** 2D scattering length ($a_{2D}$), 2D effective range ($R_{2D}$), and bound-state energies ($E_b$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Longitudinal confinement frequencies ($\omega_1, \omega_2$), 3D scattering length ($a_{3D}$), and 3D effective range ($R_{3D}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to the low-energy regime, and the derivation relies on short-range expansions and the use of Hadamard finite part integrals to regularize divergences.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the schematic confinement setup, the dependence of $a_{2D}$ and $R_{2D}$ on $a_{3D}$ for different frequency ratios ($\omega_1 > \omega_2$ vs $\omega_1 < \omega_2$), and the multichannel structure of the Hamiltonian.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically derives the scattering and bound state properties by first setting up the coupled Hamiltonian, then solving the resulting Lippmann-Schwinger type integral equations for the scattering state, and finally deriving the homogeneous integral equation for the bound state energy.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We solve the two-body problem of ultracold heteronuclear atoms in a quasi-two-dimensional (quasi-2D) geometry. The quasi-2D confinement is realized by a harmonic trap along the longitudinal ($z$-) direction, with different trap frequencies for the two atoms, as in many current experiments on ultracold heteronuclear gases. As a consequence, the longitudinal center-of-mass (CoM) motion is coupled to the relative motion, which significantly complicates the two-body problem. We solve this problem exactly and derive the 2D scattering length $a_{\rm 2D}$, the 2D effective range parameter $R_{\rm 2D}$, and the bound-state energies, as functions of the $s$-wave scattering length and effective range of the two atoms in free three-dimensional (3D) space. We show that multiple 2D scattering resonances can be induced by the coupling between the longitudinal CoM and relative motion. Around these resonances, $a_{\rm 2D}$ varies rapidly with the 3D scattering parameters, while $R_{\rm 2D}$ is strongly enhanced. Since the effective pairwise interaction in quasi-2D ultracold gases is determined by i.e., the two-body scattering amplitudes and bound-state energies, our results can be used for manipulating the effective 2D interatomic interaction in quasi-2D ultracold heteronuclear gases by tuning the confinement frequencies and the 3D scattering parameters.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03182"></a>
### [Logarithmic Sobolev inequality and hypercontractivity for the Navier-Stokes Fokker-Planck operator](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03182v1)

**Authors:** Zhi-Wei Wang, Samuel L. Braunstein  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03182v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03182_figures/2606.03182_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03182_figures/2606.03182_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03182_figures/2606.03182_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03182_figures/2606.03182_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.03182_figures/2606.03182_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proves that the stochastic Navier-Stokes system satisfies the Logarithmic Sobolev Inequality and is hypercontractive, even with the addition of nonlinear convective terms. This remarkable result stems from the fact that the convective dynamics are antisymmetric with respect to the equilibrium measure, leaving the key functional properties unchanged from the simpler dissipative case. It provides a powerful mathematical tool for analyzing the rate of approach to thermal equilibrium in complex fluid dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The mathematical techniques used to prove these functional inequalities (LSI, hypercontractivity) are highly relevant to analyzing the approach to equilibrium in complex, non-equilibrium systems, including those studied in AMO physics and quantum optics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to prove functional inequalities, specifically the Logarithmic Sobolev Inequality (LSI) and hypercontractivity, for the Fokker-Planck generator associated with the stochastic incompressible Navier-Stokes equations.

**Main result.** The LSI and hypercontractivity hold for the full generator, with optimal constants matching those of the pure Ornstein-Uhlenbeck operator, independent of the number of retained Fourier modes.

**Method.** The proof hinges on a structural property: the convective part of the generator is antisymmetric in the $L^2$ space weighted by the equilibrium measure, which causes it to vanish in the Dirichlet form and $L^q$ norm evolution.

**Model / system.** The system is the stochastic incompressible Navier-Stokes equations on the three-torus ($	ext{T}^3$), modeled by a Fokker-Planck generator $L = L_{	ext{diss}} + L_{	ext{conv}}$. The equilibrium state is the Gibbs measure $P_{	ext{eq}}$.

**Key observables.** Entropy functional ($	ext{Ent}$), Dirichlet form ($E(f, f)$), and the evolution of the $L^q$ norm ($\|F\|_{L^q}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Viscosity ($
u$) and the smallest non-zero eigenvalue of the Laplacian ($\lambda_1$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system possesses a Gibbs equilibrium, Hamiltonian conservative dynamics, and dissipation satisfying the fluctuation-dissipation relation.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the decomposition of the generator into dissipative (OU) and antisymmetric (convective) parts. It then uses the antisymmetry property to show that the Dirichlet form and $L^q$ evolution are unaffected by the convective term, leading to the proofs of LSI and hypercontractivity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The stochastic incompressible Navier-Stokes equations on $\TT^3$, completed by the fluctuation-dissipation noise, have a Fokker-Planck generator that decomposes into a self-adjoint Ornstein-Uhlenbeck (dissipative) part and an antisymmetric (convective) part. We prove two results about this generator. First, the logarithmic Sobolev inequality holds with the same optimal constant as the pure Ornstein-Uhlenbeck operator, $c_\mathrm{LSI} = νλ_1$ (where $ν$ is the viscosity and $λ_1$ is the smallest nonzero eigenvalue of the Laplacian on $\TT^3$), independent of the number of retained Fourier modes. Second, the full semigroup is hypercontractive with the same rate as the Ornstein-Uhlenbeck semigroup. Both results follow from a single structural property: the convective generator is antisymmetric in $L^2(P_\mathrm{eq})$ (where $P_\mathrm{eq}$ is the Gibbs measure), and therefore contributes nothing to the Dirichlet form or the $L^q$ norm evolution. The antisymmetry is a consequence of two properties of the incompressible Navier-Stokes nonlinearity: energy conservation and phase-space volume preservation (the Liouville property). These are the same properties that underpin the fluctuation-dissipation theorem for the nonlinear Navier-Stokes equations.

</details>

### [Nielsen complexity with multiple cost factors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02817v1)

**Summary.** This work refines the geometric theory of quantum complexity by generalizing the cost function to include multiple penalty factors associated with different non-local degrees of freedom. By analyzing the resulting modified geodesic equations and the Jacobi operator, the authors show that these multiple costs create distinct families of conjugate points. This provides a more detailed and physically realistic description of how quantum complexity scales in many-body systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The geometric understanding of complexity via generalized metrics and the detailed analysis of conjugate points provide powerful tools for characterizing the dynamical limitations and information processing capacity of complex quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper extends Nielsen's geometric approach to quantum complexity by incorporating multiple cost factors to better model the geometry of quantum evolution on the group manifold.

**Main result.** The introduction of multiple cost factors leads to a richer structure of conjugate points, distinguishing between local and non-local contributions to quantum complexity.

**Method.** The analysis involves generalizing the Euler-Arnold and Jacobi equations using a Finsler metric approach, leading to the study of the Jacobi operator's spectrum.

**Model / system.** The study is applied to N-qubit systems evolving on the unitary group SU(2N), modeled using Dyson equations and analyzed in the context of SYK-type many-body models.

**Key observables.** Complexity $C(U, 	ilde{U})$, geodesic distance, and the location/scaling of conjugate points.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Multiple cost factors ($\mu_1, \mu_2, \dots$), anisotropy angle ($	heta$), and system size ($N$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The complexity is formulated using a cost function satisfying specific mathematical properties, and the analysis often relies on approximations like high-frequency expansions or specific Hamiltonian limits.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the time evolution of the minimum eigenvalue of the Jacobi operator, showing zero crossings (conjugate times) as a function of cost factors and system type (e.g., 3-body vs 4-body Hamiltonians).

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from the standard single cost factor framework to a generalized formalism incorporating multiple penalties, deriving modified geodesic equations and analyzing the resulting structure of conjugate points in specific models like single-qubit and SYK systems.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate Nielsen's geometric approach to quantum complexity in the presence of multiple cost factors, extending the standard framework where a single penalty distinguishes easy from hard directions of the group manifold. By introducing a hierarchy of penalties associated with different degrees of non-locality, we develop a generalized right-invariant complexity geometry and analyze its implications for geodesic evolution. We derive the modified Euler-Arnold and Jacobi equations and study how multiple cost factors reshape the structure and scaling of conjugate points, where geodesic optimality breaks down. The formalism is illustrated in two settings: a single-qubit system with two cost factors, where we derive approximate analytic solutions for the complexity growth and its dependence on penalty hierarchies, and SYK-type models, where we analyze both free and chaotic regimes. In these many-body systems, we show that distinct non-local sectors generate multiple families of conjugate points whose occurrence depends on both the cost hierarchy and the system size. Our results highlight how refining the penalty structure provides a richer and more realistic description of quantum complexity and its dynamical behavior.

</details>

### [Certifying coherence in quantum devices under classical control](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03699v1)

**Authors:** Gabriele Cobucci, Nicola D'Alessandro, Raphael Brinster, Alexander Bernal, Nikolai Wyderka, Armin Tavakoli  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03699v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03699_figures/2606.03699_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Bounds on coherence properties in the channels Λdepol v ◦Λdepha u (red) and Λdepol v ◦Λamp γ (blue). **Summary.** This work provides a powerful mathematical toolbox to certify quantum coherence when the preparation process is corrupted by unknown classical variables. It achieves this by developing and comparing SDP-based criteria, offering both theoretically complete and computationally efficient methods. The results are crucial for analyzing the robustness of quantum superposition in real-world quantum devices and channels.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous mathematical tools developed for characterizing coherence in the presence of unknown classical noise are directly applicable to benchmarking quantum hardware and understanding decoherence mechanisms in open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops complete and practical methods to certify quantum coherence in devices whose preparation process is subject to hidden classical control parameters.

**Main result.** It establishes that coherence can be fully characterized by a hierarchy of Semidefinite Programs (SDPs) and provides computationally efficient SDP criteria for practical use.

**Method.** The core methodology involves formulating coherence certification as checking the feasibility of increasingly complex SDP constraints, often utilizing dual formulations and iterative optimization.

**Model / system.** The study analyzes quantum states ($ho_x$) generated by a preparation device under hidden classical control ($\lambda$), and the effect of quantum channels ($\Lambda$) on these states.

**Key observables.** Coherence quantifiers like $v^*(E)$ (based on isotropic noise) and the coherence witness $W(\Lambda(E))$ are central diagnostics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimensionality of the Hilbert space ($d$), the number of states ($N$), and the noise parameters ($v, u, \gamma$) are key parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The practical SDP criterion is an approximation of the full, infinite SDP hierarchy, and some analyses rely on approximating the Bloch sphere with a polyhedron.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the bounds on critical isotropic noise ($v$) derived from different SDP methods, showing superior accuracy for the proposed practical method. Another figure plots coherence bounds for composed channels.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the problem (coherence under classical control) to developing the full SDP hierarchy, proposing a practical SDP criterion, and finally applying these tools to analyze coherence preservation through various quantum channels.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum states that do not commute exhibit coherence, but only when the device preparing them is assumed to be unaffected by classical parameters inaccessible to the experimenter. Such hidden classical control arises both in fundamental tests of quantum phenomena and in quantum information protocols that operate under limited control assumptions. Here, we address the problem of coherence certification by developing complete and practically efficient methods. First, we prove that coherence can be fully characterised through a hierarchy of semidefinite programs. Second, we introduce a practical semidefinite programming approach that achieves useful accuracy while remaining computationally efficient even for preparation devices generating many, potentially high-dimensional, quantum states. For the important special case of qubits, we further exploit conceptual connections with the theory of joint measurability to obtain highly accurate coherence characterisation that scales to more than one thousand qubits. Finally, we apply these methods to determine whether quantum channels are able to preserve coherence or are inherently coherence-breaking. Together, these results provide a powerful toolbox for analysing quantum superposition in the presence of hidden classical control.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03004"></a>
### [Kinematical correlations via $κ$-Poincaré coproducts](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03004v1)

**Authors:** Mohammad Ali Gorji, Babak Vakili  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03004v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03004_figures/2606.03004_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: The solid curves show f  p0/κ; r  , defined in (3.4), plotted versus p0/κ for fixed</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper explores the kinematics of two-particle states in $\kappa$-Minkowski spacetime, a model incorporating quantum gravity effects via a deformation scale $\kappa$. The core finding is that the momentum composition law forces the appearance of distinct, branch-dependent correlations when the system enters a high-momentum regime. This mechanism is shown to be mathematically distinct from simpler nonrelativistic quantum models.

**Why it may be interesting.** While not directly related to AMO, the mathematical techniques used—analyzing non-linear mappings, branch cuts, and symmetry deformations—are highly relevant to understanding effective Hamiltonians in strongly coupled or highly constrained quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the kinematical consequences of the Hopf-algebraic momentum composition law within $\kappa$-Minkowski spacetime, focusing on how this structure dictates two-particle momentum correlations.

**Main result.** The structure leads to branch-dependent $\kappa$-deformed back-to-back momentum correlations in the high-momentum regime ($|\vec{P}| > \kappa$), which is a unique feature distinguishing it from standard nonrelativistic minimal-length models.

**Method.** The analysis involves comparing two parameterizations of the curved momentum space (ordered-plane-wave labels $p_\mu$ vs. classical-basis momenta $P_\mu$) and analyzing the resulting nonlinear map and its associated coproduct structure.

**Model / system.** The system is $\kappa$-Minkowski spacetime, governed by the $\kappa$-Poincaré Hopf algebra, which introduces a deformation scale $\kappa$ related to the Planck energy. The study focuses on the two-particle state structure derived from the coproduct constraint.

**Key observables.** Deformed back-to-back momentum correlations, the branch-sensitive quantity $P^+ \equiv P_0 + P_4$, and the Jacobian determinant $\det \partial P_\mu / \partial p_
u$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Deformation scale $\kappa$, the classical four-momentum $P_\mu$, and the ordered-plane-wave labels $p_\mu$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the existence of a regime where the inverse map $P 	o p$ is multi-valued (the two-branch regime), and it contrasts this with standard nonrelativistic models that lack this specific type of local inversion.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the function $f$ and the graphical solution of the inverse map, showing the transition from a single-valued to a two-branch structure as the momentum magnitude $|\vec{P}|$ crosses the threshold $\kappa$.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically compares the bicrossproduct basis (one-to-one map) with the classical basis (nonlinear map), derives the branch-resolving nature of the coproduct in the classical basis, and concludes by contrasting this structure with nonrelativistic models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study a kinematical consequence of the Hopf-algebraic momentum composition law in $κ$-Minkowski spacetime. The same curved momentum space can be described in different coordinates. In the bicrossproduct basis the ordered-plane-wave labels are the translation-generator eigenvalues, so the relevant map is one-to-one. In the classical basis, instead, the translation eigenvalues $P_μ$ are nonlinearly related to the ordered-plane-wave labels $p_μ$. This relation can fail to be globally one-to-one in a high-momentum region. When a given classical-basis four-momentum admits more than one real auxiliary preimage, the branch-sensitive quantity $P_+\equiv P_0+P_4=κe^{p_0/κ}$ enters the coproduct and resolves the branches in two-particle states. Imposing the vanishing total-momentum constraint therefore gives branch-dependent $κ$-deformed back-to-back momentum correlations. In a single-branch regime this is just a deformed correlated product, while in a multibranch regime a state specified only by $P_μ$ can be expanded into distinct auxiliary branches. If $P_μ$ are taken as the directly meaningful momenta, the physical content is the resulting deformed correlation pattern. If the auxiliary variables $p_μ$ are assigned operational meaning, the same constrained state can be interpreted as a superposition over different auxiliary branches. We also compare this structure with standard regular self-adjoint nonrelativistic minimal-length models and find no analogous smooth local two-real-branch inversion on their physical domains.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.03429"></a>

At the top is the probability distribution p1(α1) of the model with operator ϕ1 (and its c.c.) parameterized by g∗ 1 = r1u∗ 1 with u∗ 1 = z 2 5 (see red arrow in the unit circle). In the middle is the distribution p2(α1) with operator ϕ2 (and c.c.) parameterized by g∗ 2 = r2u∗ 2 with u∗ 2 = z 4 5 . </details>

**Summary.** This work introduces $q$-state spin models as a generalized maximum entropy framework for analyzing high-dimensional discrete data. It establishes that these models are characterized by gauge invariance, allowing for the identification of equivalent underlying statistical structures. The authors provide practical methods, including closed-form evidence calculations, for selecting the most parsimonious model describing complex systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous mathematical treatment of model equivalence via gauge transformations and the connection to information geometry provides powerful tools for analyzing complex, high-dimensional data structures, which is relevant for characterizing emergent order in quantum many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of modeling high-dimensional discrete data to infer the underlying microscopic constraints governing complex systems.

**Main result.** It develops a comprehensive framework using $q$-state spin models that are invariant under gauge transformations, allowing for robust model selection.

**Method.** The authors employ maximum entropy principles, utilizing loop expansions and deriving closed-form expressions for the marginal likelihood of Minimally Complex Models (MCMs).

**Model / system.** The model is a generalization of the Potts model to $q$-state spin systems defined on discrete variables, capturing arbitrary high-order correlations in data like protein sequences or neural activity.

**Key observables.** Marginal likelihood, partition function ($Z_M(g)$), and the geometric complexity of the model.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The number of states ($q$), the number of variables ($n$), and the interaction parameters ($g_\mu$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the large $N$ limit for model selection and assumes the data can be effectively described by the moments captured by the spin model.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate gauge transformations relating different model representations and show comparisons of entropy values for different discretization schemes.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the $q$-state spin models and their relation to discrete Fourier analysis, through the mathematical framework of gauge invariance, to deriving the closed-form evidence for MCMs, and finally applying this to model selection.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Modeling high-dimensional data is challenging, yet essential to understanding many complex systems. Maximum entropy models such as Ising and Potts models have been used extensively to capture pairwise interactions from correlation patterns in data, allowing to infer graphical representations of complex systems from observations (e.g., from protein sequences or neural population activity). Recently, there has been growing interest in modeling higher-order correlation patterns involving simultaneously three or more variables. While progress has been made in binary data with high-order Ising models, we extend this framework to the more general case of discrete data.   We introduce q-state spin models, a complete family of maximum entropy models that generalize the vector Potts model to include long-range and arbitrary high-order interactions. In the pairwise case, our models allow for more diverse interaction types compared to the standard vector Potts model. We discuss their statistical interpretation with examples and relate them to discrete Fourier analysis. Using a loop expansion of the partition function, we show that the statistical properties of spin models are fully captured by the algebraic structure of their interactions. We define gauge transformations under which this structure, and thus the partition function, remains invariant. Models equivalent under gauge transformations can be seen as different representations of the same abstract statistical model, despite generally having interactions of different orders, extending results from the binary case. For practical application to data analysis, we focus on a subset of models known in the binary case as Minimally Complex Models, generalizing them to discrete data. We obtain a closed-form expression for the marginal likelihood of these models, enabling fast model selection. We illustrate their use with simple real-world examples.

</details>

### [Predicting the conditions for observing the Mpemba effect](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03445v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical study predicts the necessary structural conditions for observing the Mpemba effect in overdamped Langevin systems. It shows that the effect is fundamentally governed by the presence of boundaries (walls) rather than the internal details of the potential wells. The analysis uses spectral methods to classify potentials and engineer systems exhibiting this counterintuitive thermal relaxation behavior.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous, mathematically grounded classification of the Mpemba effect, linking it directly to boundary conditions and spectral properties of the underlying dynamics, which is highly relevant for understanding non-equilibrium relaxation in complex systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the fundamental structural features of the energy landscape required for the Mpemba effect, a counterintuitive phenomenon where a hotter system relaxes faster than a colder one.

**Main result.** The existence of the Mpemba effect is primarily driven by the presence of boundaries (hard or soft walls), rather than the specific internal structure of the potential landscape. The analysis provides a unified classification across single- and double-well potentials.

**Method.** The study employs spectral decomposition of the Fokker-Planck operator and analyzes the behavior of the first nontrivial eigenmode, relating the effect to the non-monotonic dependence of the overlap coefficient on initial temperature.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled using one-dimensional overdamped Langevin dynamics for a particle in a potential V(x), governed by the Fokker-Planck equation. The analysis covers single-well and double-well potentials, considering the influence of hard or soft boundaries.

**Key observables.** Overlap coefficient ($a_2$), Mpemba temperature ($eta_M$), and the behavior of the first nontrivial eigenmode.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Initial temperature ($T_i$) or inverse temperature ($eta_i$), and the presence/location of boundaries ($L_-, L_+$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on asymptotic approximations, particularly in the low-bath-temperature regime, and assumes the potential grows fast enough at the boundaries.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate decision trees summarizing conditions for the Mpemba effect, and plots show the dependence of $eta_M$ on wall separation for various potential types.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing the mathematical framework using the Fokker-Planck operator, deriving the condition for the Mpemba effect via the overlap coefficient $a_2$, and finally classifying the necessary structural elements (boundaries) required for the effect to manifest.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Mpemba effect, a counterintuitive phenomenon where a hotter system relaxes faster than a colder one, has been widely observed in various nonequilibrium systems. Despite this progress, the fundamental structural features of the energy landscape required for its emergence remain a subject of debate. In this study, we investigate the conditions for the Mpemba effect within one-dimensional overdamped Langevin dynamics. We classify the potential landscapes based on the presence of single or double wells, their symmetry properties, and the existence of walls. We establish that the existence of the effect is primarily driven by the presence of boundaries, either hard or soft, rather than the specific internal structure of the potential landscape, such as metastability or the number of minima. By employing a spectral decomposition of the Fokker-Planck operator, we analyze the behavior of the first nontrivial eigenmode and demonstrate that its derivative acts as a Dirac delta peak in the low-temperature regime. This helps us elucidate the mechanism underlying the Mpemba effect: it appears as the interplay between this behavior and the initial population dynamics in a non-trivial way induced by the presence of the wall. Our analysis provides a unified classification across single- and double-well potentials, highlighting the crucial role of boundary conditions and asymmetry. Furthermore, we demonstrate that this framework allows for the engineering of potential landscapes capable of producing multistage Mpemba transitions.

</details>

### [Quantum Optimization Algorithms for Strongly Correlated Many-Body Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03147v1)

**Authors:** G. E. L. Pexe, L. A. M. Rattighieri, P. M. Prado, A. R. Fritsch, F. F. Fanchini  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03147v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03147_figures/2606.03147_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Comparative schematic of the main quantum optimization algorithms from a NISQ perspective. (a) In VQE, a variational circuit prepares an ansatz and measures the expectation value of the Hamiltonian, while a classical optimizer iteratively updates the parameters. (b) In QAOA, an alternating sequence of evolutions under the problem Hamiltonian Hp and the mixer Hamiltonian Hd is applied for a fixed depth p, followed by classical optimization of the angles. (c) In FALQON, the dynamics are guided by feedback based on measuring the commutator i[Hd, Hp], eliminating the external classical optimizer and introducing an iterative evolution with progressive growth of the circuit depth.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03147_figures/2606.03147_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Phenomenological signatures of quantum phase transitions and strongly correlated states targeted for investigation via quantum algorithms in the NISQ regime. (a) Divergence of the correlation length ξ and (b) algebraic closing of the energy gap ∆in the vicinity of a Deconfined Quantum Criticality (DQC) point gc. (c) Anomalous transport in the strange metal phase, evidenced by T-linear resistivity (ρ ∝T), in contrast to the quadratic dependence of a Fermi liquid. (d) Non-equilibrium dynamics of the entanglement entropy S(t) following a global quench: the Many-Body Localization (MBL) phase exhibits ultra-slow logarithmic growth, while the ergodic phase rapidly saturates following a...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03147_figures/2606.03147_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Representation of the fundamental challenges for variational quantum algorithms in condensed matter within the NISQ regime. (a) Visualization of a barren plateau, characterized by an exponentially flat cost landscape that renders gradient-based methods unfeasible. (b) Rugged cost landscape exhibiting multiple spurious local minima that trap classical optimizers. (c) Scaling of the gradient variance Var[∂θE] with the number of qubits N, demonstrating the severe exponential decay for Hardware Efficient Ans¨atze (HEA) compared to structured circuits. (d) Effect of experimental noise (η) on the flattening of the energy landscape and the displacement of the global minimum. (e)...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This perspective article analyzes the use of quantum optimization algorithms to study phase transitions in strongly correlated quantum systems. It critiques Variational Quantum Algorithms for suffering from barren plateaus and proposes Feedback-based Quantum Algorithms as a more robust alternative. The work highlights the necessity of physics-informed circuit design to achieve quantum advantage in condensed matter physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It directly addresses the computational bottleneck in simulating complex quantum matter, proposing a specific algorithmic pathway (FQAs) to overcome the limitations of current NISQ hardware for studying exotic phases.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating phase transitions in strongly correlated quantum many-body systems, which are intractable for classical simulation due to exponential Hilbert space growth or the fermionic sign problem.

**Main result.** Feedback-based quantum algorithms (FQAs) are argued to provide geometrically more robust trajectories for navigating the energy landscape than traditional Variational Quantum Algorithms (VQAs) in the NISQ era.

**Method.** The paper reviews and compares VQAs (VQE, QAOA) against FQAs (like FALQON), focusing on their application to quantum simulation.

**Model / system.** The study covers various strongly correlated models, including the SYK model (strange metals), the $J-Q$ model (DQC), the MBL model (disordered systems), and the Toric Code (topological phases).

**Key observables.** Energy expectation value, correlation length ($\xi$), entanglement entropy ($S$), dynamic structure factor ($S(q, \omega)$), and gap closing ($\Delta$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** NISQ era constraints, circuit depth, noise levels, and the ratio of coupling constants in specific Hamiltonians.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary limitations discussed are the exponential barren plateaus in VQAs and the sensitivity of all quantum methods to noise and circuit depth.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare VQE, QAOA, and FALQON approaches, illustrating challenges like barren plateaus, rugged cost landscapes, and the depth vs. fidelity trade-off.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing the classical limitations, reviewing VQAs, introducing FQAs as an improvement, and then applying these methods to specific condensed matter problems like DQC, MBL, and QSLs.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This perspective article analyzes the potential and critical challenges of employing quantum optimization algorithms to investigate phase transitions in quantum many-body systems during the Noisy Intermediate-Scale Quantum era. The simulation of strongly correlated systems is frequently intractable on classical computers due to the exponential growth of the Hilbert space and the fermionic sign problem. In this context, we review and compare the performance of traditional Variational Quantum Algorithms, such as the Variational Quantum Eigensolver and the Quantum Approximate Optimization Algorithm, against emerging heuristic approaches, specifically Feedback-based Quantum Algorithms, such as FALQON. We explore the applicability of these methods in the study of open phenomena in condensed matter physics, including Deconfined Quantum Criticality, strange metals, Many-Body Localization, topological phase transitions, and quantum spin liquids. We discuss how fundamental operational bottlenecks, notably expressibility- and noise-induced barren plateaus, severely compromise gradient-based optimization. We conclude that deterministic feedback-guided methods provide geometrically more robust trajectories for navigating the energy landscape of these systems, arguing that further advancement in the field will rely on deep hybridization and physics-informed circuit co-design towards fault tolerance.

</details>

### [Magnetic field effects on spin-split band and magnon transport in altermagnets and emergent compensated ferrimagnets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03451v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical work examines how magnetic fields influence spin-split magnon bands in altermagnets and compensated ferrimagnets. By analyzing the magnon Hamiltonian and calculating transport coefficients, the authors demonstrate that the field induces Zeeman-like shifts and that the specific magnetic symmetry dictates the resulting spin and thermal current response. This is crucial for understanding spin-charge/heat transport in complex magnetic materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study connects fundamental magnetic ordering (altermagnetism/ferrimagnetism) to measurable transport phenomena (spin/heat currents) under external fields, providing theoretical insights into emergent quantum magnet behavior.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how an external magnetic field affects the spin-split magnon band structure and the resulting spin and thermal transport properties in altermagnets and compensated ferrimagnets.

**Main result.** The study shows that the magnetic field induces Zeeman-like shifts in the magnon bands, and the specific magnetic structure dictates whether the resulting spin/thermal conductivity exhibits an asymmetric field dependence or a purely longitudinal response.

**Method.** The analysis uses theoretical modeling, transforming the spin Hamiltonian into a Bogoliubov magnon Hamiltonian, and calculating transport coefficients ($\chi^{SC}, \kappa$) using linear response theory.

**Model / system.** The systems modeled are altermagnets (using the $J_1-J_2-J'_2$ model on a square lattice) and compensated ferrimagnets (using the $J_1-J_3$ model on a kagome lattice). Both exhibit spin splitting without net magnetization.

**Key observables.** Spin-conductivity ($\chi^{SC}_{\mu
u}$), thermal conductivity ($\kappa_{\mu
u}$), magnon band dispersion, and spin polarization of magnon bands.

**Important parameters / regimes.** External magnetic field ($H$), easy-axis anisotropy ($D$), and lattice coupling constants ($J_1, J_2, J_3$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the energy scale of the magnetic field is smaller than the anisotropy gap, and transport coefficients are calculated using the relaxation-time approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the field dependence of magnon bands (showing shifts), and the resulting spin/thermal currents driven by thermal gradients in both altermagnetic and ferrimagnetic states.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the physical models and Hamiltonians for the two magnetic systems, to calculating the magnon band structure and spin polarization, and finally analyzing the resulting spin and thermal transport coefficients as a function of magnetic field and temperature.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In altermagnets and fully compensated ferrimagnets, not only the electron band but also the magnon band exhibits spin splitting without net magnetization, which enables thermal activation of the magnon spin current. Here, we theoretically investigate magnetic field effects on the magnon properties of these antiferromagnets in the presence of a weak easy-axis anisotropy which makes the collinear states robust against the magnetic field. For the altermagnet and compensated ferrimagnet, we analyze a 2 sublattice order in the $J_1$-$J_2$-$J_2^\prime$ model on the square lattice and a triple-${\bf Q}$ 12-sublattice order in the $J_1$-$J_3$ model on the kagome lattice, respectively, each accompanied by $d$-wave and $s$-wave spin splitting at zero field. It is shown that for positive (negative) magnetic field $H$ whose energy scale is smaller than the anisotropy gap, the up- and down-spin magnon bands are shifted to lower (higher) and higher (lower) energies, respectively, similarly to the Zeeman coupling in electron systems. In the altermagnet, with increasing field, the $d$-wave splitting tends to be deformed into the $s$-wave one, which is reflected as the change in the direction of the spin current generated by thermal gradient. In the compensated ferrimagnet, the $s$-wave nature, i.e., the population imbalance between the up- and down-spin magnons at $H=0$, results in an asymmetric field dependence of the longitudinal spin and thermal conductivities.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.02880"></a>
### [Towards Efficient Synthesis of Quantum Graph States by Fusing Graph Motifs](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02880v1)

**Authors:** Tingxiang Ji, Hansika Weerasena, Demitry Farfurnik, Jianqing Liu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02880v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.02880_figures/2606.02880_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: Resource graph state motifs used in this work. (a) Star motif: a GHZ-type graph state with one central qubit connected to N −1 others, totaling N qubits. (b) Linear motif: a path-like graph state with N qubits connected sequentially. (c) Ring motif: a cyclic graph state with N qubits. These three motifs serve as the primitive building blocks in the proposed CFD framework.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.02880_figures/2606.02880_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: Schematic of the Type-I fusion in a photonic imple- mentation. Two input qubits, one from each graph state, are interfered on a polarising beamsplitter followed by waveplate and detector, probabilistically merging the two fragments into a larger graph state with success probability 1/2.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.02880_figures/2606.02880_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3: Overview of the proposed Cost-aware Fusion-based Decomposition (CFD) framework. Given a target graph state, CFD first identifies the LC-equivalent graph state with the minimum number of edges, then decomposes it into ring, star, and linear motifs through three successive extraction stages. The resulting motifs are realized as photonic resource states and connected via Type-I fusion operations to synthesize the LC-equivalent graph state, from which the target graph state is recovered by applying LC operations in the reverse order.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.02880_figures/2606.02880_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4: Photonic generation rates for graph states of size n = 4 to 9 across LC equivalence classes using the CFD framework. For each target graph state, CFD is applied to every graph state in its LC equivalence class and the generation rate is computed from the resulting motif decomposition. The plotted quantities correspond to the three cases defined in the Evaluation Method: CFD (min-edge), the generation rate obtained by applying CFD to the LC-equivalent graph state with the minimum number of edges; CFD (target), the generation rate obtained by applying CFD directly to the target graph state; and CFD (best), the minimum resource overhead achieved by CFD across the LC equivalence class.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.02880_figures/2606.02880_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5: Resource overhead comparison of EDCG, DEBC, and the proposed CFD framework across graph states of size n = 4 to 9. EDCG incurs a fixed overhead of n(n −1) regardless of target topology, while DEBC and CFD both take the minimum-edge LC-equivalent graph state as input. Lower resource overhead corresponds to higher photonic generation rate.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a novel, cost-aware decomposition method (CFD) to overcome the exponential resource limitations in synthesizing photonic graph states. By leveraging local Clifford equivalence and decomposing the target state into fundamental motifs (ring, star, linear), the authors significantly reduce resource overhead. This provides a practical and scalable pathway toward building complex quantum circuits using photonic hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, scalable algorithmic blueprint for realizing complex quantum resources (graph states) in photonic systems, directly impacting the feasibility of measurement-based quantum computation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is efficiently synthesizing complex photonic graph states required for advanced quantum computing and sensing applications.

**Main result.** The proposed Cost-aware Fusion-based Decomposition (CFD) framework achieves up to an 84.6% reduction in resource overhead compared to baseline methods, leading to massive improvements in the photonic generation rate.

**Method.** The method involves a three-stage heuristic decomposition: first, finding an LC-equivalent state with minimum edges; second, decomposing this state into fundamental motifs (ring, star, linear); and third, assembling these motifs via Type-I fusion operations.

**Model / system.** The work focuses on photonic graph states, where qubits are vertices and CZ entangling operations form the edges. The synthesis is performed on a photonic platform using Type-I fusion operations.

**Key observables.** Resource overhead (total photon cost $M$), photonic generation rate $R(G)$, and physical-qubit consumption.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The exponential dependence of the generation rate on the number of fusion operations ($k$) and the resource cost.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that selecting the LC-equivalent graph state with the minimum number of edges serves as a highly effective proxy for near-optimal synthesis.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the three resource motifs (Star, Linear, Ring), the schematic of the Type-I fusion process, and quantitative comparisons showing CFD's superior performance in resource overhead and generation rate across various graph sizes and lattice structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the problem limitations, introduces the concept of LC equivalence, details the CFD heuristic framework (motif decomposition $ightarrow$ fusion $ightarrow$ LC operations), and validates the approach through extensive numerical comparisons against baseline constructions on lattice and general graph states.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Photonic graph states with advanced topologies can enable measurement-based quantum computing, distributed quantum sensing, and quantum interconnects. However, the efficient generation of photonic graph states is limited by the probabilistic nature of photonic entangling operations and the exponential dependence of generation rate on resource cost. In this work, we study photonic graph state synthesis as a cost-aware decomposition problem, exploiting local Clifford (LC) equivalence to identify more synthesis-friendly representations of the target graph state before decomposition. Specifically, we propose Cost-aware Fusion-based Decomposition (CFD), a three-stage heuristic framework that decomposes a target graph state into ring, star, and linear motifs, and assembles them via Type-I fusion operations to minimize fusion overhead and physical-qubit consumption. We further show that selecting the LC-equivalent graph state with the minimum number of edges provides a highly effective proxy for near-optimal synthesis: in many cases it matches the best generation rate observed within the LC equivalence class under CFD, and in most remaining cases it remains close to it. Numerical evaluations on graph state orbit data and 2D and 3D lattice graph states demonstrate that CFD achieves up to 84.6\% reduction in resource overhead compared to baseline constructions, and yields improvements in photonic generation rate spanning multiple orders of magnitude. These results suggest that combining structure-aware motif decomposition with LC equivalence is a practical and scalable strategy for photonic graph state synthesis.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>
