**Summary.** This theoretical work models high-Tc superconductors using an SU(2) gauge theory applied to the Hubbard model, separating electrons into chargon and spinon components. By combining DMFT with spinon fluctuations, the authors show that magnetic correlations are essential for explaining the characteristic pseudogap physics, specifically the formation of asymmetric Fermi arcs in the underdoped state.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed treatment of gauge symmetry and the explicit inclusion of spinon dynamics provide a sophisticated theoretical mechanism for understanding spectral features (like Fermi arcs) that are hallmarks of strongly correlated quantum materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the pseudogap phenomenon in high-temperature superconducting cuprates, aiming to explain the spectral reconstruction and the formation of Fermi arcs.

**Main result.** The inclusion of long-wavelength magnetic fluctuations beyond standard DMFT is crucial, as it generates an asymmetry in the damping that successfully describes the formation of Fermi arcs in the underdoped regime.

**Method.** The authors employ an SU(2) gauge theory framework applied to the Hubbard model, utilizing Dynamic Mean-Field Theory (DMFT) for the chargon subsystem supplemented by spinon fluctuations.

**Model / system.** The system is the two-dimensional Hubbard model describing high-Tc superconductors, which is theoretically mapped onto a fractionalized description involving chargons and spinons within an SU(2) gauge theory.

**Key observables.** Spectral weight, damping asymmetry, Fermi arcs (observed in ARPES), and small, closed Fermi surface pockets (from quantum oscillation measurements).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Low hole doping regime, temperature ($T=0.1t, 0.2t$), and the coupling strength $U$ relative to the hopping $t$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The approach assumes the physical state can be mapped onto chargons with long-ranged magnetic order and small fluctuations of the gauge field $R_x$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate chargon spectral functions and pseudogap formation as doping changes, showing the transition from a hole pocket to an arc-like structure due to correlation effects.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the theoretical framework (SU(2) gauge theory/Hubbard model) to applying DMFT supplemented by spinon fluctuations, culminating in the demonstration that these correlations are necessary to explain the observed asymmetric spectral weight transfer leading to Fermi arcs.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider the spectral properties of the two-dimensional Hubbard model, describing the electronic properties of high-$T_c$ compounds, within the SU(2) gauge theory, which assumes the separation of electronic degrees of freedom into those of spinon and chargon subsystems. We use the dynamic mean-field theory (DMFT) approach to describe magnetic long-range order in the chargon subsystem while also treating spinon fluctuations on top of this state. We show that DMFT supplemented by long-wavelength magnetic fluctuations is essential for describing the asymmetry in the damping between the inner and outer regions of the hole pockets and the resulting formation of Fermi arcs in the underdoped regime, especially at low hole doping. The underlying hole pockets in the chargon subsystem can be associated with those observed in quantum oscillation measurements.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces Fracton Topological Holography (FTH), an extension of Topological Holography, to study quantum systems with fracton excitations. It establishes a rigorous four-stage framework to analyze how boundary conditions induce dualities in both type-I and type-II fracton models. This offers a systematic route to understanding duality in complex topological phases of matter.

**Why it may be interesting.** The systematic development of FTH provides a powerful, general mathematical tool to analyze duality in complex, geometrically constrained quantum phases, which is highly relevant for understanding exotic correlated states.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To extend the concept of Topological Holography (TH) to systems exhibiting fracton topological orders, leading to Fracton Topological Holography (FTH).

**Main result.** FTH provides a concrete framework for organizing and understanding duality, successfully extending TH to both type-I (X-cube) and type-II (Haah's cubic code) fracton orders.

**Method.** The authors developed a systematic four-stage framework involving preparing the bulk model, determining boundary data, identifying low-energy preserving operator algebras, and switching between top boundaries to induce duality.

**Model / system.** The study uses abstract topological models like the X-cube model (type-I) and Haah's cubic code (type-II) as examples of fracton phases, analyzed using stabilizer formalisms.

**Key observables.** The low-energy effective Hamiltonian ($	ilde{H}$) on the bottom boundary, and the resulting duality mapping between different top boundary conditions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The type of fracton order (I or II), and the boundary conditions (smooth vs. rough for X-cube; Z vs. X for Haah's code).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework assumes the existence of a systematic four-stage procedure for constructing the holographic sandwich, and that the low-energy dynamics can be captured by boundary operators.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the FTH framework, showing the holographic sandwich structure and specific operator interchanges/dualities for the X-cube and Haah's code.

**Paper structure.** The paper is structured around the general four-stage FTH framework, applying it first to the X-cube model to demonstrate a plaquette Ising Kramers-Wannier self-duality, and then to Haah's code to test its applicability to type-II fracton orders.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Topological holography (TH), or SymTFT, realizes symmetries and dualities of a quantum system as boundary data of a topological bulk in one higher dimension. We formulate fracton topological holography (FTH), extending this mechanism from liquid topological orders to fracton stabilizer codes. The construction is organized as a general four-stage framework: prepare the bulk model and compute its excitations, determine boundary data and admissible gapped top boundaries, identify the low-energy preserving operator algebra together with its symmetry, relation, and twist data, and then switch among top boundaries to compare the induced boundary descriptions. As a type-I example, we develop FTH for the X-cube model with smooth and rough top boundaries; for a minimal effective Hamiltonian, both yield transverse-field plaquette Ising models, with exchanged subsystem symmetry and twist data, and the boundary switch is implemented by a linear-depth local unitary sequential quantum circuit (SQC). As a type-II example, we formulate FTH for Haah's cubic code in the Laurent-polynomial stabilizer formalism and analyze the natural $(Z)$ and $(X)$ top boundaries, which induce two two-dimensional qubit systems related locally by exchanging generalized plaquette Ising and transverse-field terms and nonlocally by a symmetry--relation duality. These results show that FTH is a genuine extension of TH to both type-I and type-II fracton orders. FTH therefore provides a concrete framework for organizing and understanding duality, with the prospect of offering a systematic route to new dualities.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a robust graph-theoretic method to classify the generation sequence of cracks in hierarchical crack patterns. It models the pattern as a DAG and uses a leaf-removal algorithm, which successfully reduces the classification problem to topological sorting. The resulting method is shown to be highly stable against minor changes in the analyzed image boundaries.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the topic is materials science/image analysis, the methodology—reducing a complex structural classification problem to a graph-theoretic sorting problem—shares conceptual parallels with analyzing connectivity or structure in complex quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the problem of classifying the generation order of cracks within a Hierarchical Crack Pattern (HCP) image fragment.

**Main result.** The proposed method successfully reduces the classification to topological sorting, yielding a robust generation assignment that is stable even when the image boundaries shift.

**Method.** The core method involves defining cracks as chains of edges near 180 degrees and constructing a Directed Acyclic Graph (DAG) where arcs point from child to parent at T-junctions. Generation is then determined by iteratively removing leaves (vertices with zero in-degree) until all vertices are processed.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the Hierarchical Crack Pattern (HCP) found in various materials, which is digitized and modeled as a graph structure. The analysis assumes the graph is connected, has vertex degrees $\le 3$, and contains no loops.

**Key observables.** Crack generations, represented by the topological ordering of the DAG vertices.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The acceptable angular deviation for defining a crack ($\le 30^\circ$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The network must be strictly hierarchical, allowing for a clear child-parent relationship, and the graph must satisfy degree $\le 3$ and no loops.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the construction of the graph and DAG, showing the step-by-step processing of the DAG using the proposed leaf-removal algorithm, and comparing results against older methods under boundary shifts.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the problem and the concept of crack definition. It then details the DAG construction and the leaf-removal algorithm for topological sorting. Finally, it presents results demonstrating the method's robustness compared to existing techniques.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Hierarchical crack patterns of various origins are ubiquitous in the world around us. We reduce the problem of classifying crack generations in an image of a part of the entire hierarchical crack pattern to the well-known topological sorting of a directed acyclic graph. The classification demonstrates robustness to reasonable shifts in the pattern image boundaries.

</details>

### [Machine-Learning Prediction of Quantum Fisher Information from Collective Spin and Spectral Features](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02986v1)

**Authors:** Yusef Maleki, Luis D. Zambrano Palma

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the Quantum Fisher Information (QFI), a key metric in quantum metrology, can be accurately predicted using machine learning (SVR) trained on low-order collective spin and spectral moments. The results show that while simple spin moments are insufficient, combining these with spectral quantities like purity significantly enhances predictive power, offering a pathway to estimate quantum sensitivity without full quantum state tomography.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, data-driven methodology for characterizing metrological resources in complex quantum systems, suggesting practical ways to estimate quantum limits using limited experimental measurements.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is predicting the Quantum Fisher Information (QFI) for multipartite quantum systems without performing computationally intensive full quantum-state tomography.

**Main result.** QFI can be accurately predicted using machine learning models trained on a reduced set of physically accessible quantities, specifically the interplay between collective covariance and low-order spectral moments.

**Method.** Support Vector Regression (SVR) with an RBF kernel is employed to learn the nonlinear mapping between a set of low-order collective spin and spectral observables and the target QFI.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on multipartite quantum systems, ranging from two to five qubits, analyzed using random pure, mixed, and hybrid quantum states.

**Key observables.** Quantum Fisher Information (QFI), collective spin moments ($\langle\hat{J}_iangle$), second-order moments ($\langle\hat{J}_i^2angle$), symmetrized correlators ($\langle\hat{J}_i\hat{J}_j + \hat{J}_j\hat{J}_iangle$), Purity ($	ext{Tr}(ho^2)$), and the third spectral moment ($	ext{Tr}(ho^3)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size (number of qubits, $N=2$ to $5$), and the specific structure of the quantum state (pure, mixed, hybrid).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the metrological sensitivity (QFI) is governed by a compact, low-dimensional set of experimentally measurable observables, bypassing the need for full state reconstruction.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare exact QFI values against SVR predictions using different feature sets (e.g., spin moments only vs. spin moments + purity + $	ext{Tr}(ho^3)$), showing increasing accuracy with the inclusion of higher-order spectral terms. One figure tracks performance degradation as the number of qubits increases.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces QFI, details the SVR methodology, and then systematically tests the predictive power by comparing feature sets: first only spin moments, then adding purity ($	ext{Tr}(ho^2)$), and finally adding the cubic spectral moment ($	ext{Tr}(ho^3)$), analyzing results for both two-qubit and larger multipartite systems.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum Fisher information (QFI) is a fundamental quantifier in quantum metrology, determining the ultimate precision achievable in parameter-estimation protocols through the quantum Cramér-Rao bound. However, direct evaluation of the QFI generally requires detailed knowledge of the density matrix, making it increasingly demanding as the Hilbert-space dimension grows. In this work, we investigate the extent to which the QFI of multipartite quantum systems can be predicted from a limited set of experimentally accessible quantities using support vector regression (SVR). By comparing different physically motivated features, we identify a dominant feature set governing QFI and show that the predictive power of collective spin moments alone decreases as system size and consequently Hilbert-space dimension grows. We demonstrate that QFI is governed primarily by the interplay between collective covariance and low-order spectral moments of the density matrix. Our results identify the physically relevant information sectors governing the QFI and demonstrate that accurate estimation of metrological sensitivity can be achieved from a restricted set of experimentally accessible quantities without requiring full quantum-state tomography.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03859"></a>
### [Subspace-selective unitary manipulation based on the Hilbert-space symmetric structures in the multiple-quantum operator algebra spaces in the quantum-computing speedup theory](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03859v1)

**Summary.** This paper develops a theoretical framework arguing that the multiple-quantum operator algebra space is the fundamental resource hub for quantum speedup. It proposes using subspace-selective unitary manipulation, implemented via specialized MQT operators, to explicitly incorporate quantum-state resources from the Hilbert space into this operator algebra space for enhanced quantum simulation and computation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a highly abstract, operator-algebraic framework for understanding quantum speedup, connecting concepts from quantum simulation (Hamiltonian decomposition) directly to the structure of quantum information processing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central problem is developing a method to effectively utilize fundamental quantum-computing-speedup resources, which are inherent in the symmetric structures of quantum systems, to accelerate quantum computation and simulation.

**Main result.** The authors propose that the multiple-quantum operator algebra space is the central hub for these resources, and subspace-selective unitary manipulation, using specialized MQT operators, is the key mechanism to bridge resources from the standard Hilbert space into this operator algebra space.

**Method.** The research employs theoretical analysis involving the construction and manipulation of multiple-quantum transition (MQT) operators and pseudo-diagonal Hermitian (PDH) operators to realize subspace-selective unitary transformations.

**Model / system.** The theory is developed for quantum spin systems (e.g., n spin-1/2 systems), analyzing the relationship between the Hilbert space (state space), the density operator space, and the multiple-quantum operator algebra space (operator space).

**Key observables.** The dimensional sizes of occupied direct-sum subspaces, and the action of the total spin operator on MQT product operators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The quantum-transition order 'p' and the coefficients defining the component Hamiltonians ($H_0, H_1, H_{02}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory assumes that the multiple-quantum operator algebra space is the correct framework for exploiting speedup resources, and that specific operator constructions (PDH, MQT) can bridge the gap between state and operator representations.

**Figures summary.** Not specified.

**Paper structure.** The paper is structured in parts: Part I investigates the multiple-quantum operator algebra spaces and their relationship to quantum simulation; Part II focuses on subspace-selective unitary manipulation, detailing the use of MQT operators and Hilbert-space-enlarging processes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The quantum-computing speedup theory considers the symmetric structures and properties of quantum systems as the fundamental Quantum-Computing-Speedup (QCS) resources which are responsible for exponentially speeding up quantum computing and simulating. At present a large and important problem is how to make use of the fundamental QCS resources to speed up essentially quantum computing and simulating. Here the author makes a great effort toward solving this important problem. The theoretical research work in this paper is mainly divided into the two Parts I and II. The Part I investigates mainly the multiple-quantum operator algebra spaces. And the relationships are analyzed among the multiple-quantum operator algebra spaces, quantum simulating for the unitary time-evolutional processes, and the fundamental QCS resources which exist in the different kinds of basic quantum spaces: the multiple-quantum operator algebra space, the density operator space, and the Hilbert space. It concludes that the multiple-quantum operator algebra space must be positioned as the central place where the QCS resources are exploited to speed up quantum computing and simulating. The Part II investigates mainly the subspace-selective unitary manipulation based on the Hilbert-space symmetric structures. Recognize that the multiple-quantum operator algebra space is the central place. Then those QCS resources original from the Hilbert space (a quantum-state space) must be explicitly taken into account in the multiple-quantum operator algebra space (a linear operator space). This is an important problem. The subspace-selective unitary manipulation is able to solve this problem. It aims to harness the fundamental QCS resources original from the Hilbert space to speed up quantum computing and simulating in the multiple-quantum operator algebra space.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper explores the quantum nature of spacetime causality by examining a scalar field propagating near a quantum source. It demonstrates that the source's quantum fluctuations induce an operator-valued shift in the light cones, making the causal structure inherently uncertain. This effect is calculated perturbatively and shows that quantum matter fundamentally alters the geometry of spacetime correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on gravity, the core mechanism—quantifying how quantum uncertainty in a source modifies fundamental geometric/causal structures—is highly relevant to open quantum systems where environmental coupling dictates observable dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how the causal structure of spacetime, defined by light cones, becomes quantum when the matter source generating the gravitational field is itself quantum mechanical.

**Main result.** Quantum matter makes the causal structure itself quantum, leading to non-commuting causal-boundary shifts and smearing the light-cone singularity, even without quantizing gravity.

**Method.** The analysis uses perturbative field-theoretic calculations in the weak-field limit, focusing on the operator-valued Shapiro time delay ($\hat{	au}_n$) for a massless scalar field.

**Model / system.** The system models a massless scalar field propagating in a spacetime perturbed by a weak Newtonian gravitational potential sourced by a non-relativistic quantum particle.

**Key observables.** Operator-valued Shapiro delay ($\hat{	au}_n$), noncommuting causal-boundary shifts, and the smearing of the Wightman light-cone singularity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Low-energy, non-relativistic limit; the gravitational coupling strength (related to $GM/b$); Planck scales ($\ell_{Pl}, t_{Pl}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a weak-field approximation and uses an instantaneous Newtonian potential, avoiding full quantum gravity treatment.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 schematically shows the effect of the weak potential on null rays. Figure 2 illustrates the quantum Shapiro delay ($\hat{	au}_n$) shift. Figure 4 shows the superposition of causal relations when the source is in a spatial superposition.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the problem by first establishing the classical Shapiro delay, then quantizing this delay into an operator ($\hat{	au}_n$). It analyzes the consequences of this operator on the commutator and Wightman function, demonstrating non-commutativity and smearing effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In gravitational physics, matter does not merely move within spacetime; it also determines the light cones that define causal relations. What happens when the matter that determines these light cones is itself in a quantum state? We address this question in a controlled low-energy setting: a massless scalar field propagating in the spacetime with the Newtonian gravitational potential sourced by a non-relativistic quantum particle. We show that the light cones are affected by an operator-valued Shapiro delay, with the three consequences: (i) causal-boundary shifts are promoted to noncommuting observables, giving the causal structure an irreducible quantum uncertainty; (ii) the causal relation between two fixed spacetime points can become a superposition of timelike and spacelike configurations; and (iii) tracing out the source smears the Wightman light-cone singularity, producing an effective UV cutoff. Thus, quantum matter does not merely fluctuate within spacetime; it makes the causal structure itself quantum, even without quantized gravitons.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03249"></a>
### [Quantum-Classical Equivalence for AND-Functions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03249v1)

**Authors:** Sreejata Kishor Bhattacharya, Farzan Byramji, Arkadev Chattopadhyay, Yogesh Dahiya, Shachar Lovett  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03249v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03249_figures/2606.03249_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: A semi-adaptive max-degree restriction tree for And3 ˝ Or2 with core variables V “ tx1, x2, x3u. Leaves correspond to the 2|V | restrictions in the tree. For each leaf restriction, the re- stricted function pAnd3 ˝ Or2q|ρ computes an And over exactly the variables left free.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work resolves a major open problem in quantum communication complexity by proving that for AND-functions, the quantum and classical deterministic communication complexities are polynomially equivalent. The authors achieve this by deeply connecting these complexity measures to the structural property of De Morgan sparsity of the function. This result significantly narrows the gap between quantum and classical computational power for this important class of functions.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical communication complexity, the techniques involving Fourier analysis, restriction/averaging procedures, and structural characterizations of functions are highly relevant to understanding the complexity of quantum algorithms and quantum information processing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the open problem of whether quantum protocols can provide an exponential advantage over classical protocols for computing general total Boolean functions.

**Main result.** It proves that for the specific class of AND-functions, the bounded-error quantum and classical deterministic communication complexities are polynomially related, up to polylogarithmic factors.

**Method.** The proof relies on structural characterizations of Boolean functions, relating communication complexity bounds to the function's De Morgan sparsity and using advanced Fourier analysis techniques on random restrictions.

**Model / system.** The model is the two-party communication complexity setting (Alice and Bob) for computing total Boolean functions, specifically focusing on functions composed with the AND-2 gadget ($f \circ 	ext{AND}_2$).

**Key observables.** Deterministic communication complexity ($D_{ccp}$), bounded-error quantum communication complexity ($Q_{ccp}$), and the De Morgan sparsity ($	ext{sparf}$) of the outer function $f$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The polynomial relationship holds up to polylogarithmic factors in $n$, and the lower bounds are derived from the sparsity $s$ of the function $f$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The equivalence is proven for AND-functions, not for all total Boolean functions (the general Log-Equivalence Conjecture remains open).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the construction of semi-adaptive max-degree restriction trees for specific functions like $	ext{And}^3 \oplus 	ext{Or}^2$ and $	ext{Orn}$, demonstrating the structural machinery used in the proof.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the general framework of communication complexity, then developing tools based on sparsity and restriction trees, and finally applying these tools to prove the polynomial equivalence for the AND-function class.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A major open problem in quantum communication complexity is whether quantum protocols can be exponentially more efficient than classical protocols for computing total Boolean functions; the prevailing conjecture is that they cannot be so. In a seminal work, Razborov (2002) resolved this question for AND-functions of the form $$ F(x,y) = f(x_1 \land y_1, \ldots, x_n \land y_n), $$ when the outer function $f$ is symmetric, by proving that their bounded-error quantum and classical communication complexities are polynomially related. Since then, extending this result to all AND-functions has remained open and has been posed by several authors.   In this work, we settle this problem in a strong way. We show that for every Boolean function $f$, the bounded-error quantum and classical deterministic communication complexities of the function $f \circ \mathrm{AND}_2$ are polynomially related, up to polylogarithmic factors in $n$. We prove this by showing that both are characterized--up to polynomial loss--by the logarithm of the De Morgan sparsity of $f$.   Our results build on the recent work of Chattopadhyay, Dahiya, and Lovett (2025) on structural characterizations of non-sparse Boolean functions, which we extend to resolve the conjecture for general AND-functions.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.02785"></a>
### [QUIVER: Quantum-Informed Views for Enhanced Representations in Large ML Models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02785v1)

**Authors:** Aritra Bal, Michael Binder, Markus Klute, Benedikt Maier, Michael Spannowsky  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02785v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.02785_figures/2606.02785_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Validation MAE during training for the DimeNet++ baseline and the quantum-inspired QDimeNet++ model. Solid lines show the mean across N = 10 paired seeds (identical data splits and initialization RNG state across the two models; only the architecture differs), smoothed with a 3-epoch rolling window. Shaded bands denote ±1 sample standard deviation across seeds (calculated using standard library functions with ddof=1). The lower panel shows the per-epoch paired difference ∆MAE = MAEDimeNet++ −MAEQDimeNet++ with the correspond- ing ±1σ band over the same paired seeds; positive values indicate lower MAE for QDimeNet++.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces QUIVER, a method that enriches classical machine learning features by incorporating a 'quantum Fisher view' derived from variational quantum circuits. It demonstrates that this quantum-geometric information provides a genuinely complementary signal, leading to measurable performance gains in diverse fields like quantum chemistry and particle physics. The work shows that quantum insights can boost ML models even without fault-tolerant quantum hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the application is ML-focused, the reliance on the QFIM—a fundamental measure of quantum distinguishability—provides a novel, information-theoretic lens for feature engineering that connects quantum geometry to classical data science.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses how to enhance the representational power of large machine learning models by integrating information derived from quantum mechanics.

**Main result.** The proposed QUIVER framework successfully improves performance on both molecular property prediction (QM9) and high-energy physics jet classification tasks.

**Method.** It introduces the quantum Fisher view, derived from a Variational Quantum Circuit (VQC), to augment classical feature vectors, treating this quantum-geometric information as a complementary modality.

**Model / system.** The methods are applied to two distinct domains: predicting molecular properties (QM9) and classifying particle jets at the LHC. The core mathematical tool is the Quantum Fisher Information Matrix (QFIM), which characterizes the geometry of the quantum state manifold.

**Key observables.** For jet tagging, the Area Under the Curve (AUC) score and QCD background rejection rate are key; for molecular regression, the Mean Absolute Error (MAE) of the HOMO-LUMO gap is used.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The study uses restricted qubit counts (e.g., up to 10 qubits) for encoding, and the performance is benchmarked across varying dataset sizes (e.g., 0.1M to 5M jets).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The quantum view is assumed to provide genuinely complementary information that classical feature augmentation alone cannot capture, and the QFIM calculation is tractable on classical simulators.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the performance (e.g., MAE curves for molecular regression) of the QUIVER-augmented models against their classical baselines, showing consistent improvements.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the need for multimodal inputs, introduces QUIVER by defining the quantum Fisher view via VQCs, and then demonstrates its efficacy by applying the framework to two distinct benchmarks: QM9 and LHC jet tagging.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Large machine learning models benefit substantially from multimodal inputs that provide a complementary view of the same example. We introduce QUIVER (QUantum-Informed Views for Enhanced Representations, a paradigm that enriches classical data-driven features with a quantum Fisher view: a geometrically motivated, basis-independent summary of higher-order correlations captured by a variational quantum circuit (VQC) trained to perform the same task. Unlike classical feature augmentation, the quantum Fisher information matrix encodes the intrinsic geometry of the learned quantum state manifold. While this feature map, motivated by quantum information theory, is ordinarily non-trivial to model classically, it can surface statistical structure that additional classical data or model capacity finds difficult to learn. This makes the quantum Fisher view a genuinely complementary modality rather than a redundant one. We demonstrate that QUIVER improves standard performance metrics on two benchmark datasets from very different fields: QM9 for predicting molecule properties, and JetClass for predicting jet flavor at the Large Hadron Collider (LHC). The core contribution, however, is domain-agnostic: the quantum Fisher view can be fused into a broad class of model architectures via targeted modifications to the base architecture, to incorporate information about the quantum geometry of the problem. These results demonstrate that quantum-geometric features, extracted from simulated variational circuits, can deliver measurable value for standard machine learning tasks, well before the advent of fault-tolerant quantum hardware.

</details>

### [Relaxation of the Random Site Coulomb glass Model in Two Dimensions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02999v1)

**Authors:** Preeti Bhandari, Vikas Malik

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical study analyzes the relaxation dynamics of a 2D random site Coulomb glass model, showing that relaxation slows down due to the depletion of the density of states near the Fermi level. The relaxation time is found to be highly sensitive to system density and disorder strength. The work establishes key scaling laws governing the transition from initial rapid decay to long-time power-law or exponential decay.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides detailed insights into slow relaxation dynamics in disordered interacting electron systems, drawing parallels between Coulomb glass physics and general disordered many-body relaxation phenomena relevant to open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the relaxation dynamics within a two-dimensional random site Coulomb glass model, focusing on how material density, onsite energy disorder, and localization length influence the relaxation process.

**Main result.** Slow relaxation is governed by the depletion of the single-particle density of states near the Fermi level, and the relaxation time is highly sensitive to changes in disorder and localization length.

**Method.** The analysis uses mean-field approximations to calculate the eigenvalue distribution of the linear dynamical matrix, modeling the system's time evolution via single-particle transitions.

**Model / system.** The system is the Random Site Coulomb glass (CG) model in 2D, described by a Hamiltonian involving random site energies ($\phi_i$) and Coulomb interactions. Dynamics are governed by a master equation tracking the probability of site occupations.

**Key observables.** Relaxation dynamics (initial rapid $ightarrow$ slow decay), eigenvalue distribution of the dynamical matrix, single-particle density of states (DOS) near the Fermi level, and the relaxation time ($	au$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Material density ($n$), onsite energy disorder ($W$), and localization length ($a$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on mean-field approximations and models the dynamics assuming single-particle transitions are dominant.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the probability density of nearest neighbor distances and interaction strengths, the DOS for single-particle energies at various densities, and the distribution of the logarithm of the eigenvalues.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the relaxation process by first establishing the theoretical framework using mean-field theory, then examining the dependence of relaxation rates on varying site density, and finally discussing the roles of disorder and localization length.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This study investigates the influence of material density, disorder in onsite energies, and localization length on relaxation dynamics within a two-dimensional random site Coulomb glass model at half-filling. To explore relaxation laws, we calculate the eigenvalue distribution of the linear dynamical matrix using mean-field approximations. Our findings indicate that the system initially undergoes rapid relaxation through energy-lowering transitions. The depletion of the single-particle density of states (DOS) near the Fermi level leads to slow relaxation, with fluctuations diminishing according to a power law. Subsequently, the system adheres to an exponential decay law after a specific period, defined as the relaxation time, which is inversely related to the minimum eigenvalue of the dynamical matrix. As the density of the system decreases, the relaxation rate slows down, resulting in an increase in the relaxation time. For a constant density and localization length, an increase in the disorder of onsite energies results in a longer relaxation time. A significant portion of the eigenvalue spectrum remains unaffected, suggesting that a reduction in localization length concurrent with increased disorder may play an equally vital role in the slow dynamics observed.

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<sub>FIG. 4. Reducing Computational Complexity through Symmetry. A direct SDP formulation of conventional n- to-1 purification (see Eq. **Summary.** This work develops a dynamical, spatiotemporal framework to redefine quantum state purification, moving beyond static post-processing methods. By incorporating pre-processing steps, the authors demonstrate 'forward-assisted' purification protocols that significantly outperform conventional methods. This advancement allows for the purification of states previously considered impossible due to fundamental no-purification theorems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The concept of distributing interventions across the noise process (pre-processing) provides a powerful, resource-efficient mechanism to enhance quantum information processing beyond what is achievable by simply averaging over many noisy copies.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Conventional quantum state purification is limited because it is inherently static, acting only after noise has degraded the quantum resource.

**Main result.** Introducing a dynamical, spatiotemporal framework allows for 'forward-assisted' purification, which can achieve performance exceeding conventional multi-copy protocols using fewer resources.

**Method.** The authors recast purification as a dynamical process using a superchannel framework, incorporating pre-processing ($	heta_{Pre}$), noise, and post-processing ($	heta_{Post}$) linked by a quantum memory.

**Model / system.** The study analyzes the purification of quantum states, particularly Bell states, subjected to various noise models like amplitude damping or local depolarizing noise.

**Key observables.** Achievable fidelity ($F^*$), performance advantage ($\Delta$), and the required sample complexity (number of noisy copies).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Noise parameters ($p, q$), the number of copies ($n$), and pre-processing parameters (local unitary angles $\alpha, eta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on extending the operational scope beyond static, post-processing-only protocols, and sometimes assumes specific noise models (e.g., depolarizing noise for Bell states).

**Figures summary.** Figures compare single-copy FA protocols against multi-copy conventional protocols, showing performance advantages and demonstrating the violation of no-purification theorems.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the limitations of static purification, then introducing the dynamical spatiotemporal framework, demonstrating operational advantages via single-copy vs. multi-copy comparisons, and finally showing how pre-processing breaks fundamental no-purification theorems.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Noise remains the primary obstacle to realizing quantum advantage, continuously degrading the resources that enable quantum technologies. Purification aims to reverse this degradation by extracting high-fidelity resources from noisy ensembles, yet its conventional formulation is intrinsically static, acting only after noise has taken effect. Here we instead recast purification as a dynamical task, introducing a spatiotemporal framework that distributes interventions across the noise process. This formulation reveals operational capabilities inaccessible to existing approaches and gives rise to forward-assisted purifications that extend achievable performance. In certain regimes, a single-copy protocol already exceeds what can be achieved with up to 50 copies under conventional purification, demonstrating a significant overhead in required resources. Beyond these gains, our framework circumvents no-purification theorems within conventional protocols, including for Bell-state ensembles, thereby enabling purification previously considered impossible and pointing toward an efficient route to mitigating noise in quantum systems.

</details>

**Summary.** This work proposes using enriched network theory to design metamaterials by systematically incorporating controlled irregularities, moving beyond simple periodic designs. It develops mathematical models that couple topology with geometry and physical constraints, utilizing machine learning tools like GNNs to enable inverse design. The key takeaway is that controlled disorder is essential for unlocking novel, enhanced material properties.

**Why it may be interesting.** The focus on structure-property relationships in disordered, non-equilibrium systems, and the use of ML to navigate complex parameter spaces, connects directly to understanding emergent behavior in complex quantum many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is designing metamaterials with controlled irregularities to achieve enhanced or novel properties, moving beyond the tractability limits of purely regular structures.

**Main result.** Irregular metamaterials can exhibit substantial property enhancements compared to periodic designs, and network theory provides a natural framework to systematically design and analyze these complex structures.

**Method.** The work integrates enriched network theory, graph neural networks (GNNs), and geometric modeling to map structure-property relationships in disordered systems.

**Model / system.** The system studied is metamaterials, modeled as physical networks (granular, truss, plate) whose structure is defined by nodes and edges embedded in space. The irregularity can be topological, geometrical, or parametric.

**Key observables.** Material properties (rigidity, negative Poisson's ratio), network metrics (percolation threshold, average degree $\langle k angle$), and irregularity quantifiers (Entropy, Variance).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The decay exponent $\alpha$ in the geometric network model, the strain $\sigma$, and the node/edge radii $d$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes simplified network structures (e.g., tree-like for percolation) or relies on machine learning to handle the complexity of the search space.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the description of various irregular materials (granular, truss, plate) by a common network structure, and show how network metrics like average degree and edge length change with the geometric decay exponent $\alpha$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing network theory as the necessary framework, to developing geometric extensions (like the geometric Erdős–Rényi model), and finally applying advanced computational tools like GNNs and physical network learning for inverse design.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Metamaterials can achieve exceptional functionality through careful engineering of their mesoscale structure. Although appropriately introduced irregularities can be advantageous, current approaches largely conform to regular structures to preserve tractability. Here, we contend that network theory, enriched with geometry and physics, provides a natural framework for designing metamaterials with controlled irregularities at relevant scales, thereby enabling the discovery of new property-enhancing structures. We examine how this augmented network theory can facilitate the creation of irregular metamaterials with enhanced or novel properties and how metamaterial research, in turn, is opening new directions in network science. Supported by machine learning and advanced self-assembly, the emerging field of irregular metamaterial networks is poised to transform inverse design and scalable manufacturing of novel materials.

</details>

### [Constraint-Enhanced Physical Search through Correlation Matching](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03554v1)

**Authors:** Song-Ju Kim  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03554v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03554_figures/2606.03554_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: Minimal TOW bandit model. A two-armed bandit trial consists of choosing an arm at ∈{A, B}, observing a binary reward Rt ∈{0, 1}, and updating the internal TOW values QA and QB. For κ = 1, the update gives exact TOW conservation QA + QB = const. The differential evidence Zt = QA −QB is compared with a zero choice boundary after modulation by the temporally correlated drive σst. Reward feedback defines the update increment dt, and the TOW rule updates the chosen arm and the competing arm in opposite directions. Thus a local reward observation is converted into an option-wise anti-correlated update of the internal state.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03554_figures/2606.03554_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Efficiency gain and synthesis diagnostic for temporal anti-correlation combined with TOW conservation. (a) Combined efficiency Eχκ = R00/Rχκ as a function of the temporal correlation parameter λ for the representative setting PA = 0.7, PB = 0.5, σ = 1.3, η = 0.5, and κ = 1. Values above unity indicate reduced regret relative to the uncorrelated one- sided baseline. The high-statistics data show a robust efficiency gain over the tested range of negative temporal correlations. Error bars show propagated standard errors obtained from the standard errors of the regret estimates. (b) Synthesis coefficient Λ, which isolates the non-additive interaction between temporal anti-correlation...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03554_figures/2606.03554_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Internal mechanism behind the optimum. (a) Temporal anti-correlation improves early exploration balance during the phase in which the drive can still affect the decision boundary. (b) The variance of the differential update obeys the expected TOW scaling Var(∆Z) ∝η2, confirming the update-noise cost in Eq. (17). (c) Increasing η also increases premature or wrong locking, showing why the transduction from exploration to evidence formation has a finite window rather than growing monotonically with update strength.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03554_figures/2606.03554_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Parameter sweeps supporting the scaling interpretation. (a) Changing the reward contrast ∆P at fixed ω0 = 1.5 reshapes the efficiency landscape over temporal correlations. (b) Changing the non-reward penalty ω0 at fixed ∆P = 0.20 also modifies the efficiency landscape. (c,d) Extracted optima |λ∗| plotted against the dimensionless update-noise ratio N = η(1 + κ)(1 + ω0)/∆P. Here N is the update-noise-to-reward-contrast ratio: large N means that the physical update scale is large relative to the reward difference that must be resolved. Because the optima are extracted from a finite λ-grid and the efficiency landscape can be broad near its maximum, the lower panels are used as...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a unifying principle for efficient physical search by showing that optimal exploration requires matching the time scale of random fluctuations to the spatial scale imposed by physical constraints. Using a minimal Tug-of-War bandit model, the authors demonstrate that the synergy between temporal anti-correlation and conservation-induced updates drives efficiency, limited by the update-noise-to-contrast ratio.

**Why it may be interesting.** The concept of matching temporal fluctuations to physical constraints for optimal information gathering has parallels in understanding how quantum measurements or environmental coupling can guide quantum computation or state preparation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a general principle for efficient physical search by matching temporal correlations in exploration to constraint-induced spatial correlations in update dynamics.

**Main result.** Efficient search emerges when the temporal correlation structure is tuned to match the physical update scale, quantified by a scaling estimate involving the update-noise-to-contrast ratio.

**Method.** The authors analyze the problem using a minimal Tug-of-War (TOW) bandit model, deriving efficiency gains and interaction diagnostics ($\Lambda$) by comparing correlated versus uncorrelated sampling.

**Model / system.** The core model is the minimal Tug-of-War (TOW) bandit, which compares two alternatives (A and B) and features a conservation-induced, anti-correlated update mechanism. The dynamics are governed by a correlated exploratory drive ($\sigma_t$) and the TOW update rule.

**Key observables.** Efficiency gain ($E_{\chi\kappa}$), Synthesis Coefficient ($\Lambda$), and the optimal temporal correlation ($\lambda^*$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The update-noise-to-contrast ratio ($N$), the temporal non-redundancy proxy ($\chi$), and the conservation strength ($\kappa$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a stationary environment and treats the TOW dynamics as a controllable reduced system, focusing on the interaction between the two types of correlations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the TOW model cycle, show efficiency gain vs. correlation strength ($\lambda$), and use the Synthesis coefficient ($\Lambda$) to diagnose constructive interaction between temporal and spatial correlations.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the physical search problem, models it using the TOW bandit, develops diagnostics ($\Lambda$) to quantify correlation synergy, derives scaling laws based on balancing exploration gain against update noise cost, and concludes with a general principle for efficient physical exploration.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Physical systems do not merely add noise to search processes; they impose constraints that generate structured correlations. We propose a principle of constraint-enhanced physical search in which temporal correlations in exploration are matched to constraint-induced spatial correlations in the update dynamics. Using a minimal tug-of-war bandit model (TOW), we show that a conservation law converts local observations into differential evidence across alternatives, while a temporally correlated drive controls the order of exploration. Search efficiency is improved not by stronger randomness or by maximal anti-correlation, but by matching the temporal correlation to the physical update scale that converts feedback into evidence. A scaling estimate identifies the update-noise-to-contrast ratio as the leading parameter that limits how strongly temporal anti-correlation can be used. The results suggest a general organizing principle for physical search: constraints and fluctuations can generate structured spatiotemporal correlations, and efficient exploration emerges when these correlations are matched to the update dynamics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03139"></a>
### [Turbulence: An Entropic Approach](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03139v1)

**Authors:** Christian Beck, Constantino Tsallis  
**Type:** both · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03139v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03139_figures/2606.03139_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Turbulent velocity distributions as measured in [15] and a fit by the stretched q-exponentials, theoretically derived in the current paper from a maximum entropy principle for Sq,δ.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03139_figures/2606.03139_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. qss as a function of r/η and Re as measured in [15]. δ is given by δ = (2 −qss)−1. The different sym- bols represent 4 different Reynolds numbers, namely Re = 69000, 133000, 266000, 540000 (from left to right).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03139_figures/2606.03139_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. The asymptotic power-law decay rate exponent w of the velocity distributions as a function of r/η for various Reynolds numbers, as measured in [15]. A scaling law is ob- served which, if extrapolated to r = η, gives w = 4 at the Kolmogorov scale r = η.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work develops a generalized entropic statistical mechanics approach to model turbulence, using the functional $S_{q,\delta}$. By applying this theory to experimental data from turbulent flow, the authors successfully predict the scale-dependent statistics of velocity differences. The framework draws deep analogies between the dissipation scale of turbulence and the physics of black hole event horizons.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical fluid dynamics, the use of generalized entropy functionals ($S_q$) and the connection to non-extensive statistical mechanics provides a powerful, transferable mathematical tool for analyzing complex, non-equilibrium systems, which has parallels in open quantum systems dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of describing the scale-dependent, non-Gaussian statistics of velocity differences in highly turbulent fluid flows.

**Main result.** The authors successfully apply a generalized entropic formalism, predicting that at the Kolmogorov scale, the system reaches a state analogous to a black hole event horizon where third moments vanish.

**Method.** The methodology involves maximizing a generalized entropic functional, $S_{q,\delta}$, subject to kinetic energy constraints, leading to generalized canonical distributions.

**Model / system.** The primary physical system studied is hydrodynamic turbulence, specifically using high-precision experimental data from Taylor-Couette flow. The theoretical framework generalizes statistical mechanics using nonadditive entropic functionals.

**Key observables.** Probability Density Functions (PDFs) of velocity differences, the asymptotic power-law decay rate exponent $w(r)$, and the existence/non-existence of higher moments.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The entropic parameters $q$ and $\delta$, the Kolmogorov length scale $\eta$, and the Reynolds number $	ext{Re}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The core assumption is that the system's statistics can be accurately described by maximizing the generalized entropic functional $S_{q,\delta}$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the successful fitting of measured turbulent velocity distributions using stretched $q$-exponentials, showing scaling laws that converge to specific values at large and small scales.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the generalized entropic formalism, derives the resulting canonical distributions, applies this theory to match experimental data from Taylor-Couette flow across different scales, and concludes by drawing analogies between the turbulent endpoint and black hole physics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that maximizing the generalized entropic functional $S_{q,δ}$ subject to standard kinetic energy constraints provides generalized canonical distributions that agree perfectly with measured probability densities of velocity differences at distance $r$ in highly-turbulent Taylor-Couette flow. The end point of the turbulent cascade is described by $δ=\frac{3}{2}$, a parameter value that also plays an important role in black-hole physics. At this point the Kolmogorov length scale $r=η$ is reached and all observable eddy structures of the turbulent flow disappear, in certain analogy to what is observed for black holes at the event horizon. Our approach generalizes statistical mechanics to more general nonadditive entropic functionals $S_{q,δ}$ such that it is applicable to turbulent flows. This approach asymptotically generates stretched $q$-exponentials as generalized canonical distributions relevant for turbulent flow, with a particular dependence of the stretching exponent $δ^{-1}$ on $q$ that follows from the well-known escort formalism in nonextensive statistical mechanics. Along this particular line in the parameter space, the physics can be described by $S_q$ on its own with suitable escort constraints, leading to the prediction $δ^{-1} (r) =2-q(r)$, thus allowing for a consistent thermodynamic description since $S_q$ is both trace-form and composable. We show that the above theoretically derived relation is well satisfied by measured high-precision experimental data for Taylor-Couette flow. At the Kolmogorov length scale $r=η$, the endpoint of our scenario, one has $δ=\frac{3}{2}$ and at this point the third moment of velocity differences ceases to exist and all eddies disappear. We point out various analogies with thermodynamic entropic approaches to black hole physics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Two-orbital $e_g$ model with bond-dependent spin-orbit coupling: A playground for emergent band topology, Kitaev magnetism, and magnetoelectricity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02716v1)

**Summary.** This work develops a theoretical model for NiX2 compounds using a two-orbital e_g Hamiltonian that includes bond-dependent spin-orbit coupling. It demonstrates that this model hosts rich physics, including topological band structures and Kitaev magnetism in the strongly correlated limit. The research extends the understanding of magnetoelectric effects by incorporating orbital-dependent spin currents.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit derivation of magnetoelectric coupling terms beyond single-orbital models, incorporating orbital degrees of freedom, provides a rich theoretical framework for understanding charge-spin coupling in correlated materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates emergent phenomena, including band topology, Kitaev magnetism, and magnetoelectricity, in layered transition metal compounds like NiX2.

**Main result.** The model predicts topological bands with non-trivial spin Chern numbers and reveals a spin-1 Hamiltonian exhibiting bond-dependent Kitaev interactions in the Mott limit.

**Method.** The study uses a two-orbital e_g model derived from DFT calculations, analyzing different limits (non-interacting, Mott insulator) and employing symmetry-based perturbation theory.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled by an effective two-orbital e_g Hamiltonian incorporating bond-dependent spin-orbit coupling (SOC) derived from ligand p-orbitals, relevant to NiX2 compounds.

**Key observables.** Spin-Chern numbers (Cs), higher-order topological indices, bond-dependent Kitaev exchange interactions, and spin-induced electric polarization (P).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ligand SOC strength (lambda), Coulomb repulsion (U), and various hopping parameters (t_1a, t_3b, etc.).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on mapping the full electronic structure onto a minimal two-orbital e_g model and using perturbation theory in the strong coupling limit.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase diagrams (SOC vs U), band structures showing topological edge/corner states, and magnetic phase diagrams (Luttinger-Tisza).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the effective two-orbital model via DFT/Wannierization, analyzing the non-interacting topological properties, deriving the effective spin Hamiltonian in the Mott limit, and finally calculating the generalized magnetoelectric response.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Inspired by the electronic structure of compounds like nickel dihalides Ni$X_2$ ($X$=Cl, Br, I), we propose a low-energy two-orbital $e_g$ model featuring bond-dependent spin-orbit terms, driven by atomic spin-orbit coupling on the ligand $X$. We demonstrate that this model hosts a rich array of phenomena. In the non-interacting band limit, spin-orbit-derived spin-dependent and spin-flip hopping terms produce topological bands with spin-Chern numbers $C_s=\pm 2, \pm 4$, and higher order topological states with fractional corner charges, respectively. In the half-filled Mott insulator limit, we recover a spin-$1$ Hamiltonian with bond-dependent Kitaev exchange interactions. We explore the magnetoelectric effect in this two-orbital model using symmetry-based perspective and microscopic calculations, going beyond the generalized Katsura-Nagaosa-Balatsky theory for the single-orbital case. Our work may be relevant to study of doping, strain, or pressure on Ni$X_2$ and related materials.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03109"></a>
### [Game, Set, Quantum: Parameterized Quantum Circuit for Correlated Equilibrium in Bayesian Games](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03109v1)

**Authors:** Param Pathak, Vidhi Oad, Nouhaila Innan, Adarsh Ganesan, Muhammad Shafique  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03109v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03109_figures/2606.03109_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Runtime memory of the LP reference solver as the player count increases. The dashed line marks the typical 8 GB system-memory limit. At n = 10, the solver reaches 10.2 GB before termination, reflecting the cost of explicit optimization over the type-action distribution.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03109_figures/2606.03109_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Overview of the quantum regret minimization workflow. The method enumerates type profiles, uses a PQC to sample action distributions, computes clipped regret with a negative entropy regularizer, and updates circuit parameters using parameter-shift gradients, gradient clipping, learning-rate annealing, entropy decay, and curriculum progression. The loop returns the learned strategy distribution σ∗(a | θ).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03109_figures/2606.03109_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Hybrid PQC architecture for approximate Bayes correlated equilibrium computation. The type register encodes private type profiles, while the action register samples joint action recommendations. Each layer applies type-conditioned rotations, local action rotations, and ring entangling blocks, yielding 3nL = O(nL) trainable parameters.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03109_figures/2606.03109_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Mean clipped regret of the quantum solver across player counts on the matched utility benchmark. The regret remains below 0.30 across all tested cases, with the lowest value observed at n = 2. The nonmonotonic value at n = 8 reflects the behavior of the specific curriculum run rather than a general scaling claim.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.03109_figures/2606.03109_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5: Mean clipped regret of MCCFR across player counts on the matched utility benchmark. MCCFR decreases from 0.481 at n = 2 to 0.323 at n = 10, but remains above the quantum solver and DCFR across the tested range.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes using a Parameterized Quantum Circuit (PQC) within a hybrid quantum-classical framework to approximate Bayes correlated equilibria in Bayesian games. By parameterizing the conditional strategy distribution, the method bypasses the exponential memory bottleneck of classical formulations. The results show that this quantum approach yields superior performance compared to established classical algorithms for moderate numbers of players.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the application is in game theory, the core methodology—using a PQC to compress an exponentially large state space into a polynomially parameterized variational form—is a powerful technique relevant to simulating complex quantum many-body states or high-dimensional quantum processes.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the computational challenge of finding equilibria in Bayesian games, where the explicit representation of the joint type-action space grows exponentially.

**Main result.** The proposed quantum solver achieves lower mean clipped regret than MCCFR across all tested player counts for up to eight players, demonstrating a viable variational representation for approximate equilibrium computation.

**Method.** A hybrid quantum-classical framework is used, employing a Parameterized Quantum Circuit (PQC) to represent the conditional strategy distribution, trained via gradient-based regret minimization.

**Model / system.** The model is a binary-type, binary-action Bayesian game, and the quantum computation is performed on a PQC operating on $2n$ qubits (type and action registers).

**Key observables.** Mean clipped regret, maximum positive deviation gain ($\epsilon_{\max}$), and memory usage scaling ($\mathcal{O}(2^{2n})$ vs $\mathcal{O}(nL)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of players ($n$), circuit depth ($L$), learning rate ($\eta_t$), and entropy regularization coefficient ($	au_t$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method is a variational approximation, meaning convergence to an exact equilibrium is not guaranteed unless the target distribution lies within the PQC's reachable family.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the mean clipped regret of the quantum solver, MCCFR, and DCFR across increasing player counts ($n$), showing the quantum solver's advantage up to $n=8$.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem, details the PQC architecture and the hybrid quantum-classical training workflow (including regret minimization and entropy regularization), presents comparative results against classical baselines, and discusses scaling and noise effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Strategic decision-making among many agents under incomplete information is central to economics, security, and multi-agent artificial intelligence (AI). Computing equilibria in such settings is challenging because the joint type-action space grows exponentially with the number of players. In binary-type, binary-action Bayesian games, an explicit representation over type-action profiles requires O(22n) entries, making direct linear-programming (LP) formulations memory intensive at moderate player counts. We propose a hybrid quantum-classical framework for approximating Bayes correlated equilibrium using a parameterized quantum circuit (PQC). The PQC represents the conditional strategy distribution with O(nL) trainable parameters, where n is the number of players and L is the circuit depth; for the largest setting studied here, n = 10 and L = 2, this corresponds to 60 trainable angles. The circuit is trained by gradient-based regret minimization with a negative entropy regularizer and a curriculum schedule over player counts. On a poker-style Bayesian game with two to ten players, the proposed solver achieves lower mean clipped regret than MCCFR across all tested player counts and lower regret than DCFR up to eight players, while DCFR performs best at ten players. These results show that compact PQC parameterizations can provide a viable variational representation for approximate equilibrium computation, while highlighting the roles of ansatz expressivity, optimization strategy, and classical simulation cost.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.03300"></a>
### [A proof of an identity for the critical exponents of jamming](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03300v1)

**Authors:** Giorgio Parisi, Francesco Zamponi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03300v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03300_figures/2606.03300_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03300_figures/2606.03300_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03300_figures/2606.03300_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03300_figures/2606.03300_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.03300_figures/2606.03300_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a rigorous, analytic proof that a key identity, $a+b=1$, holds for the critical exponents describing the jamming transition in dense hard spheres. The proof connects the mathematical structure of the full Replica-Symmetry-Breaking theory to the physical stability criteria of the system. This establishes a deep equivalence between theoretical scaling laws and mechanical stability arguments in the jamming limit.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical statistical mechanics, the rigorous mathematical techniques involving functional analysis (maximum principle) and the analysis of complex scaling equations are highly relevant to modern theoretical physics, including quantum many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To provide an analytic proof of the identity $a+b=1$ for the critical exponents governing the jamming transition in dense hard spheres.

**Main result.** The authors analytically prove the identity $a+b=1$ by showing that the phase-space marginal stability condition derived from the fullRSB equations is equivalent to the mechanical marginal stability condition.

**Method.** The proof combines analyzing the asymptotic behavior of coupled non-linear differential equations with applying the maximum principle to establish bounds on auxiliary functions derived from the fullRSB profile.

**Model / system.** The system is amorphous packings of hard or soft spheres undergoing a jamming transition, modeled using the full Replica-Symmetry-Breaking (fullRSB) Ansatz in the infinite-dimensional limit ($d 	o \infty$).

**Key observables.** Critical exponents $a, b, c$ (from fullRSB) and physical exponents $\alpha, 	heta, \kappa$ (controlling gap, force, and overlap distributions).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The jamming transition point, the infinite dimension limit ($d 	o \infty$), and the scaling exponents $a, b, c$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes the validity of the scaling equations and the marginal-stability identities established in previous work (CKPUZ).

**Paper structure.** The paper proceeds by revisiting the eigenvalue condition, deriving an algebraic identity from the fullRSB equations, and combining this with the marginal-stability identity to force the conclusion $a+b=1$.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Within the full replica-symmetry-breaking (fullRSB) solution of dense hard spheres in infinite dimension, Charbonneau, Kurchan, Parisi, Urbani, and Zamponi (CKPUZ; J.Stat.Mech.P10009, 2014) introduced three critical exponents $a$, $b$, $c$ governing the matching region of the fullRSB profile near the jamming transition. These exponents satisfy two scaling relations. The first, $b=(1+c)/2$, was established analytically by the diffusion-drift balance in the scaling ansatz. The second, $a+b=1$, was observed numerically to arbitrary precision but could not be proven. The exponents $a,b,c$ of the scaling fullRSB ansatz are related to the physical exponents $α, θ, κ$ that control the gap, force, and overlap distributions by the relations $α=a/b$, $θ=(c-a)/(b-c)$, $κ=c+1$. Crucially, the relation $a+b=1$ yields the scaling relations $α=1/(2+θ)$ and $κ=2-2/(3+θ)$ predicted on independent grounds by the mechanical-marginal-stability arguments of Wyart and collaborators. Here, we give an analytic proof of the identity $a+b=1$ from the scaling fullRSB equations. The proof was obtained through interaction with Claude (Sonnet 4.6 and Opus 4.7) and verified by us.

</details>

### [Generalised simultaneous transmission of arbitrary quantum states and classical information](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03181v1)

**Summary.** This paper details a Continuous-Variable Quantum Communication (CMQC) protocol enabling the simultaneous, low-error transmission of arbitrary quantum states and classical data. By encoding classical information via phase-space displacements and using continuous-variable teleportation, the scheme demonstrates high fidelity reconstruction. The results show that the protocol is robust, maintaining quantum correlations strong enough to violate the Bell inequality even under realistic channel losses.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses quantum networking by proposing a robust method to multiplex classical data and quantum entanglement over one physical channel, which is crucial for future quantum internet architectures.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a protocol for the simultaneous transmission of arbitrary quantum states and classical information over a single quantum channel without losing the integrity of either information type.

**Main result.** The protocol can achieve perfect reconstruction of both the classical signal and the quantum state in the limits of high classical signal strength and high squeezing, and it can violate the CHSH inequality under realistic conditions.

**Method.** The scheme uses continuous-variable quantum teleportation combined with classical phase-space displacements for encoding and subsequent inverse displacements for decoding.

**Model / system.** The system models optical quantum states transmitted through a lossy bosonic channel, utilizing continuous-variable teleportation and encoding classical data via coherent-state displacements.

**Key observables.** Symbol-Error Rate ($e_S$), CHSH expectation value ($S$), and the output state ($\hat{ho}_{	ext{out}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Transmissivity ($\eta$), squeezing parameter ($r$), and classical signal strength ($\alpha$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The protocol assumes the ability to probabilistically infer the true classical displacement and relies on the channel being a pure-loss channel for some derivations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the protocol structure, the dependence of the CHSH value ($S$) on channel loss ($\eta$) for varying classical signal strength ($\alpha$) and squeezing ($r$), and the success probabilities ($p_s$) for post-selection.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the CMQC protocol, analyzes its effective channel map in various limits (high SNR, infinite squeezing), and rigorously tests its capability to maintain quantum correlations by calculating the CHSH value for transmitted Bell states.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a protocol which allows for arbitrary optical quantum states to simultaneously carry and transmit classical data, without sacrificing the integrity of either the quantum or classical information. Our scheme encodes classical information via displacements in the phase space prior to transmission and retrieves each classical symbol via a Gaussian continuous-variable teleportation. The original quantum state is then restored by guessing the the original displacement and performing the appropriate inverse operation. In the limit of sufficiently high classical signal and high squeezing, we show that our scheme is capable of perfectly reconstructing both the input classical signal and the input quantum state without loss of coherence. An example is given in terms of the transmission of a dual-rail Bell state.

</details>
