<a id="paper-2606.03725"></a>
### [Torsion-induced gauge structure in curved quantum waveguides](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03725v1)

**Authors:** Xu-Yang Hou, Xianlong Gao, Hao Guo  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03725v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03725_figures/2606.03725_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: (a) Helical curve with Frenet frames. The tangent T , principal normal N, and binormal B are shown at rep- resentative points along the curve. (b) Transverse mode dis- tributions of the twofold-degenerate ﬁrst excited states, with |1, 0⟩oriented along N and |0, 1⟩oriented along B. Solid and dashed contours indicate positive and negative amplitudes, respectively.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03725_figures/2606.03725_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: (a) Energy dispersion E±(k). (b) Group velocity v±(k). In both panels, the blue solid curves represent the + branch, whereas the red dashed curves represent the − branch.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03725_figures/2606.03725_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: (a) Closed helix with 16 turns. A particle travers- ing the loop acquires a geometric phase exp(iτLσy) due to the torsion-induced holonomy. (b) Interference intensity as a function of the geometric winding parameter ν = τL/(2π).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical work analyzes quantum particles confined to curved waveguides, demonstrating that the curve's torsion induces a gauge potential when the transverse motion is degenerate. This results in a measurable momentum splitting in the energy spectrum, an effect confirmed by a structural analogy with classical elastic rod bending.

**Why it may be interesting.** The emergence of a gauge field purely from the spatial geometry (torsion) is a fundamental concept in geometric physics, providing a concrete realization of a Wilczek-Zee type connection in condensed matter waveguides.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how the geometric property of torsion ($	au$) induces a gauge structure in the effective quantum Hamiltonian for a particle confined to a curved space curve.

**Main result.** Torsion generates a matrix-valued Abelian gauge potential ($A_s \propto 	au \sigma_y$) in the degenerate transverse subspace, leading to a momentum shift ($\pm 	au$) in the energy dispersion relation. This effect has a direct classical analogue in elastic rod bending.

**Method.** The analysis uses thin-layer quantization and adiabatic approximation to derive the effective 1D Hamiltonian from the 3D Laplacian, comparing the scalar (non-degenerate) and degenerate transverse subspace cases.

**Model / system.** The system is a particle confined to a space curve in $\mathbb{R}^3$, analyzed using the Frenet-Serret frame. The effective dynamics are governed by a gauge-covariant Hamiltonian when the transverse subspace is degenerate.

**Key observables.** Energy dispersion $E_{\pm}(k)$, group velocity $v_{\pm}(k)$, and the geometric phase/holonomy accumulated around closed loops.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Curvature ($\kappa$), Torsion ($	au$), and the transverse confinement length scale ($\ell_\perp$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies on the thin-layer quantization limit and the adiabatic approximation, distinguishing between scalar and degenerate transverse mode subspaces.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the energy dispersion $E_{\pm}(k)$ and group velocity $v_{\pm}(k)$ for a helical wire, showing the splitting due to $	au$. The classical analogue is shown via the polarization evolution operator.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the general framework using thin-layer quantization, contrasting the scalar (no $	au$ dependence) and degenerate (gauge potential appears) cases. It then derives the explicit gauge potential and the resulting Hamiltonian, concluding with the comparison to classical wave physics (elastic rods) to confirm the structural isomorphism.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the quantum dynamics of a particle confined to a space curve within the thin-layer quantization framework. For a nondegenerate scalar transverse mode, torsion does not enter the local effective Hamiltonian, which contains only the curvature-induced scalar geometric potential. In contrast, when a degenerate transverse subspace is retained, the rotation of the Frenet normal frame becomes dynamically relevant and generates a matrix-valued Abelian gauge potential. Using a projection-based derivation in a co-rotating Frenet-frame basis, we show that this effective gauge potential is directly determined by the local torsion of the curve. The resulting effective Hamiltonian takes a gauge-covariant form and produces two transverse-mode branches whose parabolic dispersions are shifted in opposite directions in momentum space. For closed curves, the associated holonomy is controlled by the integrated torsion and leads to geometric interference. These results provide a direct realization of a Wilczek--Zee-type connection induced purely by spatial geometry in curved quantum waveguides. We further construct a classical-wave analogue using the degenerate bending modes of an isotropic elastic rod, demonstrating that the same torsion-induced gauge structure appears in continuum wave physics.

</details>

### [Towards a Hybrid Quantum Enhanced Solution for Densest k-Subgraph Problem](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03196v1)

**Authors:** Ravi Sangwan, Prabhat Anand, M Girish Chandra  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03196v1>

<sub>Fig. 1. Convergence of the best maximum density found for k = 10 subgraphs on a random graph with edge probability p = 0.3.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03196_figures/2606.03196_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4 visualizes the solutions obtained by different methods on a representative instance of the multiple dense-region graph. The GBS solution with post-selection differs from the brute- force optimum by four vertices, whereas Charikar’s solution differs by two vertices. In contrast, GBS with edge-based and ACO-based post-processing select the same subgraph and differ from the brute-force optimum by only one vertex. This</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.03196_figures/2606.03196_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4. Dense k = 10 subgraphs identified by different methods on the same multiple dense-region graph. The selected nodes are overlaid on a common layout for comparison.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores using Gaussian Boson Sampling (GBS) to tackle the hard Densest k-Subgraph Problem. It finds that GBS alone is insufficient because of strict size constraints, but by coupling GBS with classical refinement techniques (like edge-based or ACO repair), the method achieves near-optimal solutions with vastly improved sampling efficiency. This highlights the power of hybrid quantum-classical frameworks for graph optimization.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work demonstrates a practical, hybrid quantum-classical workflow for solving a hard combinatorial optimization problem, showing how simple classical refinement can unlock the potential of a quantum sampling primitive like GBS.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the NP-hard Densest k-Subgraph Problem (DkSP), which seeks a subgraph of size k maximizing induced density.

**Main result.** The combination of Gaussian Boson Sampling (GBS) with lightweight classical post-processing (like edge-based or ACO refinement) significantly improves sampling efficiency and solution quality compared to GBS post-selection alone.

**Method.** A hybrid quantum-classical approach is used, where GBS generates initial, potentially incomplete, samples, which are then refined using classical greedy or meta-heuristic algorithms to enforce the correct subgraph size k.

**Model / system.** The quantum model is Gaussian Boson Sampling (GBS), simulated using photonic quantum circuits. The problem is mapped onto the graph's adjacency matrix, and the objective function relates to the hafnian of principal submatrices.

**Key observables.** Maximum induced density $D(S)$, sampling efficiency (samples needed for optimum), and computational cost.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Target subgraph size $k$ (e.g., $k=10$), graph structure (Erdős–Rényi vs. community-structured), and post-processing parameters (e.g., ACO parameters).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The core assumption is that GBS provides a useful, structured sampling bias, but this bias must be corrected by classical post-processing to overcome strict cardinality constraints.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the convergence of various methods (GBS+Post-processing vs. GBS+Post-selection vs. Classical) in finding the maximum density for DkSP on structured graphs, showing significant performance gains for the hybrid methods.

**Paper structure.** The paper first defines DkSP and the GBS model, then analyzes the limitations of GBS post-selection. It systematically introduces and compares three classical post-processing techniques (degree, edge, ACO) to repair near-$k$ samples, culminating in performance benchmarks against classical and pure quantum methods.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the application of Gaussian Boson Sampling (GBS) to the densest k-subgraph problem (DkSP). GBS with hard post-selection suffers from poor sampling efficiency due to strict cardinality constraints. To address this limitation, we introduce effective classical post-processing strategies that transform, otherwise discarded, near-k samples into feasible solutions. A comprehensive set of simulations is carried out, demonstrating that these approaches achieve near-optimal solution quality while improving sampling efficiency by approximately 4X compared to post-selection on community-structured graphs, and also post-selection often fails to reach the optimal solution on sparse random graphs even with large number of samples. Furthermore, the proposed methods perform on par with, and in some cases outperform, established classical approaches for graphs up to moderate size. Overall, the results indicate that while GBS with post-selection alone is insufficient, its combination with lightweight classical refinement can be highly effective. This underscores the potential of hybrid quantum-classical frameworks and positions GBS as a promising sampling primitive for combinatorial graph optimization.

</details>

### [Training a Predictive Coding Network on ImageNet using Equilibrium Propagation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03584v1)

**Authors:** Tugdual Kerjan, Rasmus Høier, Benjamin Scellier  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03584v1>

NaN means that training failed and diverged, resulting in NaN values.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work pioneers the use of Equilibrium Propagation (EP) to train Predictive Coding Networks (PCNs) on the massive ImageNet dataset. By adapting EP's physics-based training framework, the authors achieve state-of-the-art performance, demonstrating that EP is a scalable and viable alternative to standard backpropagation for energy-based neural models.

**Why it may be interesting.** The application of physics-inspired training methods like EP to complex, large-scale neural networks bridges the gap between theoretical physics models (like energy minimization) and modern AI, which is relevant for understanding complex system dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops and demonstrates a novel training method for Predictive Coding Networks (PCNs) using Equilibrium Propagation (EP) at the large scale of ImageNet.

**Main result.** They successfully trained a VGG10 PCN on full-size ImageNet, achieving a top-5 error rate of 13.23%, which is competitive with the standard backpropagation baseline.

**Method.** The method combines the centered variant of EP with a novel equilibration scheme tailored for PCNs, allowing the use of physics-based training principles on large-scale deep learning models.

**Model / system.** The model is a Predictive Coding Network (PCN), which is treated as an energy-based model rooted in computational neuroscience. The training framework is based on Equilibrium Propagation (EP), a physics-based training method.

**Key observables.** Top-5 classification test error rate, total energy function $F$, and the equilibrium state $h(	heta, x)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nudging parameter ($eta$), number of nudge iterations ($K$), and the scale of the dataset (ImageNet).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that EP can effectively train PCNs, extending the applicability of EP beyond small-scale problems.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare EP performance against backpropagation across various datasets (CIFAR, ImageNet) and show the convergence dynamics of the total energy function $F$ during the nudging process.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces EP for PCNs, details the mathematical formulation using nudging and finite differences, benchmarks the method on ImageNet 32x32, and concludes with the full-size ImageNet results, comparing performance against established baselines.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Equilibrium Propagation (EP) is a physics-based training framework that has primarily been employed in energy-based models, including continuous Hopfield networks, nonlinear resistive networks and coupled phase oscillators. However, EP's practical applications have so far remained limited to relatively small-scale problems. Predictive coding networks (PCNs), another class of energy-based models rooted in computational neuroscience, are typically trained with a specialized algorithm and have likewise not yet been demonstrated at large scale. In this work, we develop an EP-based training method for PCNs which combines the centered variant of EP with a novel equilibration scheme for PCNs. Using this approach, we train a 10-layer convolutional PCN (VGG10) on full-size ImageNet, achieving 13.23\% test error rate on the top-5 classification task, close to the 12.2\% backpropagation baseline. To our knowledge, this is the first demonstration of both PCNs and EP-based training at ImageNet scale. These results significantly extend the scalability of both approaches and suggest that the primary challenges in scaling EP in other physical systems may come more from the computational properties of these systems than from inherent limitations of the EP framework.

</details>

</details>

### [Generation and time evolution of anomalous Floquet Majorana flat edge modes in two-dimensional noncolinear magnet-superconductor heterostructures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03193v1)

**Authors:** Kamalesh Bera, Priyanka Mohan, Arijit Saha  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03193v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5**

**Summary.** This theoretical work explores how periodically driving a magnet-superconductor junction can generate exotic topological superconducting phases characterized by anomalous Majorana flat edge modes. By analyzing the quasi-energy spectrum and winding number across varying driving frequencies, the authors map out a topological phase diagram. The findings demonstrate the tunability of these topological states using time-periodic driving, which has implications for superconducting quantum devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of Floquet engineering to induce and control topological superconductivity, particularly the emergence of anomalous $\pi$-modes, is highly relevant to open quantum systems and superconducting quantum circuits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the generation and time evolution of anomalous Floquet Majorana flat edge modes (FMFEMs) in a 2D noncollinear magnet-superconductor heterostructure subjected to a harmonic drive.

**Main result.** The system exhibits distinct topological phases dependent on the driving frequency, characterized by the emergence of $\pi$-FMFEMs at low frequencies and $0$-FMFEMs at high frequencies, confirmed by the winding number.

**Method.** The research employs a real-space tight-binding model analyzed using Floquet theory, supplemented by Brillouin-Wigner and Floquet perturbation theory for analytical insight.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a 2D Shiba lattice (magnet-superconductor heterostructure) described by a Bogoliubov-de-Gennes Hamiltonian, which is periodically driven by a time-dependent chemical potential.

**Key observables.** Quasi-energy spectrum, Local Density of States (LDOS), and the topological winding number.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Driving frequency ($\Omega$), driving amplitude ($V$), and the superconducting gap ($\Delta_0$). Key regimes include low, intermediate, and high frequencies.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the validity of the Floquet formalism, noting that perturbative methods (BW/FPT) fail to capture $\pi$-FMFEMs in the low-frequency regime.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the edge spectra (quasi-energy vs. momentum) for different driving frequencies, and show the winding number as a function of momentum to confirm topological invariants.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the physical model and Hamiltonian to calculating the quasi-energy spectrum across different driving regimes, followed by topological characterization using the winding number and comparison with perturbative expansions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We theoretically investigate the realization of gapless Floquet topological superconducting phases in a two-dimensional magnet-superconductor heterostructure (2D Shiba lattice) in the presence of a harmonic drive implemented in the chemical potential. Employing a real-space tight-binding model, we obtain both the regular $0$- and anomalous $π$-Floquet Majorana flat edge modes (FMFEMs) in the quasi-energy spectrum. We also study the real-time evolution of the FMFEMs and analyze their local density of states in the presence of such a periodic drive. The topological characterization is performed using the winding number, exploiting the chiral symmetry of the equivalent bulk effective momentum-space Hamiltonian. This is also supported by the corresponding edge state spectra. Furthermore, we employ the Brillouin-Wigner (BW) and Floquet perturbation theory (FPT) to gain analytical insight into the problem. We compare our exact (numerical), BW, and FPT results in terms of the quasi-energy spectra obtained across different frequency regimes. We find good agreement between the exact numerical, BW, and FPT results in the higher-frequency and high-amplitude domain, particularly close to the $0$-quasi-energy modes.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.02694"></a>
### [Dynamical Josephson Effect Between a Singlet and a Triplet Superconductor](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02694v1)

**Authors:** Morten Amundsen, Niladri Banerjee, Igor Žutić  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02694v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.02694_figures/2606.02694_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Schematic of the phase accumulated by electrons ↑, ↓ tunneling from the singlet to the triplet superconductor, and holes ↑, ↓tunneling in the opposite direction.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.02694_figures/2606.02694_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. The dissipationless (dissipative) IS (IA) current am- plitude as a function of the driving frequency Ω, normalized to I0 = π|∆0,R|/2eRN. (a), (b) for singlet-singlet and (c), (d) singlet-triplet JJs.</sub>

**Summary.** This theoretical work analyzes the Josephson effect in a junction between singlet and triplet superconductors under a time-varying gate voltage. It demonstrates that the dynamic modulation induces a novel cos(phi) current component, which is a direct consequence of the spin symmetry mismatch. This effect suggests new ways to use such junctions for spin-triplet current sources in quantum devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study of dynamical Josephson effects in unconventional superconductors provides a platform to probe spin-momentum locking and parity selection rules, which are crucial concepts in superconducting spintronics and topological superconductivity.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how the selection rules governing the Josephson effect are modified when the junction is driven dynamically, particularly when coupling a spin-singlet and a spin-triplet superconductor.

**Main result.** A time-dependent gate voltage induces a novel Josephson current component proportional to cos(phi), which is absent in the static limit and arises due to the spin symmetry mismatch between the superconductors.

**Method.** The analysis employs the Keldysh Green function formalism to calculate the time-dependent current, decomposing it into symmetric (dissipationless) and antisymmetric (dissipative) components.

**Model / system.** The system is a Josephson junction (JJ) connecting a spin-singlet (SL) and a spin-triplet (SR) superconductor, separated by a 2DEG whose coupling is modulated by a time-dependent gate voltage inducing SOC.

**Key observables.** The current-phase relation (CPR), the total time-dependent current I(t), and the relative strengths of the dissipationless and dissipative current components.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The driving frequency ($\Omega$), the SOC strength ($\alpha_1$), and the superconducting gap sizes ($\Delta_L, \Delta_R$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a simple SOC model ($\propto \sigma_z k_y$) and uses approximations in the Keldysh formalism, neglecting higher-order frequency mode contributions.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically illustrate the JJ setup with time-dependent SOC and show the phase accumulation of tunneling particles, while Figure 3 compares the normalized amplitudes of the AC and dissipative currents for singlet-singlet vs. singlet-triplet junctions.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical model (SL/SR JJ with time-dependent SOC) to deriving the current-phase relation using phase accumulation arguments, culminating in the full calculation via the Keldysh formalism to extract the time-dependent current components.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Phase-sensitive Josephson effect has long been central to identifying unconventional pairing symmetries in superconductors. Although the selection rules governing Josephson junctions (JJs) are generally determined by the symmetries of the constituent superconductors, we demonstrate that this paradigm is modified in the dynamic regime. By modeling a JJ where spin-singlet and spin-triplet superconductors are separated by a two-dimensional electron gas, we show that a time-dependent gate voltage qualitatively changes the underlying selection rules. This modification arises as a consequence of the gate-controlled spin-orbit coupling. A harmonic modulation of the gate voltage generates an oscillatory $\cos φ$ Josephson component which vanishes in the static limit. The resulting charge current contains both dissipationless and dissipative components, with the latter strongly suppressed at low temperatures. This dynamical Josephson effect could transform the use of JJs in qubits, as sources of spin-triplet currents, and as platforms for proximity effects.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper reports on the in-situ characterization of lithium intercalation in the layered material LaTe_3 using advanced multimodal STEM. The authors successfully mapped three distinct, reversible ordered phases, most notably discovering a novel, superdense three-layer lithium phase. This work demonstrates the power of combining multiple electron microscopy techniques to track complex solid-state electrochemical processes crucial for battery development.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on materials science, the study involves tracking complex, reversible phase transitions and structural ordering under non-equilibrium electrochemical conditions, which touches upon concepts relevant to non-equilibrium statistical mechanics and open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand the lithium (Li) ordering and dynamics during intercalation in layered materials for use in next-generation energy storage.

**Main result.** The researchers identified three distinct, reversible ordered phases of Li_xLaTe_3, notably discovering a new, superdense three-layer lithium phase within the van der Waals gap at high Li concentrations.

**Method.** The work employs a multimodal approach combining in-situ Scanning Transmission Electron Microscopy (STEM) with spectroscopy (EELS) and diffraction, complemented by DFT calculations.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the layered material LaTe_3 undergoing electrochemical lithiation, forming Li_xLaTe_3. The study tracks structural and chemical changes across varying lithium concentrations (x=1/3 to 3).

**Key observables.** HAADF-STEM images, EELS spectra (tracking Li/Te bonding), 4D-STEM diffraction patterns (revealing superlattice spots), and Raman phonon modes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lithium concentration (x), applied electrochemical potential (V), and in-plane strain (up to 5%).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The multimodal STEM approach is necessary to overcome the limitations of single-technique X-ray methods for observing Li ordering.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the in-situ setup, structural changes via STEM/diffraction patterns across different phases, and the evolution of electronic states via EELS.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a progression from establishing the need for advanced characterization to presenting evidence for three distinct phases, culminating in the characterization of the novel, superdense Li phase.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding lithium (Li) ordering and dynamics is foundational in energy storage. X-ray based experimental methods do not simultaneously provide atomic structure information together with chemical composition and local bonding information for lithium in solids. Here we employ scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) and combine imaging, spectroscopy, and diffraction within a single experiment, to observe, in situ, an all-solid-state electrochemical cell. By integrating multimodal STEM with other complementary techniques, we report a complete mapping of lithium intercalation in a layered system, LaTe3. We identify three ordered phases of LixLaTe3 with x ranging from 1/3 to 3 with in-plane strain of up to 5%. At a very high lithium concentration of Li3LaTe3, we discover an unexpected three-layer, superdense lithium phase with tetragonal symmetry occupying the van der Waals gap. This represents a new Li phase that is reversible. Our multimodal approach thus enables complete tracking of lithium ordering and dynamics, important for next-generation energy storage applications.

</details>

### [Attractive Hopfions and Bimerons in Thin Films of Chiral Magnets: Cluster Formation and Lattice Instability in the Conical Phase](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03016v1)

**Summary.** This work theoretically examines how topological solitons (bimerons and hopfions) interact within chiral magnetic thin films hosting a conical background. Although the interactions are attractive, leading to stable clusters, the system fundamentally resists forming a true, stable periodic lattice. This highlights a crucial distinction between local attraction and global thermodynamic stability in complex magnetic materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** The concept of topological defects interacting in a frustrated background, leading to clustering without true long-range order, mirrors complex phase behavior seen in frustrated quantum magnets or defect arrays in quantum materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the energetics, interactions, and ordering tendencies of topological textures like bimerons and hopfions in thin films of chiral magnets under a conical background state.

**Main result.** While attractive interactions promote the formation of bound clusters, the system resists forming a thermodynamically stable, long-range periodic lattice, instead favoring invasion by the conical spiral or CF-1 phase.

**Method.** The research uses theoretical energy minimization techniques applied to a phenomenological energy functional governing the magnetization profile in the thin film geometry.

**Model / system.** The system models thin films of chiral magnets or chiral liquid crystals, analyzed using an energy functional incorporating exchange, Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya interaction (DMI), and surface anchoring terms. The background state is characterized as a conical phase.

**Key observables.** Eigen-energy of isolated solitons, inter-soliton interaction potential, and the energy density dependence on the lattice period.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Applied magnetic field strength (h), surface anchoring strength (Ks), and the geometry of the conical background.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis operates in a thin-film geometry and treats the system using a phenomenological energy functional, neglecting certain nonlocal electrostatic effects.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate energy density maps, the dependence of soliton energy on radius (W(R)), and crucially, the lack of a minimum energy density as a function of the lattice period for hexagonal arrangements.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the background conical state, then analyzing the energetics of isolated bimerons and hopfions, demonstrating their attraction, and finally proving that this attraction does not lead to stable long-range crystalline order due to phase competition.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the energetics, interactions, and ordering tendencies of bimerons (cholesteric fingers of the second type, CF--2) and hopfions in thin films of chiral magnets and chiral liquid crystals hosting a conical background state. Although isolated bimerons possess positive eigen-energy with respect to the conical phase, they develop an attractive interaction mediated by the restructuring and partial overlap of their positive-energy shells, i.e., intermediate regions formed relative to the conical state. This attraction promotes the formation of bound pairs and extended bimeron chains, even in parameter regimes where a periodic bimeron lattice is no longer thermodynamically stable.   Extending the analysis to three dimensions, we show that circularization of bimerons into hopfions renders their energy finite and gives rise to a well-defined metastability window closely linked to the stability range of cholesteric fingers. Isolated hopfions likewise exhibit an attractive interaction within the conical phase, leading to the formation of hexagonally ordered clusters. The attraction originates from the competition between favorable and unfavorable twist regions and from the energetic cost of the shell structures imposed by the conical background.   Despite the presence of attractive pair potentials and cluster formation, we demonstrate that hexagonal hopfion lattices do not exhibit an equilibrium lattice period. Instead, the system evolves toward states in which the conical spiral or the CF--1 phase (cholesteric fingers of the first type) progressively invade the inter-soliton regions, thereby preventing crystallization. Our results reveal a regime of attraction without stable long-range order and clarify the interplay between topology, confinement, and conical-phase frustration in chiral magnet and liquid-crystal thin films.

</details>

</details>

**Summary.** This research demonstrates that epitaxial $\mathrm{Co_2MnSi}$ retains its critical structural order even after nanoscale patterning, which is crucial for its magnetic functionality. The intrinsic cubic magnetocrystalline anisotropy is shown to stabilize spin-wave dynamics, enabling nonlinear magnonic operation at near-zero bias fields. This establishes $\mathrm{Co_2MnSi}$ as a highly promising, scalable platform for next-generation spintronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter magnetism, the study of nonlinear wave dynamics and the use of advanced spectroscopic techniques ($\mu$BLS) to probe collective excitations in patterned systems has parallels with studying nonlinear wave propagation in quantum optical cavities or superconducting circuits.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central goal is to establish $\mathrm{Co_2MnSi}$ as a robust, scalable platform for bias-field-free nonlinear half-metal magnonics at the nanoscale.

**Main result.** The intrinsic cubic magnetocrystalline anisotropy in epitaxial $\mathrm{Co_2MnSi}$ stabilizes spin-wave dynamics, suppressing first-order instabilities and enabling low-bias field operation in the favorable Damon-Eshbach geometry.

**Method.** The study combines epitaxial growth (MBE), top-down nanofabrication (EBL/Ar+ etching), and advanced characterization techniques including $\mu$BLS, FMR, and TEM to probe structure and dynamics.

**Model / system.** The physical system is epitaxial $\mathrm{L2_1}$-ordered $\mathrm{Co_2MnSi}$ Heusler thin films patterned into nanoscale waveguides. The analysis relies on spin-wave dispersion models incorporating magnetocrystalline anisotropy and dipolar effects.

**Key observables.** Spin-wave dispersion, nonlinear instability onset (first and second order), spin-wave decay lengths ($\delta_{amp}$), and magnetization switching fields.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Intrinsic cubic magnetocrystalline anisotropy, ultralow Gilbert damping ($\alpha \le 10^{-3}$), and operation at vanishing external bias fields.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the desirable magnetic properties, particularly the crystal structure, are preserved during the challenging top-down nanoscale patterning process.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the nanoscale integrity of the L21-ordered structure after patterning, quantify the cubic magnetocrystalline anisotropy via FMR, and show $\mu$BLS spectra confirming nonlinear instabilities and stabilized spin-wave propagation.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically moves from confirming the structural integrity of the material after nanofabrication (TEM/STEM) to quantifying the intrinsic magnetic anisotropy (FMR/VSM), and finally demonstrating the functional consequences of this anisotropy on nonlinear spin-wave dynamics ($\mu$BLS/Kerr).

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Half-metallic Heusler compounds like $\mathrm{Co_2MnSi}$ allow to bridge magnonic and spintronic functionality for hybrid unconventional computing approaches with sought-after properties like 100% spin polarization and associated low Gilbert damping $α\leq 10^{-3}$. However, the desirable material parameters are inherently tied to the crystal lattice with a particularly critical dependence on structural order in $\mathrm{Co_2MnSi}$. To date, the successful fabrication of nanoscale devices with robust structural integrity remains yet a challenge, and consequently the impact of the material parameters on the resulting nonlinear spin-wave dynamics remains largely unexplored. Here, we report on a study of linear and nonlinear spin-wave dynamics in transversally magnetized $\mathrm{Co_2MnSi}$ waveguides with impeccable crystalline ordering. We show that epitaxial, $\mathrm{L2}_1$-ordered $\mathrm{Co_2MnSi}$ exhibits an intrinsic cubic anisotropy with first- and second-order contributions, stabilizing a magnetization alignment along the crystal $\langle110\rangle$ directions. We confirm the implication of an unaffected crystal structure resulting in preserved magnetic properties in the patterned structures. Herein, the persistent magnetocrystalline anisotropy reshapes the spin-wave dispersion which yields a first-order nonlinear instability suppression range extending over several GHz - even for vanishing bias fields. Moreover, the intrinsic magnetocrystalline anisotropy can be exploited to counteract shape demagnetization for a stabilized low bias field operation in the favourable Damon-Eshbach geometry with high group velocities and decay lengths. Together with the proven half-metallicity and ultralow Gilbert damping, this research establishes $\mathrm{Co_2MnSi}$ as a robust, scalable platform towards bias-field-free nonlinear half-metal magnonics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.02770"></a>
### [Early Experiments on Macroscopic Quantum Tunneling](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02770v1)

**Authors:** Willem den Boer  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02770v1>

**Summary.** This paper reviews early experimental attempts to observe Macroscopic Quantum Tunneling (MQT) in superconducting point contacts, drawing on theoretical models of potential energy barriers. By comparing calculated thermal and quantum tunneling escape rates, the authors argue that low-temperature flux transitions are likely quantum mechanical in origin. It provides crucial historical context for the development of superconducting quantum devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is foundational for understanding quantum dissipation and tunneling in superconducting circuits, directly informing the physics used in modern superconducting qubits and quantum computation platforms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the historical experimental evidence for Macroscopic Quantum Tunneling (MQT) in superconducting circuits, predating conclusive modern demonstrations.

**Main result.** The analysis suggests that at very low temperatures (1 K), the observed flux transitions are best explained by quantum tunneling rather than classical thermal activation, although the conclusion is tempered by experimental uncertainties.

**Method.** The authors analyze historical experimental data from Nb point contacts and apply theoretical models comparing thermal escape rates ($R_{th}$) derived from Arrhenius-like behavior against WKB tunneling rates ($R_{tun}$).

**Model / system.** The system modeled is a superconducting ring containing a Josephson junction or point contact, whose dynamics are governed by a potential energy landscape $U(\Phi)$ dependent on the trapped magnetic flux $\Phi$.

**Key observables.** Hysteresis in flux vs. applied flux curves, and the temperature dependence of the measured escape rates ($R_{th}$ vs. $R_{tun}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature ($T$), critical current ($I_c$), total inductance ($L$), and the superconducting flux quantum ($\Phi_0$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the applicability of the WKB approximation for tunneling and assumes the system can be modeled by a simple potential barrier, though the author notes limitations regarding dissipation and noise sources.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the potential energy barrier $U(\Phi)$ vs. trapped flux, and compare calculated thermal and tunneling rates ($R_{th}$ vs. $R_{tun}$) as functions of temperature, inductance, and critical current.

**Paper structure.** The paper reviews early 1979 experiments, establishes the theoretical framework using the potential energy landscape, calculates the ratio of thermal to tunneling rates, and concludes by contrasting these early findings with the later, conclusive work by Clarke et al.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Before conclusive evidence of Macroscopic Quantum Tunneling, MQT, was published by Clarke, Devoret and Martinis in 1985, several other groups reported experimental results interpreted as MQT. The first, in chronological order, was in 1980 based on studies done at Leiden University in 1979. This paper looks back at these experiments on low capacitance Niobium point contacts in an rf SQUID, radio-frequency Superconducting Quantum Interference Device, configuration at temperatures between 1 and 4.2 K. The research was inspired by the theoretical predictions by Ivanchenko and Zilberman in 1969 on MQT in current-biased Josephson junctions and by Leggett in 1978 on MQT in closed loops with a superconducting weak link.

</details>

### [Chirality-resolved spectroscopy of Caroli-de Gennes-Matricon states in multiband FeTe$_{1-x}$Se$_{x}$ superconductors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03637v1)

**Summary.** This work uses terahertz Faraday magneto-optics to investigate the quantized helical Caroli-de Gennes-Matricon (CdGM) states in FeTe1-xSe_x superconductors. By exploiting polarization selectivity, the researchers achieved band-resolved measurements of vortex-core dynamics. This confirms that THz magneto-optics is a powerful tool for studying helical excitations in complex, multiband superconductors.

**Why it may be interesting.** The technique directly probes the angular momentum and band character of quasiparticles confined in vortices, which is a highly sensitive probe of superconducting pairing symmetry and multiband physics, relevant to open quantum systems dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to probe the relaxation dynamics and helicity of quantized helical Caroli-de Gennes-Matricon (CdGM) states within the vortex cores of multiband FeTe1-xSe_x superconductors.

**Main result.** The experiment successfully established terahertz magneto-optics as a direct probe for helical vortex-core excitations, providing band-resolved evidence for multiband CdGM states in iron-based superconductors.

**Method.** Terahertz Faraday magneto-optical spectroscopy is employed to measure the polarization-selective optical response, which is sensitive to the helicity of the quasiparticles.

**Model / system.** The physical system is epitaxial FeTe1-xSe_x thin films, modeled as nodeless multiband superconductors. The analysis uses a theoretical framework describing the vortex momentum decomposition into independent helical contributions from each superconducting band.

**Key observables.** Faraday rotation ($	heta_F$), which is proportional to the difference in optical response between left- and right-circularly polarized light, and the resulting band-resolved vortex conductivity ($	ext{Re}\sigma_v^{\pm}(\omega)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis is sensitive to the superconducting coherence length ($\xi$), vortex mass, and the doping level ($x$), which systematically alters the relative contributions of electron and hole bands.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the superconducting state can be treated using non-interacting condensate approximations, and that the measured response is dominated by the vortex-core excitations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the expected CdGM energies for electron and hole bands, the setup for Faraday rotation measurements, and the magnetic field/temperature dependence of the Faraday rotation for different doping levels.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the theoretical basis of CdGM states and the use of THz magneto-optics. It then details the theoretical modeling of the vortex conductivity in a multiband context. Finally, it presents experimental results showing the direct measurement of band-resolved, helical vortex excitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We employ terahertz Faraday magneto-optical spectroscopy to probe the relaxation dynamics of quantized helical Caroli-de Gennes-Matricon (CdGM) states in epitaxial FeTe$_{1-x}$Se$_x$ thin films, nodeless multiband superconductors with short coherence lengths in the moderately clean limit. By exploiting polarization-selective optical transitions, we directly resolve the helicity and band origin of vortex-core quasiparticles. We observe long-lived CdGM resonances with opposite circular polarizations for electron- and hole-like bands. This enables independent, band-resolved determination of quasiparticle lifetimes, vortex masses, coherence lengths, and upper critical fields, and reveals their systematic evolution with isovalent substitution. The results establish terahertz magneto-optics as a direct probe of helical vortex-core excitations and provide dynamical evidence for multiband CdGM states in iron-based superconductors.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03041"></a>
### [Geometric aspects of spin transport in magnetic multilayers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03041v1)

**Authors:** Valentin Fadeev  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03041v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2606.03041_figures/2606.03041_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Single-FM Weyl coordinates as a function of ferro- magnetic thickness.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2606.03041_figures/2606.03041_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Weyl-chamber trajectory for the single-FM thickness sweep. The diagonal λ1 = λ2 corresponds to spin-independent radial filtering.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.03041_figures/2606.03041_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Model geometry for the single-FM example. Identical nonmagnetic leads are attached to a single ferromagnetic layer whose thickness is swept.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2606.03041_figures/2606.03041_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Spin-current components in the spacer of the FM/NM/FM stack as the analyser angle is swept. The lower panel compares the out-of-plane spin current component with the noncommutativity diagnostic ∥[r′ 13, r35]∥.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2606.03041_figures/2606.03041_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 6. Weyl coordinates for the FM/NM/FM stack as a function of analyser angle. The splitting λ1 −λ2 tracks the anisotropic part of the radial filtering response.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work develops a geometric theory for spin transport in magnetic multilayers by mapping the transfer matrix formalism onto the structure of noncompact Lie groups. It uses Iwasawa decomposition and Weyl-chamber variables to characterize spin filtering, while commutator analysis rigorously links the non-commutativity of spin filters to the generation of spin-transfer torque components.

**Why it may be interesting.** The deep integration of geometric group theory (Iwasawa, Weyl chambers) into condensed matter transport physics provides a powerful, unifying mathematical language for understanding complex spin-filtering phenomena, analogous to how geometric methods are used in quantum optics or quantum field theory.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To establish a rigorous geometric framework for describing spin-dependent electron transport through magnetic multilayers.

**Main result.** The transport dynamics are characterized by a path-ordered exponential (transfer matrix) whose action can be decomposed using Iwasawa theory, revealing minimal transport invariants (Weyl-chamber variables) and linking non-commutativity to spin-torque generation.

**Method.** The authors employ advanced group theory, specifically interpreting the transfer matrix as an element of a noncompact Lie group, utilizing Iwasawa decomposition, and analyzing commutator structures via the Baker-Campbell-Hausdorff expansion.

**Model / system.** The system is magnetic multilayers (e.g., FM/NM/FM stacks) where spin-resolved electron transport is governed by the stationary Schrödinger equation. The model incorporates spin-orbit coupling (SOC) and is analyzed using transfer matrices derived from first-order spatial evolution equations.

**Key observables.** Weyl-chamber variables ($\lambda_i$), Spin–Mueller–Jones tensor components, and the commutator norm $\|[r'_{13}, r_{35}]\|$ which quantifies out-of-plane spin current.

**Important parameters / regimes.** SOC strength ($\alpha_{so}$), spacer thickness, and the relative magnetization angles ($	heta$) between adjacent ferromagnetic layers.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the transfer matrix formalism being equivalent to a path-ordered exponential, and the use of Iwasawa decomposition for the underlying Lie group structure.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the evolution of Weyl coordinates ($\lambda_1, \lambda_2$) as a function of analyzer angle or spacer thickness, demonstrating the separation of compact (spin rotation) and noncompact (filtering) information, and showing the deformation of the Weyl trajectory due to SOC.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the transfer matrix formalism, then applying group theory (Möbius/Iwasawa) to decompose the action. It subsequently analyzes the role of SOC and non-commuting reflections, culminating in the structural derivation of spin-torque components via commutator analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We discuss spin-dependent transfer-matrix formalism applied to magnetic multilayers in geometric terms. Starting from the stationary Schrödinger equation rewritten as a first-order spatial evolution problem, we interpret the transfer matrix as a path-ordered exponential and relate its matching-matrix construction to a noncompact group constraint. We thenconnect the induced Möbius action on reflection matrices to an Iwasawa decomposition, identify Weyl-chamber variables as the minimal noncompact transport invariants, and show how torque-related spin structures arise from compact-sector and commutator contributions. A sequence of multilayer examples illustrates the transition from pure spin filtering to controlled spin-orbit mixing and the resulting deformation of Weyl-chamber trajectories. We finally comment on the extension to higher-dimensional internal spaces relevant to orbital transport and realistic calculations.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03336"></a>
### [Spin-wave phase modulation using magnetic domain walls in dipolarly coupled structures for non-volatile magnonic computation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03336v1)

**Authors:** Hanadi Mortada, Philipp Pirro, Alexandre Abbass Hamadeh  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03336v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This work proposes a novel, non-volatile phase shifter for magnonics by coupling a straight spin-wave waveguide to a half-ring containing a magnetic domain wall. By moving the DW, the local magnetic field and the spin-wave dispersion relation are dynamically altered, enabling continuous phase tuning without external bias fields or significant energy loss.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the core physics is classical magnetism, the concept of using localized, non-dissipative field perturbations (like those from DWs) to engineer wave properties (like phase) is analogous to controlling boundary conditions or potentials in quantum wave mechanics, making it relevant to understanding engineered quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is developing a controllable, non-volatile, and energy-efficient phase shifter for spin-wave (SW)-based logic and information processing.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that displacing a magnetic domain wall (DW) in an adjacent waveguide can continuously tune the SW phase shift over a wide range by modulating the local dispersion relation via dipolar coupling.

**Method.** The study relies on micromagnetic simulations to model the coupled magnetostrictive system and analyze the resulting spin-wave propagation characteristics.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a hybrid structure: a straight nanoscale waveguide dipolarly coupled to a half-ring waveguide, both made of Bi-doped Yttrium Iron Garnet (Bi:YIG). The phase shift is controlled by the static position of a magnetic domain wall (DW) within the half-ring.

**Key observables.** The primary observable is the tunable phase shift ($\Delta\varphi$) of the spin waves, which is shown to be strongly dependent on the DW position ($x_{	ext{DW}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Key parameters include the coupling gap ($\delta = 10 	ext{ nm}$), the material properties of Bi:YIG, and the operating frequency (e.g., 5.9 GHz).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the DW maintains a stable Bloch-type configuration and that the phase tuning mechanism is dominated by dispersion engineering rather than scattering losses.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the geometry, the spatial distribution of the effective magnetic field ($	ext{B}_{	ext{eff}}$) modulated by the DW position, and the resulting continuous, non-linear dependence of the phase shift ($\Delta\varphi$) on the DW position.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the need for a controllable phase shifter, details the hybrid dipolar-coupled structure, uses micromagnetic simulations to model the field and phase shift dependence on DW position, and concludes by emphasizing the non-volatile and energy-efficient nature of the mechanism.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A controllable phase shifter is a key component for spin-wave-based logic and information processing devices. Here, we propose a domain-wall-position-controlled spin-wave phase shifter that exploits dipolar coupling between two closely spaced waveguides to enable continuous phase tuning over a range approaching 360degrees while keeping the spin-wave amplitude constant. Using micromagnetic simulations, we model a bias-free hybrid structure composed of a nanoscale waveguide magnetostatically coupled to a half-ring-shaped structure both made from bismuth-doped yttrium iron garnet with strong perpendicular magnetic anisotropy. Displacing a domain wall in the half-ring modulates the dispersion relation in the adjacent straight waveguide due to the changed magnetostatic interaction, providing a compact and dynamically reconfigurable phase-shifting mechanism. This approach offers precise and non-volatile control over spin-wave propagation and is compatible with energy-efficient magnonic logic architectures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.03932"></a>
### [Emergent Hall viscosity in the integer quantum Hall phases of graphene-like systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03932v1)

**Authors:** M. Selch, M. A. Zubkov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03932v1>

**Summary.** This paper develops a unified theory for the emergent Hall viscosity in graphene-like quantum Hall systems. It distinguishes between geometric and electronic contributions, deriving explicit formulas using advanced field theory techniques like Wigner-Weyl calculus. The work establishes the topological quantization of this viscosity for massless Dirac fermions, providing a robust theoretical framework for understanding strain-induced transport phenomena.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous derivation of topological invariants for transport coefficients like Hall viscosity provides a deep connection between geometry, topology, and condensed matter transport, which is relevant for understanding exotic quantum states.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To unify and explicitly determine the 'emergent Hall viscosity' in integer quantum Hall phases of graphene-like systems, distinguishing between contributions relative to the strain field versus those relative to an emergent vielbein or metric field.

**Main result.** The emergent Hall viscosity is decomposed into 'geometric' and 'electronic' contributions, and its calculation yields quantized topological invariants for the massless Dirac case, which are robust under certain symmetries.

**Method.** The analysis employs effective field theory methods, linear response theory within Wigner-Weyl calculus, and Green's function techniques to derive response functions.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on graphene-like systems (Dirac/Pauli fermions) in the presence of strain and a background magnetic field, covering both semimetallic and semiconducting phases.

**Key observables.** Hall viscosity ($\eta_{em}^H$), Hall conductivity ($\sigma_H$), and associated topological invariants ($N_\sigma, N_\eta$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic field ($B$), chemical potential ($\mu$), and filling factors ($eta, \alpha$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations such as linear response theory, gradient expansions, and the use of the Moyal star product in Wigner-Weyl calculus.

**Figures summary.** Not specified.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the formalism by first defining the problem in terms of strain and metric variations, then deriving response functions using Wigner-Weyl calculus, and finally calculating the topological invariants for both Dirac and Pauli fermion limits.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We explicitly distinguish Hall viscosity as defined relative to the strain field vs. relative to an emergent vielbein or metric field and discuss it for graphene-like systems. Aside from the gravitational or vielbein/metric related ``geometric'' Hall viscosity prevailing throughout the literature, a contribution proportional to the Hall conductivity, the ``electronic'' Hall viscosity, due to the emergent strain induced gauge field exists. We unify both contributions within the ``emergent'' Hall viscosity, determine it explicitly for graphene in the semimetal and Semenoff semiconducting phases for integer quantum Hall states and in the latter case compare it to its non-relativistic limit. Under these circumstances two topological invariants enter the emergent Hall viscosity in the presence of translational and rotational symmetry which we derive in the Green function representation of Wigner-Weyl calculus. We discuss experimental perspectives for extracting the emergent Hall viscosity.

</details>

### [Anharmonic lattice dynamics and superconductivity in strained bulk and surface niobium](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02730v1)

**Summary.** This work uses first-principles calculations to map out how strain and surface termination tune superconductivity in Niobium. By incorporating anharmonic lattice dynamics, the authors show that tensile strain boosts $T_c$ in the bulk, and the (001) surface provides the best pairing strength among the analyzed terminations. The study highlights the crucial, interconnected roles of lattice anharmonicity and dimensionality in superconducting material design.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on BCS-type superconductivity, the rigorous treatment of anharmonic lattice dynamics and the use of MLIPs to accelerate complex many-body calculations are highly relevant to understanding quantum materials far from equilibrium.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how homogeneous strain and crystallographic surface orientation modify the vibrational and superconducting properties of niobium (Nb).

**Main result.** Tensile strain significantly enhances the superconducting transition temperature ($T_c$) in bulk Nb, while the $	ext{Nb}(001)$ surface exhibits the highest $T_c$ among clean slabs, demonstrating that strain, surface effects, and anharmonicity are key tuning mechanisms.

**Method.** The research employs first-principles calculations (DFT) combined with advanced lattice dynamics treatments, including Machine-Learning Interatomic Potentials (MLIP) accelerated Stochastic Self-Consistent Harmonic Approximation (SSCHA) to capture anharmonic effects.

**Model / system.** The physical system is Niobium (Nb), analyzed in both its bulk form and as clean, free-standing ultra-thin slabs with (001), (110), and (111) terminations. Superconductivity is modeled using the electron-phonon coupling (EPC) framework.

**Key observables.** Superconducting transition temperature ($T_c$), electron-phonon coupling constant ($\lambda$), Eliashberg spectral function ($\alpha^2F(\omega)$), and phonon spectra.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Tensile strain (up to $\sim 6\%$ expansion), surface orientation ((001), (110), (111)), and anharmonic renormalization effects.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calculated slab systems are idealized, clean, free-standing crystalline reference systems, and the harmonic approximation fails for surfaces, necessitating anharmonic corrections.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phonon dispersions, $\alpha^2F(\omega)$ for different strain/surface conditions, and comparisons between MLIP-predicted and DFT atomic forces for various Nb structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the effects of strain on bulk Nb, then addresses the necessity of anharmonic corrections for surface slabs, comparing the resulting $T_c$ and EPC strength across different surface terminations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Using first-principles calculations, we investigate how homogeneous strain and crystallographic surface orientation modify the vibrational and superconducting properties of niobium. For bulk Nb, tensile strain strongly softens the phonon spectrum and enhances the electron--phonon coupling, increasing the superconducting transition temperature from 9.5 K at equilibrium to 14.5 K at $\sim\!6\%$ lattice expansion. For the low-index Nb(001), Nb(110), and Nb(111) surfaces, harmonic phonon calculations exhibit imaginary modes, showing that anharmonic lattice effects are essential. To treat these effects efficiently, we train Nb-specific machine-learning interatomic potentials on bulk and slab first-principles configurations and use them to accelerate stochastic self-consistent harmonic approximation calculations, thereby obtaining anharmonically renormalized phonon modes that are combined with density-functional perturbation theory electron--phonon matrix elements to construct the Eliashberg spectral function. Among the clean free-standing slabs considered here, Nb(001) exhibits the strongest electron--phonon coupling and the highest calculated transition temperature of 10.0 K, while Nb(110) and Nb(111) show progressively reduced pairing strength. Finally, by analyzing the Eliashberg spectral function and the functional derivative $δT_\text{c}/δα^2F(ω)$, we identify the phonon energy ranges most effective for superconducting pairing. Our results show that strain, surface termination, and anharmonic phonon renormalization provide complementary and interrelated microscopic routes for tuning superconductivity in Nb.

</details>

### [Spatially Uniform and Defect-Tolerant Plasmonic Responses in 3D printed Gold Nanoparticle Assemblies](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03285v1)

**Authors:** Vasanthan Devaraj, Sunghyun Kwak, Hyeongjip Kim, Sang-Keun Sung, Jong-Min Lee, Thomas Zentgraf, Won-Geun Kim

**Summary.** This work establishes design principles for creating highly reliable 3D plasmonic nanostructures using printed gold nanoparticles. By demonstrating that the optical response is an ensemble average, the authors show the system is robust to structural defects. Furthermore, they prove this response can be actively tuned by engineering the composition using core-satellite nanoparticle arrangements.

**Why it may be interesting.** The concept of robust, ensemble-averaged optical properties emerging from disordered, many-body interactions is analogous to studying collective excitations in disordered quantum systems, providing experimental validation for concepts of emergent order.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how to create plasmonic nanoparticle assemblies that maintain uniform and predictable optical responses despite structural disorder, fabrication variability, and compositional changes.

**Main result.** The ensemble-averaged plasmonic response is shown to be robust and defect-tolerant due to volumetric averaging, and this response can be actively tuned by engineering the core-satellite nanoparticle architecture.

**Method.** The study combines experimental measurements (dark-field scattering, SERS) with advanced electromagnetic simulations (FDTD/Maxwell solvers) to model the collective optical behavior.

**Model / system.** The physical system is 3D printed assemblies of gold nanoparticles (AuNPs) fabricated via meniscus-guided assembly. The model incorporates core-satellite structures and simulates the effect of various structural defects (voids, missing particles).

**Key observables.** Spatially-resolved dark-field scattering spectra, near-field intensity/surface charge distributions, and Surface-Enhanced Raman Scattering (SERS) spectra.

**Important parameters / regimes.** High-aspect-ratio pillars, core-satellite ratios, excitation wavelengths (633 nm vs 785 nm), and the characteristic plasmon delocalization length.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The ensemble response is governed by volumetric averaging, and the simulation models use simplified effective-gap approximations for the complex, stochastic printed junctions.

**Figures summary.** Figures demonstrate the printing process, structural characterization via SEM, and comparative plots showing the stability of scattering spectra and SERS signals under varying defect levels and satellite concentrations.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically moves from characterizing the basic robust response of large assemblies to introducing compositional modulation via core-satellite structures, culminating in detailed analysis of defect tolerance using both simulation and spectroscopy.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Three-dimensional (3D) assemblies of gold nanoparticles (AuNPs) offer a rich platform for plasmonic coupling and near-field engineering, yet their optical behavior is often complex due to structural disorder and fabrication-induced variability. Here, we present a systematic optical investigation of large-scale 3D AuNP assemblies fabricated via meniscus-guided assembly, focusing on the reproducibility and spatial uniformity. Spatially-resolved dark-field scattering measurements reveal that high-aspect-ratio AuNP pillars exhibit uniform scattering spectra along their height and across different pillars, despite variations in geometry and structure. Electromagnetic simulations suggest that this robustness arises from an ensemble-averaged plasmonic response governed by many local coupling regions within a finite plasmon delocalization length. Simulated near-field and surface charge distributions suggest that the broad ensemble response remains spatially distributed under representative structural perturbations, consistent with volumetric averaging. Building on this robust platform, we introduce compositional modulation through a core-satellite architecture by incorporating smaller AuNPs. This yields a composition-dependent spectral redistribution, including an additional long-wavelength spectral feature in the core-satellite assemblies. Wavelength-dependent surface-enhanced Raman scattering measurements reveal contrasting responses under 633 and 785 nm excitation, reflecting redistribution of local plasmonic coupling pathways. These results establish design principles for robust 3D plasmonic nanoparticle assemblies with ensemble-averaged and composition-tunable optical responses.

</details>

### [Ferroelectric-tunable quantum nonlinearity of chiral Bloch electrons in a moiré system](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03497v1)

**Authors:** Zitian Pan, Jundong Zhu, Yu Hong, Jingwei Dong, Dongxia Shi, Kenji Watanabe, Takashi Taniguchi, Luojun Du, Wei Yang, Guangyu Zhang  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.03497v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

</details>

**Summary.** This paper reports on ferroelectric control over quantum nonlinear transport in a moiré graphene heterostructure. By utilizing the ferroelectricity in the top hBN layer, the authors demonstrated a switchable nonlinear Hall effect linked to Berry curvature. This establishes the platform as a promising material system for electrically programmable quantum electronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides an experimental realization of electrically programmable quantum geometric properties (Berry curvature) using ferroelectric switching, which is highly relevant for developing novel quantum electronic devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the coupling between sliding ferroelectricity and quantum nonlinear transport in a moiré heterostructure, aiming to demonstrate ferroelectric control over quantum nonlinearities.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrated a ferroelectric-switchable nonlinear Hall effect, confirming a direct coupling between the ferroelectric polarization and the system's Berry curvature, which is robust and non-volatile.

**Method.** The research combines electrical transport measurements (measuring $R_{xx}$ and nonlinear Hall response $\langle V_{xy}^{2\omega} angle$) with optical characterization ($	ext{SHG}$) and voltage pulse measurements to probe switching kinetics.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is a hexagonal boron nitride ($	ext{hBN}$) encapsulated twisted double-bilayer graphene ($	ext{TDBG}$) moiré heterostructure, which exhibits ferroelectricity due to rhombohedral stacking in the top $	ext{hBN}$ layer.

**Key observables.** Nonlinear Hall response ($\langle V_{xy}^{2\omega} angle$), longitudinal resistance ($R_{xx}$), polarization magnitude ($P_{2D}$), and ferroelectric switching kinetics (nucleation time $t_c$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature range ($1.7-200	ext{ K}$), applied gate voltages ($V_t, V_b$), and excitation electric fields.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The ferroelectricity is attributed specifically to the rhombohedral stacking in the top $	ext{hBN}$ layer, and the nonlinear effect is linked to chiral scattering induced by Berry curvature.

**Figures summary.** Figures detail $	ext{SHG}$ confirmation of ferroelectricity, $R_{xx}$ shifts under gate voltage sweeps, and hysteresis loops for the nonlinear Hall effect ($\langle V_{xy}^{2\omega} angle$) showing switching behavior under voltage pulses.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically confirms the ferroelectric nature of the system, characterizes the temperature-independent polarization, and then demonstrates the ferroelectric-switchable nonlinear Hall effect, establishing the coupling mechanism.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Sliding ferroelectricity in van der Waals materials shows great potential for designing robust memory devices. However, its thermodynamic behaviors and the coupling with certain quantum effects remain largely unexplored. Here, we demonstrate ferroelectric control over quantum nonlinear transport in a hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) encapsulated twisted double-bilayer graphene moiré heterostructure. The ferroelectricity is attributed to the presence of rhombohedral stacking in the top hBN, confirmed by both electrical transport and optical second harmonic generation (SHG) measurements. Remarkably, the polarization magnitude remains temperature-independent across 1.7-200 K, while nucleation time exhibits thermally activated behavior, decreasing with increasing temperature. Furthermore, we demonstrate a ferroelectric-switchable nonlinear Hall effect, attributed to the chiral scattering induced by Berry curvature, with outstanding fatigue-resistant and nonvolatility, demonstrating direct coupling between sliding ferroelectricity and quantum geometric properties. Our results establish sliding ferroelectrics as a platform for exploring electrically programmable Berry curvature physics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.02858"></a>
### [Negative temperature coefficient of Gilbert damping in magnetic bilayers](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.02858v1)

**Authors:** Lulu Cao, Yuting Gong, Xianyang Lu, Yongbing Xu, Ya Zhai, Jing Wu, Roy W. Chantrell, Richard F. L. Evans  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.02858v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

Simulated time evolution of the magnetisation trace (points) for the Py (6 nm)/Nd (32 nm) bilayer at different temperatures (a) T = 0 K, (b) T = 300 K, (c) T = 800 K showing a systematic de- crease in the effective Gilbert damping αeff with increasing tempera- ture as seen from the amplitude of the envelope function depicted by the shaded region. The lines are fitted to the data using Equation 3.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2606.02858_figures/2606.02858_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>

The data show a consistent decrease of the effective damping with tem- perature for Py (6 nm)/Nd (8, 16, 24, 32 nm) bilayers, and the ex- pected increase for the case for pure Py.</sub>

</details>

<img src="2606.02858_figures/2606.02858_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Experimentally measured effective Gilbert damping as a function of the pump fluence of the laser IF for Nd (t = 0, 8, 12, 16 nm)/Py (6 nm) bilayers.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This work explores the temperature dependence of Gilbert damping in magnetic bilayers, finding a surprising negative temperature coefficient in Py/Nd systems. The effect is explained by interface-enhanced damping due to spin pumping, which weakens as temperature rises. This offers a new mechanism to control the dynamic properties of spintronic materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides a clear example of how interface effects and thermal dynamics can lead to non-monotonic or counter-intuitive behavior in magnetic damping, which is relevant to understanding energy dissipation in nanoscale spintronic devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the unusual temperature dependence of the Gilbert damping in magnetic bilayers, specifically observing a decrease in damping as temperature increases in Py/Nd systems.

**Main result.** The damping exhibits a negative temperature coefficient in Py/Nd bilayers, which is attributed to interface-enhanced damping effects from spin pumping that diminish at higher temperatures.

**Method.** The study combines atomistic spin dynamics simulations using the sLLG equation with complementary time-resolved magneto-optical Kerr effect (TR-MOKE) measurements.

**Model / system.** The physical system is magnetic bilayers, such as Py/Nd, modeled using a classical spin Hamiltonian and governed by the stochastic Landau–Lifshitz–Gilbert (sLLG) equation. The interface damping enhancement due to spin pumping is a key physical mechanism.

**Key observables.** Effective Gilbert damping ($\alpha_{	ext{eff}}$), relaxation time ($	au$), and precession frequency ($f$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature ($T$), Nd capping layer thickness ($d_{	ext{NM}}$), and the magnitude of interface spin accumulation.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The sign of the temperature dependence of $\alpha_{	ext{eff}}$ depends critically on the magnitude of the interface spin accumulation (large accumulation $ightarrow$ negative coefficient).

**Figures summary.** Figures show simulated and measured $\alpha_{	ext{eff}}$ vs. temperature, demonstrating the systematic decrease in damping for Py/Nd bilayers compared to the expected behavior in pure films.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theory by modeling spin dynamics and then presents results comparing the temperature dependence of damping in different bilayer structures ($	ext{Py}/	ext{Nd}$ vs. $	ext{Pt}/	ext{Py}$), contrasting the mechanisms responsible for the observed sign change.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Gilbert damping of magnetic materials is an important magnetic parameter that determines the switching speed and energy dissipation of spintronic devices. In simple metals, the intrinsic Gilbert damping increases with temperature and diverges near the Curie temperature as a result of spin fluctuations. Here we present atomistic simulations and experimental measurements showing surprising and opposite behavior in Py/Nd bilayers, where the Gilbert damping decreases with increasing temperature. The effect arises because of the enhanced damping at the interface as a result of spin pumping, where elevated temperatures cause a dynamic separation of the interfacial and bulk magnetization during relaxation. Furthermore, the temperature dependence of the damping can be controlled by varying the thickness of the Nd capping layer. Our findings present a new spintronic effect that can be used to modify the dynamic properties of nanoscale materials and devices for enhanced energy efficiency or with improved switching dynamics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Data-driven mapping of borophene growth pathways](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03886v1)
