<sub>FIG. 1. Snapshots of the 21 different allotropes included in the training dataset in this study. The nomenclature (α, β, χ, and δ) refers to the coordination of the boron atoms, while the subscript has no particular meaning and is only used to distinguish between different allotropes (except for the δ allotropes, where the subscript indicates the coordination). We selected these allotropes because (1) they include all those experimentally observed on silver27 (α, β8, β10, β12, and χ3) as well as those synthesized on copper, gold, and aluminum (α1, α5, χ3), and (2) they cover the full range of vacancy densities ν (from 0 for δ6 to 1/3 for δ3), a critical parameter for borophene properties....</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>FIG. 2. Data-driven classification of borophene al- lotropes. a. Dimensionality reduction of the LMBTR de- scriptors for the different allotropes in the training set through a two-components t-SNE projection. Each point corresponds to a vacancy in the structures obtained during the growth process, and the color corresponds to the allotrope it belongs to. Even with a 2-components t-SNE projection, the different allotropes are quite well separated in the descriptor space. **Summary.** This work develops a predictive framework to control the synthesis of borophene polymorphs on silver surfaces. By combining machine-learned potentials with Monte Carlo simulations, the authors demonstrate that growth is governed by kinetic pathways, not just thermodynamic stability. This allows them to identify specific temperature and substrate conditions that favor desired, stable phases.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous coupling of advanced classical simulation techniques (GCMC, MLIP) with data-driven classification methods provides a powerful template for predicting phase behavior in complex, low-dimensional materials, which is relevant for understanding quantum confinement effects.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The deterministic synthesis of borophene is challenging due to the competition between numerous polymorphs during nucleation and growth.

**Main result.** The study establishes a predictive framework by showing that polymorph selection is governed not just by thermodynamic stability, but by kinetic pathways that allow for self-propagation.

**Method.** The authors combined reactive machine-learned interatomic potentials with grand-canonical Monte Carlo simulations and employed machine learning classifiers trained on local atomic descriptors.

**Model / system.** The system is borophene, a 2D boron sheet, grown on Ag(111) and Ag(100) substrates. The simulation tracks the evolution from early nuclei to extended layers.

**Key observables.** Growth maps (temperature-pressure substrate maps), the proportion of different allotropes over time, and the self-propagation metric ($N_{\infty}^{\square}/N_0^{\square}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (e.g., 500 K vs. 700 K) and substrate type ($	ext{Ag}(111)$ vs. $	ext{Ag}(100)$) are key controlling parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The classification algorithm relies on a probability threshold (0.4) for identifying allotropes, and the simulation assumes the MLIP accurately captures the necessary physics.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate snapshots of the 21 allotropes, t-SNE projections of descriptors, melting temperature comparisons, and time evolution of vacancy counts during growth.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a structure of defining the problem (polymorphism), developing a multi-stage simulation pipeline (MLIP + GCMC + ML classification), presenting results showing kinetic control over stability, and concluding with actionable synthesis windows.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Deterministic synthesis of borophene remains challenging because many polymorphs compete during nucleation and growth. Here we combine a reactive machine-learned interatomic potential with grand-canonical Monte Carlo simulations and data-driven structural classification to track borophene formation from early nuclei to extended layers on Ag(111) and Ag(100). We build temperature-pressure substrate growth maps and resolve how vacancy motifs, phase intermixing and seed structure govern polymorph selection. The simulations reproduce key experimental trends, including the prevalence of $β_{12}$/$χ_3$ phases and their temperature-dependent competition, while revealing kinetic pathways that connect metastable nuclei to long-range order. We identify conditions that suppress competing motifs and promote targeted phases, providing actionable synthesis windows. These results establish a predictive framework for directing borophene growth and, more broadly, for controlling polymorphism in low-dimensional materials by coupling atomistic simulation with machine-learning-enabled phase recognition.

</details>

**Summary.** This work reports on using moiré superlattices in tDBLG to create a novel, energy-efficient synaptic memory. By combining inherent electronic hysteresis (plasticity) with a tunable second-order nonlinear electrical response, the authors show that complex neuromorphic functions can emerge purely from symmetry breaking physics in a single-element carbon material.

**Why it may be interesting.** The emergence of complex, tunable electronic functionality (second-order nonlinearity and plasticity) purely from symmetry breaking physics in a single-element 2D material is highly relevant to understanding emergent quantum phenomena in condensed matter systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is achieving electronic synaptic functionality in a single-element quantum material without relying on external charge traps or polar components.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated a second-order synaptic memory device by harnessing the combination of electronic plasticity (hysteresis) and tunable second-order nonlinear electrical response in moiré superlattices.

**Method.** Measurements involved using synchronized lock-in amplifiers to probe both first-order ($\omega$) and second-order ($2\omega$) voltage drops while varying carrier density and vertical displacement field.

**Model / system.** The system is twisted double bilayer graphene (tDBLG) moiré superlattices, which are purely carbon-based and exhibit electronic properties governed by moiré periodicity and strain-induced symmetry breaking.

**Key observables.** Electronic hysteresis in resistance ($R_S$), second-order nonlinear electrical response ($\propto V_{2\omega}/I_{ds}^{2\omega}$), and multi-level synaptic states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angle disorder, carrier concentration ($n$), vertical displacement field ($D$), and temperature ($T$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The observed hysteresis is attributed to inhomogeneous flexoelectric coupling arising from intrinsic strain gradients, rather than simple ferroelectricity or extrinsic traps.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate $R_S$ vs. $n$ showing superlattice band filling anomalies, $R_S$ vs. $D$ showing pronounced hysteresis loops, and operational diagrams demonstrating synaptic potentiation/depression cycles using $V_{2\omega}$ measurements.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the fundamental electronic plasticity via $R_S$ hysteresis, to confirming the intrinsic second-order nonlinearity, and finally demonstrating the combination of these two effects to realize a functional, low-power, second-order synaptic memory device.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Achieving synaptic functionality electronically in a single-element quantum material is a fundamental challenge, as conventional methods rely on the introduction of extrinsic charge-traps or polar components. Here, we demonstrate that twisted double bilayer graphene (tDBLG) moiré superlattices, composed purely of carbon, exhibit electronic hysteresis and plasticity in presence of twist-angle disorder. Inversion symmetry breaking at the moiré length scales also gives rise to second-order nonlinear electrical response via disorder-mediated extrinsic mechanisms. Such second-order nonlinearity is highly tunable in both sign and magnitude by varying carrier concentration and vertical displacement field. We harness the coexistence of electronic plasticity and second-order nonlinearity to realize a second-order synaptic memory device. Our findings establish strained moiré carbon systems as a powerful new platform for energy-efficient neuromorphic computing, demonstrating that complex electronic functionality can emerge purely from symmetry breaking physics in a single-element material.

</details>

### [Enhanced superconductivity in atomically thin noble metals: From quantum confinement to interface-induced Lifshitz transition](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.03663v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical study develops a unified framework to understand superconductivity in atomically thin noble metals by combining quantum confinement effects with precise interface engineering. The key finding is that the highest superconducting critical temperatures are achieved not merely by increasing electron density, but by engineering the interface to induce a Lifshitz transition, which fundamentally reshapes the Fermi surface and maximizes electron-phonon coupling.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter superconductivity, the mechanism relies on controlling the electronic band structure topology (Lifshitz transition) via atomic interfaces, which is a concept relevant to designing quantum materials and controlling electronic states in nanoscale devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is unlocking and enhancing intrinsic superconductivity in noble metals (Au, Ag, Cu), which are fundamentally non-superconducting in their bulk form.

**Main result.** Superconductivity can be significantly enhanced by engineering interfaces, specifically by inducing a Lifshitz transition in the Fermi surface, which is more effective than simple quantum confinement or charge transfer.

**Method.** The study employs first-principles calculations (DFT/DFPT) to model electronic structure, phonon dispersion, and electron-phonon coupling (EPC) to estimate the superconducting critical temperature (T_C).

**Model / system.** The research focuses on atomically thin noble metal films (Au, Ag, Cu) and their heterostructures, particularly those involving hexagonal boron nitride (h-BN) interfaces.

**Key observables.** Superconducting critical temperature (T_C), Electron-Phonon Coupling constant (lambda), Density of States (DOS) at the Fermi level, and the presence/absence of an interface-induced Lifshitz transition.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Film thickness (1L to 7L), interfacial stacking configuration (N-bonded vs. B-bonded), and the electronic topology of the Fermi surface.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on DFT approximations and the McMillan-Allen-Dynes formula for T_C estimation, while also comparing results against simplified analytical models.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate crystal structures for various film thicknesses and heterostructures, showing the evolution of electronic and superconducting properties, and mapping the k-space distribution of electron-phonon coupling for different interface types.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the problem of intrinsic superconductivity in noble metals, then analyzes the thickness dependence of superconductivity in pure films (Au, Cu, Ag). The core of the work compares different interface configurations (h-BN/Cu), demonstrating that the B-site interface promotes a Lifshitz transition, leading to superior T_C compared to N-site or pristine films.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Unlocking superconductivity in intrinsically non-superconducting noble metals (Au, Ag, Cu) represents a fundamental challenge in low-dimensional physics. While quantum confinement in the atomically thin limit is known to trigger emergent superconductivity, strategies to amplify this marginal effect to experimentally accessible temperatures remain a key open question. Using first-principles calculations, we establish a unified framework linking intrinsic confinement effects with interface engineering in noble metal films. We reveal that intrinsic superconductivity is element-specific: it is suppressed in Ag by a stiff phonon spectrum, but emerges in trilayer Cu ($T_{\rm C} \approx 0.78$ K) and pentalayer Au ($0.63$ K) driven by confinement-induced density-of-states (DOS) enhancement and phonon softening, respectively. In h-BN/Cu(111) heterostructures, $T_{\rm C}$ is critically dictated by the interfacial stacking configuration. We identify the thermodynamically stable N-bonded interface as a reliable platform for accessible superconductivity ($T_{\rm C} \approx 3.23$ K), whereas manipulating the system into a metastable B-bonded configuration boosts $T_{\rm C}$ to $7.00$ K. This enhancement originates from a B-bonded-induced Lifshitz transition, where the Fermi surface forms a tangential contact with the Brillouin zone boundary at the M point, enhancing electron-phonon coupling beyond DOS effects. Our work unifies the understanding of intrinsic two-dimensional superconductivity with atomistic interface design, offering a blueprint for functionalizing noble metals as emergent superconductors.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a mechanochemical approach to synthesize and pattern atomically thin superconducting $	ext{Pd}_7	ext{MoTe}_2$ nanowires on $	ext{MoTe}_2$ using localized AFM force. By applying mechanical stress, the synthesis is driven at near-room temperatures, significantly lower than thermal methods, and achieving nanometer precision. This establishes a powerful new paradigm for engineering quantum materials using mechanical force control.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a novel, generalizable paradigm for synthesizing quantum materials by coupling mechanical energy directly to chemical reaction barriers, which is highly relevant for designing controlled quantum devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is understanding and controlling the synthesis and patterning of atomically thin metals, like superconductors, on 2D materials using mechanical forces rather than traditional high-temperature thermal methods.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated mechanochemical nano-writing, enabling the growth of superconducting $	ext{Pd}_7	ext{MoTe}_2$ nanowires at near-room temperatures with high lateral resolution.

**Method.** The study combines experimental Atomic Force Microscopy (AFM) force application with advanced computational modeling, including Density Functional Theory (DFT) and *ab initio* Grand Canonical Monte Carlo (*ai-GCMC*) simulations.

**Model / system.** The system involves depositing Pd onto a $	ext{MoTe}_2$ 2D material template, forming the superconducting compound $	ext{Pd}_7	ext{MoTe}_2$. The process is modeled using stress-assisted activation kinetics derived from mechanical force application.

**Key observables.** Nanowire height ($\approx 1.6 	ext{ nm}$), stoichiometry ($	ext{Pd}:	ext{Mo}:	ext{Te} = 7:1:2$), superconducting critical temperature ($T_c \approx 0.8 	ext{K}$), and growth rate dependence on applied stress.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Synthesis temperature reduction from $\sim 200^\circ	ext{C}$ to near-room temperature; 50 nm lateral resolution; mechanical stress ($\sigma$) and temperature ($T$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The growth kinetics are governed by a stress-assisted thermal activation model, and the mechanical force directly influences the chemical reaction pathway.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the AFM setup and process (Fig. 1), comparison of thermal vs. mechanochemical growth (Fig. 1), and simulation results showing growth rate dependence on stress and temperature (Fig. 2).

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the physical gap in understanding force-driven synthesis, presents the experimental mechanochemical writing process, validates the mechanism using theoretical modeling (DFT, *ai-GCMC*), and concludes by demonstrating the resulting superconducting quantum material.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Mechanical energy accelerates many physicochemical processes, including materials syntheses that are hard to produce with thermal energy alone. However, physical understanding connecting applied mechanical forces with internal stresses and ensuing reaction mechanisms is lacking. Here we demonstrate mechanical force-enabled synthesis and nanoscale patterning to metallize a two-dimensional (2D) material, producing an atomically-thin superconducting material. Localized force applied by atomic force microscope tips to van der Waals (vdW) encapsulated stacks of 2D bilayer MoTe2 and adjacent source Pd guides 2D Pd7MoTe2 growth with 50 nm lateral resolution. Force accelerates reaction kinetics exponentially per Eyring's stress-assisted thermal activation model, reducing synthesis temperatures from ~200 °C to near-room temperature. Finite element simulations, density functional theory, and ab-initio grand canonical Monte Carlo calculations show that tip-induced compression facilitates Pd chemisorption to tensile-strained MoTe2 that converts to uniform Pd7MoTe2. This demonstrates a new, generalizable paradigm for nanoscale synthesis of quantum materials, and high-precision engineering of superconductivity.

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

**Summary.** This paper theoretically analyzes the shift current conductivity responsible for the bulk photovoltaic effect in monolayer SnS using an effective tight-binding model. By comparing short-range and long-range hopping approximations, the authors demonstrate that while the simple model captures the qualitative physics, the full long-range interactions are necessary for quantitative accuracy. This provides a transparent design tool for understanding photocurrent generation in 2D semiconductors.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed theoretical decomposition of the photocurrent into shift and injection components, coupled with the comparison between model approximations (SR vs. LR), provides a rigorous framework for understanding nonlinear optical responses in 2D materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the bulk photovoltaic effect (BPVE), specifically the shift current conductivity, in monolayer SnS.

**Main result.** The essential features of the shift current conductivity are captured by a minimal short-range tight-binding model, although long-range hopping processes provide quantitative corrections to peak positions and magnitudes.

**Method.** The analysis uses an effective tight-binding model derived from first-principles calculations, decomposing the shift current into transition intensity and a shift vector.

**Model / system.** The system is monolayer SnS, a 2D semiconductor. The model employs an effective tight-binding Hamiltonian (up to $12 	imes 12$) comparing short-range (nearest-neighbor) and long-range hopping approximations.

**Key observables.** Shift current conductivity ($\sigma_{	ext{shift}}$), linear optical conductivity ($\sigma_{ij}(\omega)$), Berry curvature ($\Omega_z(\mathbf{k})$), transition intensity, and the shift vector ($R_{ij}^{nm}(\mathbf{k})$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis compares results derived from short-range (SR) versus long-range (LR) hopping models.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on constructing an effective model from DFT results and assumes the separation of the second-order current into shift and injection components.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare band structures and optical responses derived from SR and LR models, and show contour plots of the shift vector and transition intensity for both model types.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theoretical framework for the shift current, derives its expression using the length-gauge formalism, and then applies this framework to compare the predictions of SR and LR tight-binding models for monolayer SnS.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the bulk photovoltaic effect in monolayer SnS using an effective tight-binding model derived from first-principles calculations. By comparing short-range and long-range hopping models, we show that the essential features of the shift current conductivity are captured by a minimal model. The shift current is decomposed into transition intensity and shift vector, enabling identification of dominant interband transitions. The comparison reveals that long-range hopping processes quantitatively modify the peak positions and magnitudes, while the short-range model retains the characteristic low-energy structure of the nonlinear response. Our findings provide a transparent framework for understanding and designing bulk photovoltaic effects in two-dimensional materials.

</details>
